PROGRAM PEIFORMANCE AUDIT REPORT
CHILE
FIRST, SECOND, AND THIRD STRUCTURAL ADJUSTMENT LOANS
(LOANS 2625-CB, 2767-CH AND 2892-CB)
PB FACE
1.       This is a Program Performance  Audit Report (PPAR) on Chile First,
Second, and Third Structural Adjustment Loans, involving three IBRD loans,
Loan 2625 in the amount of US$250 million; Loan 2767 in the amount of
US$250 million; and Loan 2892 in the amount of US$250 million, for a total
amount of US$750 million. Loan 2625 vas approved on October 22, 1985, and
became effective on November 12,  1985.    The Closing Date was October 31,
1986 and final disbursement was made on June 30, 1986. Loan 2767 was
approved on November 20, 1986, and became effective on November 24, 1986.
The Closing Date was December 31, 1987 and final disbursement was made on
June 15, 1987. Loan 2892 was approved on December 15, 1987 and became
effective on December 16, 1987.    The  Closing  Date was June 30, 1989 and
final disbursement was made on February 23, 1989.
2.       The  audit  consists  of  the  PPAR  prepared  by  the  Operations
Evaluation Department (ORD) and the Program Completion Report (PCR)
prepared by Country Operations Department IV, Latin America and the
Caribbean Region. The PPAR is based on the attached PCR, the President's
Reports, the loan documents, a study of project and country files, and
discussions with Bank staff. An OED mission visited Chile in June 1989 and
interviewed numerous past and present government officials who had been
involved with the formulation and implementation of Chile's economic
programs. The mission also met with private sector representatives and
with a diversity of academic and professional experts.
3.       The PCR provides a very good account and assesment of the program
experience, and discusses the performance of the Bank and the Borrower.
The PPAR elaborates on particular aspects such as the economic policies
pursued by the Chilean Governments prior to the introduction of the
Structural Adjustment Program, some innovative features of the latter--such
as the Government's program to rescue the financial sector--and the main
issues that emerged during the implementation of this program.
4.       The Govermnent's comments on the  PCR  are  included as Annex 5 to
the PCR. The draft Audit Report was also sent to the Borrower for conments
but no further comments were received.
5.       The Country Department has indicated  its basic agreement with the
general conclusions reached with regard to performance of the Structural
Adjustment Program and the supporting Structural Adjustment Loans, but was
unable because of the time constraint to review in depth the individual
chapters of the final report.

PROGRAM PERPOUM)WCE AUDIT REPORT
CHILE
FIRST, SECOND, AND THIRD STRUCTURAL ADJUSTMENT LOANS
(LOANS 2625-CH, 2767-CU AND 2892-Cl)
EVALUATION SUMMARY
Introduction and Overview
1.       The World  Bank  provided  three  successive Structural Adjustment
Loans (SALs) to Chile in 1985, 1986 and 1987, totaling US$750 million.
They were an essential part of the Structural Adjustment Program (SAP)
undertaken by the Chilean administration beginning in 1985. The SAP con-
stituted the last stage among several that integrated the economic policy
of the Government which took power  in  1973.   Hence, an evaluation of the
Bank's SALs would be incomplete without reference to previous economic
policy, the SAP, and the Bank's role in the policy and financial package
that the international financial commnmity contributed to the Chilean
program.
2.       The Chilean Government's SAP produced  results, both on the demand
and on the supply side of the economy, that were dramatic improvements
compared with the  previous  period.   Comparing  the Chilean economy with
other Latin American countries during the period 1985-88, Chile's perfor-
mance was the best in terms of economic expansion. The SAP's growth and
fiscal performances were better than expected.
3.       The Bank was  relatively  disengaged  from  Chile between 1981 and
1984, but it reentered the scene in the second part of 1984, contributing
to the SAP design. The three SALs focused on three main problems: the
need to accelerate and to diversify exports, the insufficiency and disequi-
librium in savings-investment, and the financial disarray. The three SALs
supported a coherent and long-term adjustment program that the Government
initiated in early  1985.    SAL  III  stressed  long-term capital markets,
social security, and health.
4.       The key contribution of the Bank since late 1984 was two-fold:  to
instill a medium-term dimension and approach in economic policy and to make
such policy possible through direct and catalytic effects on financing. It
is generally recognized that successive short-term policies would have
doomed the adjustment effort to failure.   The permanent emphasis on export
promotion and savings mobilization and investment was well placed to alle-
viate the then most strategic weaknesses in the economy.
S.       Although the contribution of SAL II was less dramatic than that of
its predecessor, it was equally necessary to sustain the SAP. The main



additional emphasis was on cutting the terms and costs of financial reha-
bilitation, in which it  succeeded.    The  orientation of SAL III included
fast action in the social  sectors.    While  intended to balance the whole
package, its effects are more disputed than those of the earlier SALs. In
general, social expenditure was directed to the more needy sectors of the
population. This is true in the sense that the programs that were enhanced
were directed to alleviate the effects of unemployment. Here the impact of
this type of expenditure has been to alleviate the hardships of some groups
in extreme poverty. The next group up the income ladder, however, was made
worse off.
The Challenges Three Periods of Economic Policy
6.       The economic policy  of  the  Government  of Chile can be roughly
subdivided into three periods9
*   the early liberalization (1975-81);
*   economic crisis and transit-on (1982-84);
*   the Government's  Structural Adjustment Program (1985-88
and still continuing).
The Early Liberalization
7.       When the authorities took power in September 1973, they were faced
with record inflation, output that had been declining since 1971, a public
sector deficit exceeding 2O of gross domestic product (GDP), and a money
supply that was out of control.   The authorities initially stressed fiscal
order and some deregulation of  the  economy.   In 1975 real copper prices-
-copper represented over half of total exports--hit their lowest level in
20  years.    The  depth  of  the  economic  crisis  helped  to  define the
Government's economic policy of drastic austerity and liberalization.
8.       By 1976, a  fiscal  surplus was  achieved  by reducing the public
sector and improving its efficiency.   Prices were gradually freed, tax and
subsidy distortions removed, barriers to trade were lessened and exchange
controls were  eliminated.    Following  a  large  initial  devaluation the
exchange rate continued to be adjusted periodically for a while. Inflation
dropped to an annual rate of 302 and economic growth picked up, led by non-
traditional exports. Thus, despite low copper prices, the international
reserves recovered. This progress, however, was accompanied by a high rate
of unemployment.
9.       Furthermore, some of the  policies  implemented  after 1978 led to
unsustainable disequilibria. Chile's opening of its capital account, which
started gradually in 1978, was completed by April 1980. This, in conjunc-
tion with the fixing of the exchange rate in June 1979 and the full index-
ation of wages to past inflation, led to massive inflows of unguaranteed
long-term credit. These capital flows financed an unsustainable expansion
of private consumption and investment in 1980 and early 1981. The boom of



- xi -
that period was fundamentally financed with foreign credit. As a conse-
quence, external debt rose from US$6.7 billion at end-1978 to US$17.2
billion by end-1982, or the equivalent of 69Z of GDP. More than 1OOS of
the increase in debt during this period was contracted by the private
sector, since government net foreign liabilities declined continuously from
1975 to 1981.   At  the  same  time,  the  competitiveness of the country's
tradeable sector was being eroded by the fixed exchange rate. Furthermore,
the corporate sector was generally weakened by excessive real domestic
interest rates, which exceeded  30S  per  year  at  their  peak in the late
seventies.
10.      Finally, the financial sector was affected by the deterioration in
the quality of their loans. This was due to two main causess the general
problems of the corporate sector and the granting of credits to companies,
the ownership of which was related to that of the lending institutions.
This generalized practice undermined the stability and the public confi-
dence in the financial system.    With world interest rates at record high
levels, copper prices at record lows, and voluntary lending from abroad
discontinued, Chile belatedly devalued the peso in 1982, in the face of one
of the most severe crises in the country's modern history.
11.      On narrow economic grounds, liberalization  left a legacy of rela-
tive fiscal soundness and more efficient Government administration, a re-
allocation of resources more in line with international prices, and a
deregulation that made the economy more flexible. From a broader perspec-
tive, it exposed Chilean entrepreneurs to international competition,
elicited supply responses through institutional changes, created interest
groups supporting this economic approach, and nurtured a consensus in favor
of economic stability. But the shortcomings of the policy--chiefly, lack
of prudential supervision of financial institutions--increased the vulner-
ability of the economy to the point of collapse.
Economic Crisis and Transition
12.      The unavoidably sharp devaluation  and decline in expenditure were
followed by a massive economic crisis: a 15? fall in GDP (partly due to
falling terms of trade), 25? unemployment (despite 500,000 employed in low-
wage emergency employment programs, EEPs), and insolvency (partly hidden)
of half the productive and financial sectors. In January 1983, the author-
ities "intervened" some of the largest firms and banks to keep them afloat,
and the Central Bank had to provide subsidized credit to debtors. Internal
problems were compounded by the high (mostly private) foreign debt and
negative international setting. Interest payments on that debt amounted to
302 of exports and 502 of savings. The Government was forced to guarantee
part of private foreign obligations in order to reschedule its own debt
amortizations due in 1983-84 and to get new money to cover current account
deficits. Loans from international financial institutions were not forth-
coming.
13.      The severity of the 1983 adjustment (consumption 72 lower, invest-
ment down 15Z) put the stabilization program on track again and improved



- xii -
the external sector, but the public accounte deteriorated. An attempt at
recovery in 1984 proved unsustainable.    Imports rose 172, and the current
account deficit rebounded to 10? of GDP. An ensuing devaluation and rise in
Import tariffs--resisted by the business cammunity--from 20? to 35? failed
to bridge the external gap and sealed the fate of the country's fourth
economic team in two years.
14.      The crisis and transition  period. however, left other influences
besides the immediate, precarious situation, the main ones not purely
economic.   In  1984,  the  unsustainable  relief  from  the  crisis and an
unsuccessful attempt to revive protectionism forced a sounder analysis
whose outcome was the widely perceived need for a medium-term view of the
economy in order to avoid the repeated failures of short-term approaches
and to restore and sustain public confidence. It also endorsed a return to
a more liberal economic policy.
1S.      In early 1985, a new  Economic  Minister took charge.  Despite the
compression of income and imports (451 since 1981) and steady growth in
export volumes, Chile still required significant capital inflows, and
copper--now at very low prices--still provided more than 50? of foreign
exchange earnings. Chile's US$20 billion external debt equaled its GDP and
was five times the value of its merchandise exports. The main priorities
included recovery from extremely low levels of savings and investment and
the financial rehabilitation of banks and firms. These were key among the
many challenges to be faced.
The Government's Structural AdJustment Program
16.      The crucial  year  for Chile's  structural  adjustment effort was
1985. The administration decided to address the challenge decisively with
an approach that had evolved in late 1984, both in Chile and in the inter-
national financial institutions. Vhen the program was conceived, the coun-
try had the worst ratio of  foreign  debt  to  GDP in Latin America, with a
current balance of payments  deficit  of US$2.1  billion.   The open urban
unemployment rate was 18.5Z.    Investment was  13.6Z of GDP, and domestic
savings 12.6X--not enough to finance investment, and even less sufficient
to service the  foreign  debt.    The  overall fiscal deficit--quasi-fiscal
shortfalls aside--was at a maximm 4.42 of GDP, mostly due to near-peak
expenditures of 30.7? of  GDP.    The  current  fiscal surplus was far from
adequate to finance public investments of 6.4? of GDP.
17.      The crucial concepts of the  resulting SAP were that a medium-term
view was necessary for short-term stabilization to hold, and that the pol-
icy and financial elements, by mutual reinforcement, provided consistency
and continuity to the program.    The medium-term horizon led to multiyear
projections that helped to trace the consequences of alternative courses of
action--and to discard some. The tough decisions of setting and keeping a
high exchange rate--to raise public savings significantly and swiftly and
to let real wages fall--both allowed and were sustained by foreign finance:
the concept of a "policy and financial package' was developed with the
international institutions.



- xiii -
18.      Its design was clears
* a high and stable exchange rate, to stimulate exports and
Improve trade balance;
*   a fiscal  shock,  initially  in  both  supply and demand,
later with emphasis on ezpenditure restraint, to raise
public savings;
*   an effort to rehabilitate  the  financial sector in order
to increase private savings.
This macro-economic framework was directed at tackling the key vulnerabili-
ties of the  economys    to  generate  the  external and internal transfers
aecessary to avoid 'snowballing' of the foreign debt, and to generate
confidence in foreign and domestic lenders and investors so as to fill the
external gap and return to the growth path.
19.      This "core' was supplemented bys
*  measures--among  them, the   CSF--to  develop  noncopper
exports and efficient import substitution;
*   a voluntary debt reduction mechanism;
*   other measures to reorder public current expenditures and
Investment  (including  privatization   of  state  enter-
prises); and
* institutional changes in the financial markets.
20.      In the application of the  SAP, the new economic team blended the
approach of the 1970s--a free, open, privately operated economy with fiscal
equilibrium--with a realistic handling of specific problemss reasonable
domestic price bands for domestic producers of traditional crops; some
protection; finAncial freedom with supervision rules for banking enforced
by the Superintendency of Banks and Financial Institutions (SBIF); moder-
ate, stable export-oriented exchange rate; wage restrictions to stimulate
employment; and debt conversion schemes.
21.      The implementation of the SAP can be divided into two stages: the
crucial year 1985, during which the financial package and some aspects of
the policy package were not yet settled; and the following three years
under the uninterrupted  financial arrangements.    The authorities showed
restraint at several opportunities--stopping an inflationary revival in
1987 and attenuating a 1988 boom. SAP implementation was helped, especial-
ly after 1987, by positive external shockst   a  fall in oil and a rise in
copper prices, lower interest rates, a cheaper U.S. dollar, and world eco-
nomic recovery.



- xiv -
The World Bank and the SAP
The Bank's Role
22.      The international financial institutions--the  1MF and World Bank-
-helped to design and implement essential components of the SAP's policy
and financial package. Before the SAP, the IM4 had agreed to two Stand-bys
for 1983 and 1984, which paved the way for two compensatory financing
facility purchases in 1983 and 1985 and for the subsequent US$750 million
extended Fund facility (EEF), which  became  a  crucial SAP component.  The
Bank had been relatively disengaged from Chile since 1981, but it reentered
the scene in the second part of 1984, contributing to the SAP design.
23.      In 1985 the two institutions put together a US$2 billion financial
package integrated by the IMF's EFF, the Bank's US$250 million SAL I, and
private banks' US$1.1 billion in new finance. To help wrap up the package,
the Bank set precedents by guaranteeing half of US$300 million in syndi-
cated B-loan co-financing for the highway sector. The Government, however,
had to yield to the private banks' pressure and guarantee part of the
private domestic debts. This opened the door to debt renegotiations with
the banks and with the Paris Club.    The conditions set by the IMF and the
Bank were mutually reinforcing. The former established macroeconomic goals
for the monetary, fiscal, and foreign sectors; the latter set conditions
for export promotion, sav3ngs mobilization, the public investment program
(PIP), financial rehabilitation, and  employment  generation.   In 1986 and
1987 the Bank approved SALS II and III, for US$250 million each, bringing
Its total SAL assistance  to  US$750  million.   Negotiations with creditor
banks in 1986 and 1988 rescheduled US$12.5 billion, with maturities trans-
ferred to 1988191. Chile obtained a retiming of (annual) interest payments
that implied savings of US$450 million.
24.      The "core" of the SAP  suffered  little  change since its start in
1985. Within it, the SALs I and II addressed three main problems: the
need to accelerate and to diversify exports, savings-investment insuffi-
ciency and disequilibrium, and the financial disarray. Other questions,
mostly social, were barely addressed. By late 1986 the SAP's initial phase
had been implemented. In SAL II the Bank emphasized the recapitalization
and reprivatization of banks and corporations while keeping the other
objectives. By the second part of 1987, except for external events, there
appeared to be little room for policy changes or for increases in consump-
tion. Thus the SAP's orientation was to maintain the policy framework.
SAL III placed its stress on long-term capital markets, social security,
and health, all of which affected the program's sustainability. The high-
lights of the main SAL components are summarized below.
The Structural Adiustment Loans (SAL8)
Non-copper Exports a Top Priority
25.      Poor price prospects  for Chile's  major  primary exports and its
huge foreign debt made expansion of noncopper exports a top priority. The
SAP cperated through:



*   the reduction of import tariffs,
*   a  real  exchange  rate  attractive  for  export-oriented
investment,
*   coordination of  investment  (channeling  to forestry and
infrastructure),
* a larger role for PROCHILE, the Chilean Export Promotion
Agency, in promoting small producers' exports, and
*   stabilization of  the  effects  of  copper market fluctu-
ations.
26.      Main actions were a 25?  real devaluation of the peso, maintenance
of the real exchange rate throughout the period, an establishment of the
Copper Stabilization Pund (CSF) to resist pressures from the volatility of
copper prices. The CSF became active in late 1987 and its reference price
bands were changed to strengthen  it.    Import tariffs (fixed by law since
June 1986) were reduced to a uniform 20S, and the application of an anti-
dumping surcharge  was  moderate.    Tax  and  credit measures strengthened
export stimuli.   An  insurance  system  reduced  the  risk  of opening new
markets, and legislation was enacted to help to develop export insurance
firms.   The  production  capacity  of  CODELCO,  the  Chilean State Copper
Corporation, increased moderately, and high-potential ore bodies were
opened to private investors.   As regards import substitution, the authori-
ties attracted investors for petroleum exploitation and exploration, for a
coal mine, for a methanol plant, and for salt production. Reference price
bands for wheat, oilseeds, and sugar were reduced.
Savints and Investment Increased
27.      To absorb the unemployed and  for the economy to grow, substantial
new Investment was needed; because interest payments on the external debt
would absorb much of savings for years ahead, savings had to be increased.
The major effort would be borne by the public sector, then the principal
debtor abroad. Hit by the impact of the earlier social security reform,
the income tax reform, and the financial relief for the banking system, the
administration resorted to the value-added tax (VAT), to taxes on luxury
goods, to sales of  assets,  and  to  expenditure  austerity.   Some of the
measures, like income tax reform, mobilized private savings; pension fund
administrators (AFPs) invested 4S of GDP.
28.      A three-year PIP, to avoid  the stop-and-go pattern of annual pro-
grams, focused more on exports and private investment. After World Bank
review, several projects (the Santiago subway, large highway and irrigation
projects) were eliminated in favor of faster, higher-return works (chiefly
maintenance  and  rehabilitation).    As  the  SAP  progressed, expenditure
austerity gained ground; there were more wage restraints and reductions in
social programs. The PIP included earthquake reconstruction and emphasized
private investment support (transportation) and upgrading production in



- Zvi .
public enterprises. In SAL III it was possible to sustain the level of the
PIP and to keep increasing public savings and reducing the fiscal deficit
while absorbing the costs of recapitalization of the banking system.
Reforms for Banks and Pirms
29.      To avoid  a  general collapse  of Chile's  firms  and banks, the
Central Bank had provided general debt rescheduling programs, preferential
exchange rates, and subsidized debt swaps. The Government guaranteed most
of the foreign debt of private financial entities. Thus, the losses of the
system--then at least US$3.5 billion--had to be borne entirely within the
country, threatening severe macroeconomic repercussions: massive transfers
and commitments to continue them into the future. An unsolved issue was
how to make up for the losses since 1987 (up to then, accomplished by for-
eign borrowing). The solution was to contain the subsidies to banks and
firms and to find resources to pay the past subsidies.
30.      The generalized reprogramming of  domestic loans ended by November
1985, and preferential exchange rates and subsidized swaps were discon-
tinued by December 1986. Bank supervision was strengthened. To recapital-
ize the banks, the Central Bank bought their bad loans, but the original
stockholders could not receive dividends until all these loans had been
repurchased. Legislation limited the public participation in the equity of
intervened banks to 492, of which at least one-fifth had to be sold annual-
ly to the private sector.    In  1986, through case-by-case debt renegotia-
tion, practically all "viable'  firms  had completed the financial arrange-
ments; the banking system had returned to a position of positive net worth;
and some banks had been sold.    There was time to move from rehabilitation
of firms to development of the capital market, while at the same time
pressing financial entities to regularize their situations. The authori-
ties moved to diversify the portfolios and to channel the funds of institu-
tional investors (for example, the APPs) into private debt and equity by
lifting restrictions; to encourage equity investments and intermediation
through fiscal measures; and to change all important risk classification
procedures.
New Activities Addressed Social Issues
31.      The main thrust of the new  activities was in the social aspects.
Several  studies were  started,  supported   by  a World  Bank  Technical
Assistance Loan (TAL).   In  the  final  stages  of  the SAP, as the fiscal
situation improved, the Government moved to protect the security of pension
funds by tightening regulations. It also adopted measures to increase the
efficiency of the system, to extend its long-ternm coverage, and to enhance
public confidence in the system. In 1988, current nonpersonLIel outlays for
health were increased to consolidate progress in maternal and child health
care, to extend it to cover chronic diseases, and to upgrade the efficiency
of the decentralized health care system.



_ xvii -
Evaluation of the SAP
32.      Performance of the IAP can  be evaluated from different angles, by
comparisons of the behavior of the Chilean economy under the SAP with
respect tot
*   its evolution in previous periodst
* the behavior of the other economies;
*   programmed economic goals.
Although these comparisons enrich one's vision of the SAP's performance, it
cannot be ignored that they are essentially counterfactual historical exer-
cises. In one case the standard is past history; in another it is foreign
economics; in the third it is an imaginary scenario. The last of these,
however, is amenable to a disaggregation of the causes of the differences
between program and results.
Dramatic Comparisons with Previous Years
33.      The SAP launched in 1985 produced  effects, both on the demand and
on the supply side of the economy, that are dramatic compared with the
previous period (Table S.1).

Comparisons with Other Latin American Economies
34.      The highlights from comparing the  behavior of the Chilean economy
under the SAP (1985-88) with that of other Latin American countries are
presented in Table S.3. Chile's performance was the best in Latin America
in terms of economic expansion, which keeps accelerating, while in the
region growth has decelerated since  1986.  A contributing factor has been
the improvement in the capacity to import. Chile's exports climbed to 139
on average over  1985-89  (1984-100)  while  in  the  region  as a whole it
deteriorated to 93. In terms of inflation, Chile was well below the Latin
American average (and declining), whereas since 1986 inflation has acceler-
ated in most of the other countries. Chile's foreign debt declined by 0.62
annually--a performance surpassed only by Venezuela--while external indebt-
edness grew at 2.1Z annually in Latin America as a whole. International
comparisons of unemployment figures are not trustworthy because of methodo-
logical differences  among  countries.    But  quantitative and qualitative
indications suggest that unemployment was higher in Chile than in the rest
of Latin America until 1988.    The trend in Chilean unemployment, however,
has been downward throughout the SAP period, even though the Government has
practically discontinued its employment programs.



-  ix-
35.      The bottom line is that the evolution of the Chilean economy under
the SAP has been outstanding, albeit helped in part by marked terms of
trade improvements. A large part of the burden of adjustment has been
borne by laborers, and only after four years hae unemployment approached
nonmal levels and  real wages begun  to  Increase.    Chile'. comeback is
remarkable because it was one of the Latin American countries most severely
affected by the 1982 international debt crisis.

Programmed Economic Goals and Results
36.      The discrepancies between the  goals  set and the results achieved
in the main variables of the SAP are summarized in Table S.4. These are
compared to the targets and projections included in earlier stabilization
programs, supported by the  IMP.   Under  the  SAP, both growth and fiscal
performances were better than expected:    the mean deviations were smaller
and showed overcompliance with the goals; the variance of the devlation.
was also smaller, suggesting a more consistent policy. If compared with
the 1982-84 programs,the SAP performance is not only superior in compliance
with goals; it also shows smaller and more stable deviations, perhaps the
consequence of a more consistent and firm set of measures and of rapid
reactions to circumstances. One reason that the SAP surpassed several of
its goals was that strong positive external shocks helped growth, the
balance of payments, and public finance.  When the policies were designed,

38.      The reversal in the overall fiscal  balance from a deficit of 4.4?
of GDP to a surplus of 2.9Z--an improvement of 7.31 of GDP, despite a rise
of 1Z of GDP in public investment--came more from current expenditure
reductions (general and social) than from revenue increases. Only *other
revenues' (proceeds from the sales of public enterprises) added signifi-
cantly to the improvement. At the same time, the public financial sector-
-the Central Bank and State Banks--was recovering from its losses. From a
peak of 20.9Z of GDP in 1985--a reflection of the costs of salvage and
rehabilitation--these losses were down to 0.3Z of GDP by 1989.
39.      The impact of the program in the areas of financial rehabilitation
and capital market development is less easily portrayed quantitatively.
But confidence in the banking system was restored, and traumatic events
were averted. With the exception of a few banks whose ability to repur-
chase their bad loan pottfolios is In doubt, the domestic financial enti-
ties are solvent and profitable. The stock exchange has expanded in volume
and value, and financial intermediation has developed both institutionally
and in terms of instruments, with consequent increased (financial) savings
and imprwved savings allocation.
Lessons from the Chilean SAP
Importance of Country's History in Program Design and
Implementation
40.      One clear lesson from the  Chilean experience is the importance of
the background of a country's recent economic history for the success of a
SAP--and thus the importance of taking that history into account In the

rehabilitation--these losses were down to 0.3Z of GDP by 1989.

and capital market development is less easily portrayed quantitatively.
But confidence in the banking system was restored, and traumatic events
were averted. With the exception of a few banks whose ability to repur-
chase their bad loan portfolios is in doubt, the domestic financial enti-
ties are solvent and profitable. The stock exchange has expanded in volume
and value, and financial intermediation has developed both institutionally
and in terms of instruments, with consequent increased (financial) savings
and lmproved savings allocation.
Lessons from the Chilean SAP
Importance of Country's history in Program Design and
Imlementation
40.      One clear lesson from the  Chilean experience is the importance of
the background of a country's recent economic history for the success of a
SAP--and thus the importance of taking that history into account in the



- xxii -
program's design and implementation, not only to avoid errors but also to
enhance chances of success. Some of the features of the preceding liberal-
ization, crisis, and transition that influenced the SAP includes
o   private external debts  are  risks  not  only for foreign
creditors but also for the entire domestic economy;
*   firms may not be  able  to  adjust fast enough to survive
the simulcaneous impact of up-hill competition from
imports and of high real interest ratesg
*   banking prudential rules  and  surveillance are necessary
safeguards  for  the   operation  of  domestic  financial
markets, rather than unnecessary restrictions.
Other Lessons From the 1985-89 Period
* It seems the essence of economic crises that (a) it takes
a long time to fully understand all their implications,
and (b) current institutional arrangements may be inade-
quate or too unstable to handle them;
*   in terms of the  Chilean  financial  crisis, only in late
1982 were there first quantitative estimates of its mag-
nitude, and it took several years (and finance ministers)
to bring it under control;
-   It is necessary to separate monetary effects from distri-
butional, allocative, and waste (in terms of lost con-
sumption and investment) effects of the financial crisis.
Importance of *Ownershi&' of Program
41.      Another lesson of the Chilean experience is that Chile 'owned" its
SAP. The program design was Chilean, and this greatly helped implementa-
tion. The DMF, active since the early 1980s, gave support and policy
advice. The World Bank was active and preparing for future action by mid-
1984. But if the SAP incorporated the ideas or suggestions of these insti-
tutions, it did so through coincidence or persuasion, not by imposition.
This experience also commends a sound, simple conceptual framework and its
adaptation to particular aspects of reality.   A corollary is a clear sepa-
ration of the essential--nounegotiable--elements of the program from other
aspects or effects.
42.      Another element in the  SAP's  success  was that it adopted fairly
conservative estimates of exogenous factors (for instance, the copper
price). Although the program consequently looked too restrictive initial-
ly, it demanded more from economic policy and left some leeway to respond
to requirements later on. The Government was committed to the SAP: the
President was convinced; the new economic minister had total authority; and
there was support from other cabinet members.



- xxiii -
Importance of Political Cohesion and Quality Economic Leadership
43.      Thus, another lesson from  the  SAP's implementation is the impor-
tance of full political support and cohesion behind the program and of the
quality of the economic team and supporting technocracy. But would Chile's
success have been possible under a different political economy? There are
as many counterfactual historical as ideological angles, and no objective
criterion exists to select among  them.    But history provides examples of
successes and failures under both democratic and authoritarian regimes.
Catalytic Effect of the Bank's Intervention
44.      Another observation is the decisive catalytic effect of the Bank's
intervention, due to the faith it showed in the program's eventual success.
This suggests that it is wise for the Bank to bide its time, so as to step
in when the Government has shown its determination, while continuing to
monitor the country along the path of program design and early implementa-
tion. The administration did not loosen its grip on the SAP when positive
external shocks set in. with excellent results; this underlines the impor-
tance of keeping to the right priorities--and also keeping an eye on not
relaxing because of early  successes.    The  implementation  of the SAP in
Chile faced no serious resistance; institutional reforms were smooth and,
after generally sound studies, were rapidly performed. The Government's
commitment was exceptional; the external environment was better than
expected; and the economic policies were correct (with some nonprice ques-
tions debatable). The SAP could not have been the same without support
from the international financial institutions--for example, in the service
of foreign debt, where the Bank played a key role. The Bank provided good
advice on the whole, and  proper  disbursements.   There again, the ensuing
success validates the usual criteria for implementation of SAPs.
Replicability of SAP Experiences and Its Sustainability
45.      The replicability of experiences  with reference to sustainability
very much depends on the political economy of the particular country and
its external environment. With all these caveats, some observations may be
ventured.
*   Structural adjustment, as a  form  of social change, ini-
tially faces resistance, but as it proceeds (and if it
starts to work) it gains adepts and loses opponents.
Continuity of the program in itself reinforces sustain-
abilitys    this  observation  supports  the  practice of
granting successive SALs during spans of no less than
three years.
*   Creating reserves, during  economic  upswings,  is a con-
venient way to prepare for downswings--as was the case
with the CSF. Later, part of the reserves were devoted
to solving a longer-term problem--reducing the foreign
debt.



-   iv -
* Those features of adjustment that imply direct participa-
tion of the population (in the Chilean case, 'popular
capitalism*) create wider support for the system of poli-
cies.
Summing up the SAP Experience
46.      The performance  of Chile  in  terms  of  the usual macroeconomic
goals--GDP growth, inflation, current balance of payments, foreign trade,
and fiscal results with respect to GDP--has been very favorable compared
with previous periods, with Latin American countries, and with the pro-
grammed goals. The SAP provoked sharp positive trend reversals in all
major objective variables, with marked improvements within the period.
Almost all of the overcompliance with GDP growth goals in 1987 an 1988 is
explained by the direct effects of unanticipated positive external shocks.
If these shocks also had indirect effects, it can be argued that perfor-
mance without the shocks vould have fallen short of the goals. But this
ignores the policy reactions of the authorities under alternative condi-
tions. On the whole, and taking account of the observations in the
Technical Appendix, the model behind the projections (the model of the
Central Bank) portrayed adequately the principal interactions of the
Chilean economy.
4'7.     The lesson to draw from  the controversial evolution of the social
sectors during the SAP is that the main content of the SAP should not be
compromised for short-run social improvements made at the cost of economic
growth and adjustment.    However,  some  low-cost  concessions to palliate
extreme or focused social problems enhanced the SAP's sustainability.
48.      In terms of a  conventional checklist  of sustainability of SAPs,
Chile scores high. The adjustment policy did not suffer from sociopolit-
ical overoptimism and followed closely its original design, with some ade-
quate changes in emphasis and a few concessions. There were significant
reforms and widely recognized competence in economic management. The coun-
try is far better prepared to face its problems than it was four years ago,
but important challenges remain.
Lessons From the SALS
Exchange Rates and Export Promotion
49. *   A main element  in  successful  adjustment is a realistic
and stable exchange rate, whose credibility is a prereq-
uisite for economic agents  to invest in an intrinsically
risky activity such as exporting.
*   Early  achievement  of  a  critical  mass  of diversified
exports sufficient  to  create  a  new  class of interest
groups with enough political power to resist changes in
economic policies  that would  penalize  exports  to the
point of interrupting their normal flow is important.



- XXv -
* It is Important to look at export promtion gas a whole*:
including institutional developments (such as port opera-
tions), focused infrastructure, direct "new activities'
programs, etc.
Public Sector Management
50.      The main lessons from Chile's public sector manag8ment are differ-
ent expressions of  centuries-old  sound  criteria:    austerity to restore
equlibrium and sensible administration.   The  backbone of the success was
the set of difficult decisions on the expenditure side, some of them firmly
proposed and backed by  the  Bank:    ending  the bail-out of the financial
system, allowing wages to fall to levels compatible with foreign competi-
tion, containing the EEP and health expenditures through creative specific
programs, and streamlining the PIP by higher-return and better-quality
investments.
51.      $he fiscal and monetary  handling  of  the  cost of the financial
crisis was also important.   In  Chile  the fiscal effects of high interest
rates and devaluation were compensated as those of trade liberalization
would bes the tax system was improved. The solutions did not suffer from
delays, except in the organization of decentralized activities. The debat-
able aspects were the distributional effects of some policies. Here again,
replicability of the experience may be limited because of the specific
political conditions and of the very low initial level of domestic public
debt.
Foreign Debts of Private Firms
52.      An analysis  of  financial  crises  shows  that widespread foreign
debts of private firms do not commit the Government as long as the economy
is in a normal  situation.    However,  if  there  is a balance of payments
crisis, the whole economy bears the exchange burden. If there is a domes-
tic financial crisis, the State bears the brunt of the cost and is unlikely
to succeed in transferring to foreign creditors their proportional share of
costs--unless it itself becomes insolvent, but this would entail other
costs to the country. The moral is to only adopt rehabilitation measures
to avoid the collapse of the "viable' companies and banks, thus limiting
costs. This often calls for making 'stock' losses explicit and for design-
ing how they will be supported and financed while cushioning their infla-
tionary, distributional, and allocative  effects.   The World Bank followed
the adjustment process in Chile by shifting the emphasis in successive
SALe8 first to stabilize the financial sector, then to limit Government
involvement, and finally to recapitalize financial entities and develop
capital markets. An especially useful Bank contribution was its guidance
and support in developing an exemplary system of prudential rules and sur-
veillance system for financial institutions.    It was learned that initial
panic and disorder tend to hasten salvage measures, which are often ineffi-
cient and costly. In the Chilean experience, the benefits of rapid recapi-
talization, without 'statization' of the system--in terms of confidence,
fluidity, and savings-investment behavior--justified the element of subsidy
in the price of shares offered to the public.



- xxvi -
SALs and the Social Sectors
53.      To draw lessons from  the  performance  of  the SALs in the social
sectors faces several difficultiess
*   separating the effects of the measures adopted since 1985
from the trend of previous years, since in those sectors
delays are longer than in many productive activities;
*   agreeing on the  criteria  and  figures,  which are hotly
contested, to appraise the situations.
Some observations are:
* a large portion of the containment in social expenditures
came from a fall in public sector real wages, which
cannot be sustained indefilnitelyt
* extreme targeting can alleviate narrowly defined--often
pressing--problems but is no substitute for a long-term
sectoral solution and frequently causes imbalances that
increase tensions later;
* a corollary to both cases is that the sacrifices ex-
tracted from, and the palliatives given to, the social
sectors--while  congruent  with   the   economic  policy
approach--are mostly short-term solutions that require
compensation later, and that projections for future years
should take this  into  account:    selective recovery of
real wages,   restoration   of maintenance,  rebuilding
infrastructure. etc.
*   another corollary is that the period of adjustment should
also be used to design and begin structural adjustments
In the social sectors, and that the phasing out of struc-
tural  adjustment  assistance   should  contemplate  some
social sector loans;
* the most important special programs were those oriented
toward the poor. The perception is that the lower-middle
classes and the not-so-poor suffered more, at least rela-
tively. On the other hand, it is possible that this
perception is  exaggerated:    the  protest  voice of the
lover-middle class is frequently stronger in relation to
that of the poor.
Lessons For the World Bank's SAL Activity
Maintaining and Active Policy Dialogue
54.      Owing to the Government's reluctance  to maintain an active policy
dialogue with the Bank, beginning in 1981 the Bank's activity in Chile



- xxvii -
dwindled, as reflected in the staff inputs assigned to cover the country.
It was only by mid-1984 that the Bank became once again active in the coun-
try, and it resumed a significant role.   The IMF was present in Chile dur-
ing the years that the Bank was  not.    This was of no practical signifi-
cance, but it could be elsewhere.  There  is some merit in a policy of hav-
ing the Bank keep closer monitoring--if no physical presence--with country
authorities even in periods of little Bank activity in the country. There
are, of course, cost considerations here. But coordination and division of
labor with other international institutions may reduce some costs.
Desirability of Having a Small Number of Conditions
55.      In contrast with SALs I and  II, in SAL III targets were more dis-
persed and less focused, owing to the incorporation of a variety of social
aspects. This trait of the last SAL has been subject to some criticism in
Chile on several grounds. A first observation is the desirability of hav-
ing a suall number of conditions--four to six as a rule of thumb--related
to the core of the SAP, whose compliance should be strictly controlled.
Other less essential aspects could be subject to monitoring, unrelated to
tranche releases; but even then, it is advisable to keep their number at a
minimum. Another corollary is the need for restraint in changing the con-
ditions of successive SALs. A related question--since it is more common to
find a target requiring several actions than vice versa--is to find a
proper balance between conditions based on results and on activities. A
last (but not least) recommendation is careful preparatory economic and
sector vork, in close contact with the country, as a basis for the determi-
nation  of  reasonable   policy   conditions,   which  then  become  shared
objectives.
Monitoring: Linking SAL to the SAP
56.      Chile's performance was so good that  it left few issues for moni-
toring. There were a few cases of promptly corrected near compliance and
notable overcompliances, as in GDP growth and in the current balance of
payments. The function, then, was one of alerting the authorities and
speeding their actions. A message here is that six-month tranching leads
to a timing of Bank missions that allows them to control execution and to
determine the basic elements of the next SAL. A corollary is that monitor-
itg should not be too narrowly focussed on compliance but also on linking
the SAL fimly to the overall SAP.
SAL Time Limitations and Phase-Out Plan
57.      Projections of the capital account  of the Chilean balance of pay-
ments show that debt-service obligations will continue to represent a heavy
burden. This observation suggests the convenience of setting a time limit-
-three to five years at most as a rule of thumb--for successive SALs and a
prospective phase-out program from the onset, in order to avoid substitut-
ing debt accumulation for domestic efforts.    There is a need for a fairly
gradual phase-out scheme in  countries  whose  export earnings are volatile
and dependent on external factors.   A  final matter related to phasing out



- Xviii -
is the possibility that the country's good behavior may be penalized, since
it repurchased its debts at higher prices than others and may be excluded
from the Brady plan. While this is at most a half truth, a corollary is to
avoid, as far as possible, perverse incentives in solutions to the debt
problem.
Lessons for Evaluation: Grasping the 'Black Box' of Inter-
relationships
S8.      Finally, there are lessons to  learn  about evaluation itself.  It
is in the nature of evaluations to try to grasp all the richness of reality
without pre-conceive4, rigid formats and to look beyond the mere control of
goal and target compliance.    But  it  also helps  evaluation to know, as
explicitly as possible, the simplified 'model' of reality that lies behind
a program.
59.      An important  element  is,  thus. the methodology  of evaluation
itself. The objective is to grasp the 'black box' of interrelations among
variables and among objectives and policies, but to stop short of con-
structing full-fledged, sophisticated models.    That is, to have available
the few computer runs that were taken into account in designing the program
or in setting targets would be useful for understanding the rationale
applied and the options faced. Considerations of efficiency and cost point
in the same direction. A practice of the utmost importance in evaluation,
however, is contact with a large and varied number of people in the recip-
ient country itself. They bring to the evaluation points of view, empiri-
cal data, and a variety of perceptions and value judgments impossible to
grasp or even imagine from  afar.    It  is this vivid experience that adds
content and conviction to the evaluation, an exercise always in danger of
remaining formal or superficial.
60.      A final element is institutional memorys not only the availabili-
ty of records, but also the ease and speed of retrieval, selection, and
classification. Institutional memory in the Bank is satisfactory and quite
good in comparative terms.   The costs of obtaining additional improvements
are probably high in terms of a scarce resource--staff time.
Some Open Issues
A Question of Timing
61.      A first open issue refers to the timing of the Bank's active pres-
ence in the country.  Until early  1983  the Chilean economic team was so
convinced that its own program was flawless, that it could not be motivated
to enter into a meaningful policy discussion with the Bank. The Bank sub-
mitted important memoranda to the authorities showing that their present
policies were untenable, but such presentations were essentially ignored.
However, a small window of opportunity may have opened in mid-1983 during
which a decisive Bank effort to put together a SAL might have elicited a
more positive Government  response.   Would  a  SAL in 1983--by supporting
Minister Caceres' effort and reduce some of the costs of his policy--have



- xxix -
extended the program and so avoided the more expensive adjustment later?
Or would a SAL at that time  have  been insufficient in any case to sustain
politically the program, making more difficult the Bank's reappearance
afterwards? From the Bank's side, the proper questions ares Would the
Bank have given Chile a SAL in 1983 if the preparations at the technical
level were ready? Or would it have waited in any case until 1985 because
the international financial atmosphere and the political sensitivities of
its Executive Board were not favorable?
62.      A majority of observers feel that had the lank come forward with a
SAL for Chile in 1983, both the economic and the political environments
were not sufficiently favorable to give the SAL good cbances of turning the
economic tide. Maybe, even at high cost, the experience of minimal relief
and the danger of retreating from a 'liberal" approach was a necessary
condition for gathering solid support behind a new stabilixation-plus-
adjustment effort. By the same  token, it  is not clear that the interna-
tional flnancial coamunity or the Bank's Board were ready to approach the
relation with Chile decisively and with a medium-term view. In fact, such
a view started to evolve at the end of 1983 but only gained full acceptance
in late 1984. Against this background of doubtful counterfactual histor-
ies, there is the conclusive evidence of the successful SAL operations
started in 1985, when the situation was ripe for action and both Chile and
the Bank seized the opportunity.
Income-Distributional and Allocative Implications of the Fiscal
Costs
63.      A subject of debate in  Chile  is whether the fiscal costs (bene-
fits) of some important policies during 1985-88 could have been substan-
tially cut (extended)s    specifically,  the  salvage and rehabilitation of
banks and corporations5 the debt conversions; and the privatization of
enterprises (the last two were not part of the SALs but have important
implications for the SAP and its oustainability). The whole question has
important income-distributional and allocative consequences. For the first
theme, relevant figures of the public sector have been obtained.

These figures show both the size of the financial crisis for public fi-

its rapid falling off after 1985, partly as a consequence of SAL condition-
