through increased public indebtedness (domestic and foreign).
Social Effects of SAP
64.      Whatever the opinions about the growth rate of the Chilean economy
from 1973 to  1984,  the  lower-income  segments  of the population clearly
carried a severe burden in the form of high, prolonged unemployment and
relatively low average real wages (the figures are worse if the EEPs are
included). The SAP had positive effects on growth, employment, and real
wages (better if the EUPs are included), but the Governtment's policies in
the social sectors were  severely  restricted by public expenditure auster-
ity. Officials of the previous administration defended the policies on the
basis that good macroeconomic performance--in the face of the problems
confronted in 1985--was the best possible response to social needs, that
aggregate social expenditures did not decline under the SAP, and that the
SAP more effectively achieved social goals because of increases in produc-
tivity and a better targeting of expenditures to the poorest strata of
society.
65.      Critics of the  past  Government,  often  referring  to the entire
period of its tenure, point out that SAP policies imposed a severe burden
on the low-income segment of the population; that high-income groups cap-
tured a large share of social benefits (for example, housing) because many
of the program elements were not redistributive (for example, the social
security system); that there were many inequities and no social safety net;
and that some programs, while effective in a narrow focus, were short-
sighted and not integrated into sectoral strategies, especially in health
and education. Figures from different sources give divergent views of the
evolution of social expenditure.   There  are  no uncontested answers.  The
opposite argument appears well-founded and  strong.   With regard to social
expenditures the picture is mixed. Evidence tends to show declining public
health expenditures and a less than progressive structure, although several
specific programs (such as maternal and child care) were successful and
oriented toward the poorest. On the other hand, other categories of social
expenditures increased markedly, most notably social security and social
welfare.
The SAP and the Achievement of Sustained Growth
66.      A question of the utmost  importance is whether the four-year SAP,
supported by the international financial institutions, has effectively
placed Chile on the path of sustained growth--to keep unemployment at
acceptable rates while managing service of the foreign debt.
67.      Economic progress since the crisis  has been remarkable.  GDP rose
7S in 1988 (the third consecutive year above 5.5Z)--regaining in per capita
terms its 1981 peak. Open unemployment dropped from 14.5? in 1982 to 8.8?
in 1988, and the  EEPs  have  practically  ended.  Although consumption is
16.32 below its 1982 record, inv.. -ient recovered from 9.8S of GDP (less
than the capital stock depreciation) to 16.8Z in 1988, supported by an
upsurge in the domestic savings (from 9.42 of GDP in 1982 to 24Z now). The



- xxxi -
trade balance moved from a US$2.7 billion deficit in 1981 to a US$2.2
billion surplus in 1988, vita non-copper export growth outperforming copper
export growth every year, even after the sharp copper price recovery since
1987. The terms of trade are 52 off their peak of 100 in 1980 (the trough
was 76 in 1986). The current balance of payments deficit was still US$200
million in 1988, down from US$4.7 billion in 1981 and US$2.1 billion in
1984. The external debt declined from a maximum of US$20.7 billion in 1986
to US$19.0 billion in 1988. The usual ratios improved as followss
* total foreign debt/annual exports, from 4571 in 1986 to
230X now;.
* debtIGDP, from 1271 in 1986 to 862 today;
* interest on foreign debtlexports, from 52? in 1982 to 22?
currently; and
e   interest on domestic  and  foreign  debtiDP, from 121  in
1985 to 8? in 1988.
The worst fiscal year was 1982, with a current deficit of 4.4? of GDP; in
1988 it was a 7.6? surplus, mostly due to a decline of expenditures from
30.71 to 24.52 of GDP. The overall deficit of 4.42 of GDP became a 0.52
surplus. The Central Bank deficit also has lost significance lately.
Difficult Economic Management Challenges Ahead
68.      Thus, today's picture of the  Chilean  economy may suggest that it
is well on the path of self-sustained  growth.   A closer look at the facts
and figures as well as at likely trends and developments, however, tends to
qualify such assessment and to raise important doubts. As indicated ear-
lier, a significant part of GDP expansion in the past two years is directly
attributable to positive external shocks; the excess of domestic savings
over investment does not cover interest on the foreign debt; thus, addi-
tional foreign finance and renegotiations will be needed, as well as con-
tinued efforts to mobilize domestic savings.
69.      Other questions come  from  future  trends  and developments, both
external and internal.   The  economy  is  still highly dependent on copper
exports. The price of copper has passed its peak--some experts predict a
decline to historical levels--though initially the fiscal impact will be
cushioned by resorting to the CSF.   The growth of the leading fruit export
is declining, except for some specialty and new products. Although there
has been an expansion of capacity in the production of copper, gold, for-
estry products, and in efficient import substitutions, there are health and
education needs whose satisfaction has been long delayed. Similarly, there
will be higher investment requirements in economic infrastructure and pro-
duction, both because of previous postponements and because of the need for
additional capacity. On balance then, in spite of the remarkable accom-
plishment of the Government's SAP and of the supporting SALs, the new
authorities face a difficult economic management challenge in the years
ahead.



PROGRAM PERPOENANCE AUDIT REPORT
CIIILE
FIRST, SECOND, AND THIRD STRUCTURAL ADJUSTHMENT LOANS
(LOANS 2625-Cl, 2767-CH AND 2892-CH)
I. INTRODUCTION
1.01     As described in Section II of the Program Completion Report (PCR),
Chile's sustained efforts toward structural adjustment date to 1973, and
have gone through different phases.   A  crucial year was 1985.  First, the
Government and the 11F entered into an Extended Fund Facility (EFF)
arrangement in 1985, followed later in the year by the first of the Vorld
Bank's Structural Adjustment loans (SALs) and by creditor banks new loans.
These provisions completed a financial and policy package that set the
basis for the still ongoing Structural Adjustment Program (SAP).
1.02     From the point of view  of  this Program Performance Audit Report,
the Bank's participation in Chile provides two sources to draw lessons from
the ex-perience: the Chilean SAP as a whole, and the role and imbrication
(or *overlap') of the Bank's invulvement within the SAP it helped to
energize.   The  relation  between  a  program  performance  audit  and its
corresponding PCR is analogous to that between external and internal audits
of a firm.   The  latter  concentrates  on  specificity, whereas the former
takes a wider perspective.
1.03     The organization of  the  Report  reflects  this conception.  This
chapter summarizes  and  appraises  the  economic  background.   Chapter II
describes and appraises different aspects of the SAP and the Bank's role
through its  SALs:    implementation;  and  economic,  sectoral  and social
impact. Chapter III provides a comprehensive evaluation of the Bank's SALs
and Chapter IV presents the main findings and issues of the audit. The
Technical Appendix contains an analytical framework for quantitative ex
post evaluation of structural adjustment efforts.
A. Background
1.04     From the 19309 until  1973  the  predominant economic policies of
Chilean Governments were import substitution and active intervention
(extended controls of prices and quantities, widespread taxes and subsi-
dies) and increasing public  ownership  and management.   The results were
often disappointing: low growth, rapid inflation, inefficient production,
dependence on copper exports (60Z of total exports), and balance of pay-
ments problems reflected in a growing foreign debt. Periodic efforts to
reverse these trends proved insufficient.
1.05     The Government that  took  power  in  September  1973 faced world-
record inflation, negative international reserves, and arrears in foreign



- I -
debt  payments.    Output  had  been  declining  since  1971,  the  central
Government deficit exceeded 202 of gross domestic product (GPP), the money
supply was out of control, the financial system was nearly destroyed, and
the domestic currency was grossly overvalued.
1.06     The administration's economic policy stressed private initiatives,
free market forces, and the opening of foreign trade and capital movements.
It gradually freed prices, removed tax and subsidy distortions, lessened
trade barriers sharply, and eliminated foreign exchange controls. After a
huge devaluation, the exchange rate was periodically adjusted. The public
sector was reduced, its deficit became a surplus in 1976, and the Govern-
ment's efficiency improved dramatically.
1.07     In 1975 real copper  prices  hit  their  lowest  level in 20 years
while inflation continued.    In  April  1975  the administration applied a
drastic austerity program that was followed by the worst depression since
the 1930s with the trough in 1976:1.
1.08     By 1977 GDP had  returned  to  1974  levels, and in 1978 inflation
dropped to 30? (down from 6002 in 1973). Non-traditional exports grew 30?;
despite low copper prices and heavy foreign debt service, international
reserves were 4.5 months of imports. Government efficiency kept improving.
The boom (7Z yearly growth) lasted until 1980 and led the Bank to foresee
continuous economic growth, alleviation of extreme poverty, and full
employment by  1985.    Instead,  in  1982-83  Chile  experienced its worst
depression in 50 years--caused by inconsistent macro-economic policies,
extremely  low  copper  prices,  high   real  interest  rates,  and  global
recession.
1.09     In July 1979 the authorities had  pegged the peso to the US dollar
(the peg would last three years); since Chilean inflation was twice US
Inflation, the peso appreciated by more than 25? while wages remained
indexed. Tradable goods industries, already suffering from tariff reduc-
tions, were severely affected.    In  spite  of  clear indications that the
growing overvaluation of the currency was imposing a heavy burden on the
domestic economy, the authorities refused to devalue. They believed that
domestic prices would fall, thus restoring international competitiveness.
Capital movements were freed, and with foreign real interest rates well
below domestic rates, the incentive to borrow abroad was strong. There was
an import boom, fueled by a doubling of the external debt to 70? of GDP,
most of it private. The 1980 and 1981 trade deficits proved unsustainable,
the resource gap reached 102 of GDP, and interest payments on foreign debt
rose to 7S of GDP.   By  the  time voluntary outside lending ended in 1982,
real world interest rates had reached record highs, copper prices had
plummeted to record lows, and Chile was forced to devalue.
1.10     The 1982 expenditure reduction  and devaluation were the immediate
cause of a massive crisis: a 15? fall in GDP (due in part to the deterio-
ration in the terms of trade), 25Z unemployment (despite 500,000 jobs
created by emergency programs), and insolvency of half the productive
sectors and of the largest banks (partially hidden because of the



interlocking relations between corporations and financial institutions).
In January 1983 the government was forced to intervene some of the largest
banks. To keep firms and banks alive, the Central Bank decided to provide
subsidized loans to debtors in general.
1.11     Domestic problems were compounded by the high foreign debt and the
unfavorable international setting.   Interest  on  the foreign debt reached
302 of exports and 50? of domestic savings. The Government was forced to
assume the guarantee of part of the private sector's foreign obligations in
order to reschedule the 1983-84 amortization payments and to get new money
for  the  1983  current   accoint   deficit.      Loans  from  international
institutions were not  forthcoming:    earlier  reluctance  to use official
borrowing had drained the Chilean official loan pipeline. The severity of
the 1983 adjustment--real consumption 72 lower, real investment 15? down-
-put the stabilization program on track again and sharply improved the
external accounts. But the fiscal situation deteriorated.
1.12     A recovery attempt  in  1984  proved  unsustainable.  Imports rose
172, and the current account deficit again reached 10t of GDP. An ensuing
devaluation and an increase in import tariffs from 20? to 35? failed to
reduce the need for more foreign funds.
1.13     In early 1985 a new economic  team  took charge. Despite the com-
pression of income and imports (452 since 1981) and steady growth in export
volumes, Chile still required significant capital inflows. Copper, even at
very low prices, still provided more than 502 of export earnings. Chile's
foreign debt of US$20 billion equaled its GDP and was five times the value
of its merchandise exports.   The  economic  agenda also included  recovery
from extremely low levels of domestic savings and investment, as well as
Improvement in bank and corporate finances.
1.14     As the Government  decisively  addressed  its agenda, the interna-
tional institutions began to support the adjustment effort in the medium
term.  A US$2 billion package was  assembled:   an SDR 750 million ?2"W from
the IMF, the Bank's US$250 million SAL I (with the possibility of
successive SALs), and US$1.1 billion in new money from creditor banks. The
financial and policy package was designed to redress the balance of pay-
ments imbalance, to increase savings sharply while reorienting investment,
and to rebuild the financial system.    The  conditions set by the Bank and
the IM? were previously agreed and mutually reinforcing: the IMF estab-
lished macroeconomic targets for the monetary, fiscal, and foreign sectors;
and the Bank set conditions for export promotion, savings mobilization, the
public investment program (PIP) financial rehabilitation, and employment
generation.   The  distribution  of  functions  between  these institutions
worked well and with quick effect in Chiles inflation dropped from 30? to
below 152? GDP grew at  over  52 annually; unemployment was halved, exports
expanded over 6? yearly; the  current account deficit fell sharply, invest-
ment and savings (private and public) increased; and debt ratios improved.
1.15     In 1986 and 1987 the Bank provided two more SALs, each in the same
amount of SAL I; thus US$750 million in Bank assistance was disbursed from



- 4-
1985 to 1988 in support of Chile's SAP. Priorities of the Bank's program
as it evolved are mentioned  in  Chapter  II.    SAL  II (1986) and SAL III
(1987) continued to press for orientation leading to self-sustaining, long-
term growth.
B. Appraisal
1.16     Policies implemented in April  1975--and the economic upswing that
followed, which in 1977 surpassed previous peaks--followed the predictions
of the monetary approach to the balance of payments as it was then
promulgated. Vision at the time overlooked some of the shortcomings of the
approach, and these later forced upon the approach a sudden end and very
costly corrections.
1.17     Thus, in early 1980  the  Bank foresaw continuous economic growth,
alleviation of extreme poverty, and full employment in Chile by 1985.
Instead, external and internal factors forced the country into its worst
depression in 50 years.
1.18     The flaws of the monetarist  model have subsequently been unveiled
(notably, in a 1982 World Bank seminar).l1 Among those significant in the
Chilean case were:
* the appreciation of the exchange rate, which led to a level
of imports that was unwarranted from a long-term perspec-
tive and to an unsustainable accumulation of foreign debt:
* the widely held belief that private foreign debt was only
the  creditors'  risk   and   did   not   pose  a  domestic
macroeconomic problem;
X   the  expectation  that  domestic  firms  could  adjust  and
survive the simultaneous effects of uphill competition with
imports and very high real interest rates.
1.19     Another unnoticed misconception  at  this  time  was the confusion
between market freedom and the absence of prudential rules in the financial
sector. This led to an unhealthy network of relations between banks and
enterprises--and to their later mutual collapse, with its high social
costs.
1.20     After the crisis detonated there was considerable delay in recog-
nizing the depth of the  financial  problems.    Only  In late 1982 did the
first quantitative estimates of its magnitude become available. There was
also a delay--shared with the world financial community in general--in
1/ See N. Ardito Barletta, M.I. Blejer, and L. Landau, eds., Economic
Liberalization and Stabilization Policies in Arsentina, Chile, and
Uruauays   Avplications  of  the Monetary  Approach  to  the Balance of
Payments (Washington: World Bank, 1984).



understanding and spelling out  the  full Implications of the international
debt crisis. The debt crisis loomed over solutions from the onset of the
Chilean crisis In August 1982 until 1985.
1.21     Although the Bank kept up  its  routine monitoring of the Chilean
economy In the early 1980s, there are indications that the Bank's intensive
analytical involvement was only resumed In 1984 (in part because of the
Chilean authorities' reluctance to use official borrowing). The complemen-
tary role of the Bank and IMF in addressing the situation with a longer-
term perspective in 1984-85--the 5FF/SAL I financial and policy package was
a forerunner of the P.S. Baker Plan--deserves full credit.
II. DESIGN. InaLENTATION, AND IMPACT OF ADJUSTHMENT PROGRAMS
A. Design
2.01     The Government's Immdiate response  to  the 1982-83 crisis was ad
hoc, as if Chile were facing only short-term problems. By mid-1983 there
was increasing acknowledgment of the need for a coherent longer-term pro-
gram, and In early 1984 an initial three-year PIP was introduced and later
improved.
The Structural Adjustment Program (SAP)
2.02     The 1985-87  SAP made  consistent macroeconomic  projections and
specified the structural policy measures to reach three of its immediate
goals: to accelerate and diversify exports, to correct the savings-invest-
ment disequilibrium, and to alleviate financial stress. The question of
employment generation vas also tackled, but the scope for solving it was
limited.
2.03     By late 1986 the SAP's  initial phase  had been successful.  Full
implementation--and its effects, sustainable output and employment growth-
-was within reach of the economic team, provided that the world economy
kept expanding moderately and that trade restrictions did not worsen. A
distinct element of the SAP's second phase was more attention to the
recapitalization and reprivatization of banks and corporations. The other
priorities were an increase in public savings to 4.4 X of GDP, an austere
and efficient PIP, and the strengthening of export incentives and social
security programs.
2.04     By mid-1987 it was  clear  that Chile had adjusted by sacrificing
current consumption in favor of savings, servicing the foreign debt, and
long-term growth that would maintain creditworthiness despite one of the
heaviest debt service burdens in the hemisphere. If copper prices remained
low, and if the debt burden continued to absorb a third of export earnings
and more than half of domestic savings, there would be little scope for
changes in policy and room for only moderate increases in consumption. But
the SAP also entailed diversifying the economy away from copper and build-
ing new, dynamic comparative advantages while Jncreasing savings and



- 6 -
investment beyond traditional rates, and this could be accomplished only by
several years of the current policies and new patterns of savings and
investment. Thus the SAP's orientation was to maintain the framework of
economic policy and to emphasize long-term capital markets, social securi-
ty, and health, which affected its social sustainability.
B. SAL I (2625-CH, 25 October 1985)
2.05     SAL I was  part  of  a  US$2  billion  financial package that also
included a three-year, SDR 750 million EFP and US$1.1 billion in new money
from commercial banks.21 Chile was able to reschedule amortizations with
financial and official creditors falling due in 1985 and 1986 (US$120
million). SAL I's aim was to support the Chilean Government's 1985-87
program of economic adjustmefiLt  and  recovery.   It stressed the correction
balance of payments disequilibria while alleviating the impact on growth
and productive employment by stimulating exports, increasing domestic
savings, reactivating the private sector, and capitalizing the banking
sector.
2.06     The main areas covered by the  SAP in the medium-term were, first,
to strengthen incentives for noncopper exports and, second, to increase
public savings and the efficiency of the PIP and to ameliorate banking and
corporate finances.   Complementary  studies  to improve public management,
state enterprise divestment, employment creation, export assistance, and
forestry development programs would be partially financed by the Bank's
prior Public Sector Management Technical Assistance Loan (TAL 2504-CH,
April 1985). The terms of SAL I were repayment in 15 years (including
three years grace) and disbursement in two tranches: half upon effective-
ness and half after a performance review within six months of effective-
ness. It could be used to finance any imports except luxury and military
goods.
Export Promotion Program
2.07     Chile made  important  adjustments  to  its  export  incentives in
1983-84. Poor price prospects for its prim.mry exports made expansion of
noncopper exports a top  priority.    The  program  planned to achieve this
through the reduction of import tariffs, a real exchange rate attractive
for export-oriented investment, coordination of investments in forestry and
energy, a larger  role  for  PROCHIDU    (the  Government's export assistance
agency) in the promotion of small producers' exportables, and stabilization
of the effects of copper market fluctuations. Following their pledges of
free trade and low and uniform import tariffs, the authorities reduced
tariffs to 202 and vowed to apply anti-dumping surcharges only when unfair
trade practices were confirmed.
2.08     After a 252  real  devaluation  of  the  peso,  the government had
undertaken to maintain the real exchange rate stable throughout the three-
year SAP. An essential feature would be a Copper Stabilization Fund (CSF)
2/ SAL 1, in the amount of US$250 million, was accompanied by a US$300
million B-Loan, half of which was guaranteed by the Bank.



to  resist  the  exchange   rate  appreciation  (depreciation)  and  demand
pressures that arise when volatile copper prices jump (decline). Although
direct export incentives were difficult to institute because of the
limitation on fiscal resources for subsidies, the administration had
strengthened export stimuli by various tax and credit measures and was
preparing an insurance system to reduce the risk of exporters' opening new
markets as well as legislation to develop export insurance firms. PROCHILE
was being reoriented to organize small-and medium-size exporters, to
support reaching new markets, to promote and coordinate participation in
international  trade  fairs,  and   to  identify--and  analyze  and  define
incentives for--nontraditional exports and potential export goods and their
quality improvement.
2.09     A high-level  public/private  task  force  had  been  appointed to
develop and implement public and private investments for high-potential,
rapid expansion of forestry exports. Only moderate increases were expected
in copper prices. The government strategy was to promote only a moderate
rise in CODELCO's (the Chilean State Copper Corporation) export production,
from 20 to 22? by 1996, and to open up high-potential ore bodies to private
investors.   As  for  import  substitution,  the  Government  was trying to
attract private investors into package deals linking concession rights in
the Magellan oil fields with exploration obligations in the Precordillera
region. The Government had also received International Finance Corporation
(IPC) support for an open-pit coal mine in the Magellan area and had agreed
with private investors for the construction of a methanol processing plant.
To discourage inefficient import substitution, the authorities had reduced
reference price bands for wheat, oilseeds, and sugar and considered further
reducing sugar price supports in 1986.
Savings Mobilization and the PIP
2.10     While substantial new  investment was  needed  for the economy to
grow, interest payments on the external debt would be absorbing much of
Chile's savings for  years  aheads    thus  the need  to increase domestic
saving. The major effort would be borne by the public sector, which might
have to service part of the private sector's foreign obligations (which the
Government had  guaranteed).    Challenged  by  the  impact  of  the social
security reform (22 or more of GDP), the high share of social expenditures
(30? of the total), the income tax reform, and the financial relief of the
banking system, the government would resort to value-added and luxury goods
taxes, sales of assets (pension plan administrators--AFPs--were to invest
in their purchases) and expenditure  austerity.   Some of the measures were
expected to mobilize private savings; e.g., AFPs would soon invest up to 42
of GDP, and income tax reforms would amount to 0.6? of GDP.



-8-

A three-year PIP (to avoid the  stop-go of annual programs) was directed to
promote exports and to remove bottlenecks affecting private investment
growth. Thus, the PIP's priorities were copper expansion, energy invest-
ments, and infrastructure to export and to generate jobs in agriculture,
forestry, and selected industrial activities. Equally important, following
a World Bank review, several investment projects were eliminated from the
PIP (the Santiago subway, large irrigation projects) in favor of higher
priorities (road maintenance, small and fast irrigation rehabilitation).
3inancial Rehabilitation
2.12     To deal with the joint crisis of Chile's business firms and banks,
the Central Bank during 1982-84 had implemented across-the-board debt-
rescheduling programs, a preferential exchange rate system, and subsidized
swap operations. The Government had been forced to guarantee foreign debts
of the private financial sector.   This made it necessary to distribute the
losses of the Chilean financial system--then at least US$3.5 billion-
-entirely within the country, with major macroeconomic repercussions. The
Government sustained massive losses from transfers and commitments to
continue some of the guarantees in the future. An unsolved issue remained:
where would the resources come from to pay for these losses beyond 1987
(1985-86 would be covered by foreign borrowing).
2.13     The solution called for limiting  the subsidies to banks and finms
and finding resources to pay for the past subsidies, which up to that point
had been financed by  issuing  Central  Bank  notes.    In July 1985 it was
announced that the preferential exchange rate programs and currency swap
operations would be phased out by December 1986, reducing the cost of these
programs from 42 of GDP in 1984 to 1.52 of GDP in 1986. To improve
repayment performance of bad loans, the generalized reprogramming of



- 9 -
domestic loans would end by November 1985. The authorities also planned to
strengthen prudential legislation affecting companies and banks and the
supervision of the latter.
2.14     To recapitalize the  intervened banks,  legislation in April 1985
established that Government participation in the capital of such banks
should not exceed 491, of which at least one-fifth would be sold annually
to the private sector.   More  important,  the  Central Bank bought the bad
loans from the banks at face value, but ordered that the banks would not be
allowed to pay dividends on the shares of original capital until the entire
bad loan portfolio had been repurchased.
Employment
2.15     Although there were indications of a more elastic relation between
output and employment, the low economic growth expected for 1985-87 made
indispensable the continuation of the emergency employment programs (EEPs)
whose cost was 1S of GDP.  The government wanted to enhance the social and
productive aspects of these programs  and  it intended to undertake a labor
study toward this end.
C. SAL 1I (2767 CH, 21 November 1985)
2.16     At the  time  of  SAL  II's  approval,  the  steering committee of
Chile's creditor banks had accepted the World Bank's insistence that
commercial  creditors  should   finance   the  remaining  foreign  eschange
requirements of the  country  for  1987.   The  precise  amount would be
estimated by  December 1986.   The  Bank  was also exploring cofinancing
possibilities vith the Inter-American Development Bank (IDB).
2.17     The objectives of  SAL  II were  to  support the government's SAP
along the lines of SAL It  to strengthen incentives for noncopper exports,
to increase public savings and the efficiency and impact of public expendi-
tures, and to improve  banking  and  corporate  finances, with the emphasis
this time on the latter.    Complementary  studies to improve health sector
management, employment generation, export assistance programs, and a fores-
try development program would be partially financed by a previous TAL from
the Bank. The terms  of  SAL  I1  were  extended  to repayment in 17 years
(including four years grace) and disbursement  of the US$250 million in two
tranches: "front-loading' of US$150 million upon effectiveness, and US$100
million after a performance review within  six months of effectiveness. As
before, the new  SAL  could  finance  any  imports but luxury and military
goods.
Financial Rehabilitation
2.18     To develop an  efficient  and  safe  capital market,  in 1986 the
Government was taking measures to recapitalize private firms (through case
by case renegotiations of their debts) to recapitalize the banking system,
to reprivatize the intervened banks and companies, and to strengthen the
supervisory and legal framework of the capital market and the financial



- 10 -
sector. The authotities expected that by end-1986 all viable firms would
have completed financial arrangements.   By August 1986 many of the inter-
vened enterprises had  been  sold,  and  the  comissions  handling the two
largest holdings, COPEC and  CCU, were  to  be  dissolved when the sale of
their companies was completed.   By  the  same date the government had sold
two Intervened banks, merged the other three into two (60? of whose stock
was already private); the administration of these banks was to become
private by end-1986. The banking system again had positive equity capital;
the annual cost of the bailout was about US$100 million, to be shared by
the Central Bank and old  shareholders.   The legislature was considering a
new baitking law, the financial situation of corporations approached normal-
ity, and the stock exchange recovered.    But to contain the Central Bank's
losses, the authorities would end the provision of subsidies and toughen
its stand with private banks.
Savings Mobilization
2.19     Public revenues were expected to decline by 22 of GDP in 1986, the
net result of the continuation of the tax reform and depressed interna-
tional oil and copper prices.   But  sales of public enterprises would pro-
vide US$100 million, and expenditures were to drop by 3.52 of GDP because
of wage restraint and reductions in unemployment and social (mostly health)
programs. The three-year PIP took account of reconstruction works for the
March 1985 earthquake and emphasized infrastructure projects for private
investments (e.g., transportation) and upgrading production in public
enterprises. Tax reforms, the growth of pension funds, the strengthening
and reform of the banking system, and the consolidation of the stock
exchange, it was thought, would stimulate private savings. The scope for
debt purchases was expanded in May 1986; such measures, together with a
favorable climate for foreign investment and privatization, were intended
to increase both direct foreign investment and local savings.
Exnort Promotion
2.20     The Government had renewed its commitment to maintain low and even
import tariffs (since June 1986 they were fixed by law) and a constant real
exchange rate throughout  1986-87.    It  also  planned to modify operating
procedures to stimulate further small- and medium-size exporters. The
administration would define its investment strategy and policy for fores-
try--of high export potential--upon a commission's report that was due in
December 1986. Export-oriented foreign investment was already under way in
minerals (methanol, copper, mixed salts).
Social Poiicies
2.21     The authorities planned to perfect targeting of its social expend-
itures.  In 1986 it would  decentralize  administration of its main social
programs. Still, it was probable that low investment in health would
decrease access to health facilities, with long-term effects. A study due
in March 1987 was to provide the  basis for a medium-term strategy.  If GDP
grew at 5  in 1986, expenditures  on  EEPs could be reduced, but these pro-
grams would last at least 3 more  years.  Studies under the Bank's TAL will
shed light on the productivity of these programs.



- 11 -
D. SAL III (2892-CH, 16 December 1987)
2.22     When SAL III was approved,  the  SAP financing for 1988 was fairly
well set as a result of Chile's 1987 negotiations with its foreign credi-
tors to cover 1987 and 1988. The international banks had agreed to Oretim-
ing' and 'repricing' interest payments and spreads, to restructuring loans
for US$12.5 billion (with four years grace), and to extend US$1.7 billion
in short-term credits. The Paris Club had also refinanced loans to the
public sector.
2.23     The purpose of SAL III was to continue support of the Government's
SAPs to maintain the sound framework for economic policy and to reinforce
its social sustainability, with emphasis on long-term capital markets, and
on social security and health. SAL 1I1, in the same amount and in the same
terms as SAL II, was to be disbursed in two equal tranches--the first upon
effectiveness, and the second after a performance review within 12 months
of effectiveness. The SAP still had its risks: inability to generate
savings and investment, adverse terms of trade, and world interest rates,
and insufficient access to foreign capital, which could result in unsatis-
factory growth or impede  debt  servicing.   Domestic political uncertainty
could also affect private investment.
Financial Rehabilitation
2.24     The aithorities would  continue  reconstruction  of the financial
system, enforring collections of previously reprogrammed debts and re-
stricting pa'aents of profits to  precrisis owners who still had repurchase
obligations with the Central Bank. It would also augment its monitoring of
the collection of debts sold to the Central Bank, improve banks' efficiency
to speed up their loan repurchases from the Central Bank, and publicize the
low limits on State guarantees to depositors. Once rehabilitation of the
banking system was completed, the authorities would focus on diversifying
the portfolio and channeling funds of institutional savers (for example,
AhPs, which handle 60S of private savings) into equity and private debt.
2.25     To stimulate capital  markets,  the  Government's program included
fiscal measures to encourage equity investment and the growth of intermedi-
ation, changes in the all-important risk classification procedures, and
liberalizing the restrictions on institutional savers that had impeded
investments of acceptable risk.
Social Sectors
2.26     To improve the operation of  the  social security system, the Gov-
ernment intended to protect the security of the pension funds (tightening
regulatory control of their investments, and affirming the principles of
portfolio management); to increase the efficiency of the system (unifying
the collection of contributions and increasing transparency to promote
competition); to extend its long-term coverage (ensuring the continuation
of disability and survival insurance to the unemployed or part-time workers
and encouraging affiliation of the self-employed); and to enhance the
acceptance of the program (by simplified commission structures, easier



- 12 -
choice of companies, voluntary payroll deduction, and reduction of fixed
fees). Public current nonpersonnel outlays for health care would rise by
151 in real terms  in  1988.    To  increase  the  output for this level of
expenditure, the authorities would consolidate the progress made in
preventive health care and extend it to cover chronic diseases and would
increase the efficiency of  the  decentralized  system.   To redress market
imperfections in operation of the rapidly expanding public health insurance
plans (ISAPRE8), the Government would strengthen their regulation and
clarify their respective role within the national health system.
Macroeconomic and Export Promotion Policies
2.27     SAL III would be  important in consolidating macroeconomic adjust-
ment policies in light of the politics surrounding the 1988 plesbicite-
-that is. only moderate increases in domestic consumption, diversification
into new exports, and efficient import substitution. To uphold competi-
tiveness, the Government would maintain and strengthen the CSF--to become
active in 1987:3--by narrowing its price bands and abandoning the annual
averaging procedures. The Government would also preserve the real exchange
rate by daily nominal devaluations, would review the export incentive
framework if nontraditional exports failed to grow in real terms as
planned, and would adequately disclose that foreign financing was forthcom-
ing. The authorities had also taken measures to offset some effects of the
202 uniform tariff. Finally, the Government intended to promote nontradi-
tional exports actively through an export insurance program (unsubsidized
and run by specialized private firms) and to provide small firms with
export financing (through creation of a fund to extend partial public guar-
antees for investment credits and to cover risks of working capital loans).
Noncopper exports were projected to grow at 6-82 annually in real terms.
Public Finance and Investments
2.28     The Government would increase public savings  to 5Z of GDP in 1988
(from 4.8 and 4.4 in previous years) and would limit the overall public
deficit to 1.61 of GDP in 1987 and less in 1988. The monetary and fiscal
program would absorb the cost of recapitalizing the banking system. The
PIP would amount to 7.51 of GDP.  The PIP's US$1.3 billion budget appeared
not to have major distortions, and minor changes had been made at the
Bank's suggestion (in housing and  petroleum).    The Bank would review the
PIP's progress with the Chilean authorities.
E. Aipraisal of SALs' ConceDt and Design
2.29     The Government's  main  thrust  of  the  response  to the economic
crisis was to  confront  short-term  problems.   The  design of policy was
adapted in the search for stability and relief of payments imbalances. The
economic policy framework was agreed with the IMF and incorporated condi-
tionalities required for the Fund's financial assistance. The two-year IMF
Stand-by arrangements for 1983-84 paved the way for the subsequent EFF and
two Compensatory Financing Facility (CPF) purchases in 1983 and 1985.



- 13 -
2.30     The economic policies  put  forward  underwent  several shifts and
setbacks in 1983-84--the result of  escalations  of the economic and finan-
cial crisis, oan one hand, and of the different views of successive economic
cabinets and government Insiders, on  the  other.   The main feature of the
1982-84 programs was the lack of quantified medium-term objectives. It was
not until 1985 that a medium-term scenario was envisaged, when the chances
of structural adjustment began to look more solid. The size of the foreign
debt became an important issue, and the vulnerability of the Chilean econ-
omy to external shocks became the cornerstone of the SAP. The 1983-84
transition period saw problems in policy design and implementation. Unan-
ticipated effects of the severe financial crisis and inadequate handling of
the exchange rate raised a considerable appreciation of the peso. The
program was conceived to counteract insufficient demand and a 15? unemploy-
ment rate and also included a contingency investment plan equivalent to
0.8? of CDP that could be activated in the second balf of 1984. So long as
the evolution of demand was positive, the contingency plan was not put in
operation. In the 1984 IMF agreement there was no formal monitoring of the
Central Bank's operational deficit and no limit to the short-term external
debt.
2.31     The program started in  1985  by  a  new economic team changed the
design of policy. On  the whole, more emphasis was placed on medium-term
solutions and new goals, and measures were agreed both with the IMF, which
continued supplying financial support and monitoring and with the World
Bank, which contributed to consolidation of the financial rehabilitation,
export incentives, public sector management, and so on while providing and
catalyzing additional finace. Both types of financial support shared the
objectives of stabilization and structural adjustment, but their condition-
alities did not necessarily overlap.    The IMF was interested in macroeco-
nomic policies and in reforming the exchange rate and trade areas to re-
store market freedom and  efficiency.    The  Bank was oriented more toward
export incentives, public sector resource management, financial rehabilita-
tion, and reprivatization. Targets of the Government program produced for
1985 were subject  to  further  revisions  depending on performance of the
economy.
2.32     The performance criteria were  modified  to gain more flexibility.
In the 1983-84 programs they were set for 12-month calendar periods. The
conditions in 1985 set the performance criteria for six-month periods. The
second tranche of SAL I could be released only after review of performance
and conditionalities six months  after  effectiveness.    The targets were
often reviewed, and it was the IMP staff's contention that the deviations
between actual and programmed outcomes reflected the best efforts of the
country to adjust its economy.    The validity of performance tests to re-
orient economic policy was one of the main concerns of the monitoring
activity. The I)P was evaluating performance in the field and kept the
Bank informed on the subject. Annual program targets established under the
EEF agreement were adapted to take account of changes and deviations from
original assumptions without  altering  medium-term  objectives.   The main
goals were to influence the rate of growth of GDP, the rate of inflation
(usually measured by the consumer price index, CPI), the current account



- 14 -
balance both in U.S. dollars and as a percentage of GDP. and the balance of
payments and fiscal balance as a proportion of GDP.
2.33     The SAP incorporated  contingency  elements  for more flexibility.
The PIP included a contingency item of O.52 of GDP to be activated during
the second half of the year if private expenditure dropped or if the gov-
ernment obtained long-term foreign finance in an amount higher than pro-
grammed. The 1986 program introduced the CS?, which was one of the condi-
tionalities of the Bank's SALs.   This  fund was created as a contingency
against unforeseen deviations in the copper price. The CSP mechanism was
in full operation by the beginning of 1988, when the reference price for
copper was established as a medium-term price rather than a projected price
for a given period.
2.34     The key contribution of the Bank since late 1984 was two-folds  to
instill a medium-term dimension and approach in economic policy and to make
such policy possible through direct and catalytic effects on financing. It
is generally recognized that successive short-term policies would have
doomed the adjustment effort to failure.   The permanent emphasis on export
promotion and savings mobilization and investment was well placed to alle-
viate the then most strategic wants of the economy.
2.35     Although the contribution of SAL II was less dramatic than that of
its predecessor, it was equally necessary to sustain the SAP. The main
additional emphasis was on cutting the terms and costs of financial reha-
bilitation, in which it succeeded. The orientation of SAL III--fast action
in the social sectors--while intended to balance the whole package, is more
disputed in some of its effects than were the earlier SALs (see Chapter
IV).
F. Implementation
Conditionalities and Performance
2.36     Chile was favored in that its previous stabilization policies were
applied in the context of different types of external finance obtained from
other international sources, particularly the IMF. This experience signif-
icantly reduced the number of conditionalities required in the granting of
the SALs. In order to evaluate results, however, the Bank's loans were
given in two tranches--the first upon effectiveness, and the second upon
compliance with the second-tranche conditionalities.
SAL I
2.37     The conditionalities for -the  release  of  SAL I's second tranche
were related to the five main objectives of the SAP.
2.38     Export Promotion.  The Government had already shown its commitment
to a realistic export incentive system by a 25? real devaluation of the
exchange rate (between September 1984 and July 1985) and a reduction of
import tariffs from 252 to a 202 even rate. Other anti-export biases had



- 15 -
been removed, and additional incentives had been provided to support new
exports, primarily from small and medium-sized producers. Before release
of the second tranche it was requireds
*    to revise Chile's export stimuli, to improve 1985 noncopper
export performance, and, if the projected 61 real growth
was not achieved, to change the incentives to the satisfac-
tion of the World Banks
*    to place in effect, by January  1, 1986, a CSF to stabilize
the fiscal revenues from CODELCO's exports.
2.39     Public PFinuces.  The SAP's priorities were to limit the growth of
public current expenditure, to follow up the PIP carefully. and to improve
the cost effectiveness of state  companies.   The public savings goals were
31 of GDP for 1985 and 4.5Z of GDP for 1986. A trimmed-down and export-
oriented PIP had been published.    The  conditions for disbursement of the
second tranche vere:
*    satisfactory  execution  of   the  1985  public  investment
budget;
*    review and agreement on a 1986 public sector budget;
*    introduction of fiscal measures  to achieve public savings
of about 4.51 of projected 1986 GDP.
An additional requirement was preparation of an action plan to improve
public institutions management.
2.40     Financial Rehabilitation.  To contain the losses from Central Bank
transfers, the Government had announced several measures to encourage the
restructuring or bankruptcy of the remaining insolvent firms--which were
the banks' debtors--and the recovery, with interested ownership, of finan-
cial entities' viable  financial  structures.    The  authorities, and Bank
staff had clearly begun to estimate more precisely future losses and their
monetary and fiscal consequences.   The  conditions for second tranche dis-
bursement in this regard were:
*    review of progress on  the bank capitalization programs and
of financial flows between those entities and the Central
Bank;
e    agreement on a program to share, within the domestic econo-
my, local banks' asset losses (contingent on a review of
the 1985 loan portfolio performance of commercial banks);
*    executive approval  of  satisfactory  legislation governing
transparency of bank and corporate ownership;



- 16 -
*    an end to the general  rescheduling of loans extended to or
by financial entities, a freeze on new concessional credit
lines for this purpose, the phasing out (by December 1986)
of the preferential exchange rate program, and elimination
of subsidized currency map operations.
2.41     Employment Generation.  Since  the  projected annual GDP growth of
3S would generate an employment growth rate of 2.5 and the labor force was
increasing 22 yearly, the unemployed and UEP beneficiaries would still be
162 of the work force by 1990, forcing maintenance of the UEPs. In 1985
the government began nine pilot programs to lmprove the efficiency and
reduce the costs of EEPs.    Two  studies,  under  the World Bank's Public
Sector Management TAL, were to assess employment policies and programs-
-specifically, cost-effective reforms, reduction of the program of miniMUM
employment (PEN) and the program of employment for heads of families
(POJH), and employment generation.
2.42     Other Areas.  Other  conditions  to  trigger the second tranche of
SAL I were as follows:
-    development of a forestry plan;
-    satisfactory progress on the other studies under the Public
Sector Management  TAL:    medium-term  fiscal and monetary
projections, actions to increase exports by small- and
medium-scale producers, public firm management, and meas-
ures for local sugar prices to reflect international prices
in the long run.
SAL II
2.43     The conditionalities of SAL  II  naturally  reflected the goals of
the SAP and SAL I. While these objectives are roughly the same as in SAL
I, their relative emphasis and scope varied over time.
2.44     Financial Rehabilitation.  Recapitalization  of the financial sys-
tem was largely completed; the restructuring process was expected to last
two more years because of the complexity of negotiations. What was needed
was to move from the achieved short-term restoration of solvency to ensur-
ing the system's long-term stability.  Thus, the commitments for the second
tranche of SAL II to become operational weres
*    to make substantial progress  in the program for recapital-
izing the banking system;
*    to show that the intervened banks were or would be able to
generate operational profits by July 31, 1986 and to reach
a debt-equity ratio between 15sl and 18:1 (this would en-
tail additional purchases of US$250 million in substandard
portfolios by the Central Bank; these would not mean losses
for the Central Bank but, in principle, only an annual
subsidy of 0.1? of GDP);



- 17 -
*    to strengthen the monitory functions of the Superintendency
of Banks and Financial Institutions (SBIF)--the asset qual-
ity of banks would be monitored by the bank, and both the
commercial banks" performance and portfolio would be ana-
lyzed over time;
*    to put into effect a  system of bank deposit insurance--the
guarantee fund for sight deposits would be based on new
reserve requirements of twice the net worth of commercial
banks, supplemented by the Central Bank; specifically, by
June 30, 1987 a complete guarantee for deposits up to
US$2,000 would be operative;
*    to formulate a fiscal  and monetary program for absorption
of costs incurred by the Central Bank in recapitalizing the
banking system (new projections and contingency plans for
alternative interest rate forecasts);
*    to eliminate the remaining net  cost to the Central Bank of
the deposit scheme for the restructured external debt of
the financial sector;
*    to phase out  the  preferential  exchange  rate program for
foreign currency debts larger than US$50,000.
2.45     Public Finances.   Disbursement  of  the  second  tranche would be
conditional upont
*    a satisfactory execution of the 1986 PIP;
*    preparation of  a  1987-89  PIP within  a budget including
Central Bank operations;
*    putting into effect measures  to  raise  public savings to
4.71 of GDP in 1987 (with a target of 5.51 of GDP by 1990);
*    securing enough external financing  to  close the 1987 bal-
ance of payments.
2.46     Export Promotion. The measures  already taken had ended specula-
tion about the Government's commitment to an open and free economy and
promised to induce more private investment in export-oriented and efficient
import substitution activities. Release of the second tranche was condi-
tional on:
*    maintenance of the CSF;
*    revlew of 1986 noncopper  export performance  and, if the
projected 61 real growth was not achieved, satisfactory
modification of the incentive structure.



- 18 -
2.47     Social Programs.  The Government  had started to reverse the nega-
tive effects of the SAP on investment and maintenance of health infrastruc-
ture, but a balanced program would be needed in the future. The condition-
alities for making the second tranche operational were satisfactory prog-
ress ons
*    assessing the efficiency of the SEP's (the PEM had become a
pure income subsidy mechanism); and
*    evaluating the main management issues of the health sector.
Both studies would have the auspices of the Bank's Sector
Public Management TAL.
2.48     Other Areas.   Other  conditions  for second tranche disbursements
were:
*    preparation of an  action  plan  and  a preliminary 1988-90
investment program for forestry;
*    evaluation of the impact of private pension funds on domes-
tic savings;
*    evaluation of the  efficiency  of public enterprise manage-
ment.
SAL III
2.49     SAL III's set conditions--both for  the presentation to the Bank's
Executive Board and for the second tranche disbursement--concerned four
areas that corresponded roughly to those of the previous SALs.
2.50     Macroeconomic  and Export  Promotion  Policies.    The government
intended to resist demands for increased consumption and pressures to
change  policies:    to maintain  Chile's  competitiveness  in traditional
exports and in import substituting activities, to offset some effects of
the 202 uniform tariff, and to promote nontraditional exports. The condi-
tions to activate the second tranche of SAL III weres
*    to strengthen  the  CSF,  which would  have received US$35
million in the second part of 1988, by narrowing the band
around  the  reference   price  from US$O.0OS-US$0.125  to
US$0.04-US$O.lO, and by abandoning the annual averaging
procedure;
*    to maintain the real  effective  exchange rate through fre-
quent and regular adjustments in the nominal exchange rate;
*    to review noncopper export performance  and,  if the real
growth projection for 1988 failed to reach 72, to modify
the incentive structure to further increase exports;
*    to prove the adequacy of the external financial program.



_ 19 -
2.51     The Government would also permit  indirect exporters access to the
benefits of capital goods purchases under decree law 1226 and change
finance decree 409 to grant exporters drawbacks, independently of who
imported the  inputs.    In  addition,  the  authorities would establish a
private, unsubsidized export credit insurance system (operated by special-
ized firms) and would give small and medium-size companies partial (up to
501) guarantees for investment credits and working capital loans (up to
US$150,000). The State Bank would operate a US$4.4 million fund for this
purpose. These measures were conditions for the Bank staff's Board presen-
tation of SAL III. Other Board presentation requirements were to maintain
the CSF and the real effective exchange rate and to modify the incentive
framework if real noncopper exports grew less than 61 in 1987.
2.52     Public Finances and Investments.  The second tranche conditionali-
ties in this area weres
*    to restrain public  expenditures  so  as  to limit the 1988
nonfinancial public sector deficit to 1.6Z of GDP in 1988;
*    to increase public savings to 52  of GDP in 1988 (from 4.8Z
in 1987 and 4.4Z in 1986);
*    to carry out  satisfactorily  the  1987  and 1988 PIPs (the
latter was still under some discussion);
*    to prepare a 1988-90 PIP of 7.51 of GDP each year.
2.53     Financial Rehabilitation.  To  complete  its rehabilitation of the
banking system, the Government was augmenting its monitoring of the collec-
tion of debts sold to the Central Bank and was trying to improve the effi-
ciency of banks in accelerating repurchases of bad portfolios sold to the
Central Bank. The Government would also make clearer to depositors the low
limits of State guarantees.   These were conditions for board presentation.
Other requirements were to amend decree law 2500 to permit enhanced partic-
ipation of pension funds In equity markets and to strengthen the regulation
of insurance companies and mutual funds (with prudential guidelines for
portfolio diversification and limits to related-party investments). The
attention was turning to medium-time financial issues affecting savings and
investment. The second tranche conditionalities weres
*    to encourage capital (particularly equity) markets through:
-    revisions in the tax  treatment of income from securi-
ties--more symmetrical treatment of capital gains and
losses in quoted stock transactions, deductions of
purchases of mutual funds subscribing primary issues,
and so on, and
-    a reduction by a half  in  the  2.42 stamp tax rate on
credit and bond issues;



-20-
*    to improve the system of risk classification of securities,
in order to stimulate the capital markets, by increasing
the number of companies vhose paper the private pension
funds would be allowed to purchase;
*    to carry out studies  on  efficiency and competition in the
finamcial system, broadening the range of its instruments
and encouraging open corporations.
The Government was also toughening prudential regulations in securities
markets  and  allowing   greater  market  participation  by  institutional
investors.
2.54     Social Sectors.   The  social  security  and health programs were
essential both for the sustainability of the SAP and for the poor. In the
first instance, the administration intended to protect the security of the
pension funds, to increase the efficiency and long-term coverage of the
system, and to enhance the  program's  acceptance.   In the second, it pur-
ported to increase current nonpersonnel outlays by 152 in 1988 and to raise
productivity. The conditionalities to release the second tranche weres
*    to change administrative  procedures  in  order to increase
the collection of social security contributions, unifying
and simplifying procedures before end-1988;
*    to carry out a study on:
?    the viability of  augmenting  affiliation (it was then
251) of the self-employed (202 of the work force) to
the social security system vis-a-vis support from the
fiscal budget, and
ways to reduce the  operating costs of private pension
funds, and to diminish or elimnate fees, which
resulted in low-income contributors receiving 15-171
less in payouts than high-income contributors;
*    to increase  public  understanding  of the social security
reforms by facilitating entry (simpler commission struc-
tures) and the choice of insurance companies; other actions
foreseen by the authoriti4s were to reduce the capital
requirements for establishing an APP from US$330.000 to
US$83,000, to encourage funds for individual firms, and to
introduce voluntary savings accounts (to be managed by the
APPs) set up through automatic payroll deduction;
*    to strengthen preventive health care programs (for adults),
including coverage  of chronic  diseases (smoking-related,
cervical and breast cancer, cardiovascular diseases), and
to tax cigarette advertisement on top of the sales tax
(then 69X);



- 21 -
*    to improve  cost  contaiament  and  accountlng,  as well as
user's fee collection, by the decentralized public health
system and to streamline Its management;
*    to strengthen the  regulatory  framework for private health
insurance and to clarify the rules for transactions between
the public health system and private insurers;
*    to undertake  a  program  to  rehabilitate public hospitals
(252 of machinery and equipment was out of order) starting
January 1, 1989.
G. Appraisal of Imolementation
2.55     The short-term objective of  encouraging  the maximum expansion of
economic activity compatible with a gradual decline of external financial
resources was given a concrete form in the macroeconomic program of 1985-88
supported by the ITF's mF.    The long-term objective of consolidating the
conditions for sustained growth through the SAP was supported mainly by the
World Bank's SALs I (1985-86), II (1986-87), and III (1987-88). The
program attracted and received support from the international creditors
banks and official creditor agencies through negotiations in 1985, 1987 and
1988. As will be seen in this subsection, the implementation of the three
SALs was successful to a high degree.
Implementation of the SAP
2.56     The SAP, initiated in 1985, was successfully carried out.  Targets
were surpassed, and necessary readjustments were made to maintain the
direction of economic policy on a reasonable track. The main goals under
MFl surveillance were the rate of GDP growth, the inflation rate, the ratio
of the current account balance to GDP, the balance of payments, and the
ratio of the fiscal deficit to GDP.  The 1985 program was revised yearly,
with the first revision taking place in early 1986. Since then, new revi-
sions were made in each following year. Those modifications consisted in
introducing actual data to the performance criteria and projecting the
target for the following years by taking account of new developments.
2.57     GDP Growth. As far as GDP growth was concerned, higher rates than
expected were experienced with the exception of 1985, when GDP grew 2.41
(against the projection of 31).  In  the following years, actual growth of
output surpassed targets, and this meant an upward revision of the future
rates (Table 2.2).

the planned rate. On this matter the 1986 and 1987 revisions lowered the
targets (Table 2.3).    In  1987  the  rate  of  inflation slipped away and
reached 21.SZ; nearly 82 above the last revision.

2.59     Current Account Deficit.    The program Increasingly overestimated

following years. The 1986 revisions of the program were pessimistic, par-
ticularly for 1986 and 1987.    Later  adjustments  to reality proved to be
very close to the 1985 original forecasts.

2.60     Balance of Payments.    Performance  here  also  showed  a need to
readjust the targets (Table 2.5).    Initially  a US$27 million surplus was
expected in 1985, higher than the actual US$4 million. Revisions of the
program in 1986 and 1987 increased the values of the surplus: experience
showed a larger amount in 1986 and a similar negative figure a year later.
In 1988 the situation changed again and the overall balance reached US$838
million, reflecting an improvement in the outlook for exports, lower
interest rates, and better than expected results of debt conversion.

January 1986. This implied the activation of 0.1? of GDP from the contin-
