2.62     Macroeconomic policies. Macroeconomic policies under the SAP were
sounder than In 1983-84. The program was directed to reverse the decline
of private savings and anti-export biases.    The real rate of interest was
positive. For the years 1985, 1986 and 1987, the effective annual rates of
interest for short-ternm deposits were'31.6?, 18.92 and 25.12, respectively;
given Inflation rates of 26.4?, 17.42, and 21.52 in those years, annual
real interest rates were positive but very mall: 4.12, 1.32, and 32. The
monetary program was consistent with efforts to reduce inflation and to
meet the balance of payments goals and this was complemented by a policy of
wage restraint in the public sector. The government did not interfere with
wage negotiations with the private sector.
Implementation of SAL I
2.63     Chile demonstrated its commitment to structural adjustments before
the SALs (See Sections II and III of the PCR). In the early 1980s the fall
in prices of copper and other major export commodities caused enormous
losses in Chile's earnings. The economic crisis of 1982-83 prompted deval-
uations that increased the indebtedness of the productive sector and sig-
nificantly constrained its ability to pay the debt. The financial system
was near collapse, and the State was forced to intervene in January 1983,
taking over the administration of various financial institutions. The
Government had to assist banks and firms in distress by granting loans and
providing guarantees. Nevertheless, difficulties were also an outcome of



- 26 -
past economic policies. The permissive liberalization process of the mid-
1970s led to an undue reliance on foreign loans and increased external
indebtedness. The latter leveled off when it amounted to roughly the size
of GDP. In 1984 the authorities recognized the need for structural adjust-
ments and elaborated, jointly with the World Bank, a three-year PIP.
2.64     The Chilean adjustment  efforts  were  significant and measures in
exbcange rates, tariffs, and the elimination of low-return projects in the
PIP induced the Bank to impose few conditionalities on a loan that would be
spent on imports. The adjustment program for SAL I was prepared by the
Ministries of Economy and Finance and the Central Bank and was presented to
the Bank in November 1984.  By  September 20, 1985, negotiations were com-
pleted, and in November 1985 effectiveness was to take place. The first
tranche was disbursed in December 1985, and the second tranche was released
in June 1986. Conditionalities for disbursement were described above. In
many cases they referred to studies rather than policy actions, since Chile
had already started to adopt strong adjustment measures. One of the most
important of these studies was the scheme for rehabilitation of the banking
system.
2.65     Export Promotion.  The  Government's  SAP for 1985-87 attempted to
correct the in balance of payments disequilibrium while trying to minimize
the impact on economic growth. The authorities expected that their actions
would produce GDP growth of  2-4X  in  1985, increasing to 3-5Z in 1986-87.
The pace of growth quickened in 1986, to 5.42, led by fishing (102), agri-
culture (8.7X), transportation (8.1X), and industry (82). The main factors
of growth were increases in  domestic  investment  and external trade.  The
objectives of the three-year program carried out by the Government were
amply met in 1986. The plan was built under realistic assumptions. Copper
prices were assumd to have a moderate upward trend starting from US$0.65 a
pound. Real interest rates were slowly decaying, to a 32 annual level by
the end of the decade.
2.66     The issues in export promotion were to diversify exports, encour-
age the development of efficient import substitution, accelerate mineral
exports, and improve Government support for nontraditional exports. The
measures taken by the Government included a strong initial devaluation in
1985 and, later, an exchange rate gradually adjusted in response to the
difference between domestic and  international  inflation.   Looking at the
past, there was a real devaluation of 702 between June 1982 and March 1985.
It continued: it was 462 in 1985-86. The import tariff reduction from 352
to 202 was started on February 1985 and completed by June 1985, one year
ahead of schedule. External trade was being freed by removal of quantita-
tive restrictions and by a low and even tariff. The administration's pro-
gram expected real growth in noncopper exports to be 62 a year so as to
realize a current account deficit of US$l billion by 1987. The expansion
that actually took place doubled the target, and the measures directed to
correct the balance of payments problems permitted attainment a current
account deficit of US$1.2 billion in 1987 (down from US$2.1 billion in
1984). Tariff reductions of 52 and 102 were introduced from March to July
1985. The price-band policy designed to level off changes in cereals and



- 27 -
oilseeds prices. which had been applied since 1983, was adjusted to corre-
spond with the tariff reductions in Harch 1986 for the 1986-87 agricultural
year. An assessment of the state sugar monopoly's competitiveness was
completed by 1986sl, and based on its results a price support was estab-
lished for sugar.
2.67     Exports also were promoted in July 1985 by a 1OX rebate on the net
earnings of minor exports and by adapting the rebate system to dampen the
impact of custom duties on exporters who imported inputs. The mechanism of
deferred payments of customs duties on the value of capital goods imported
for the production of exportable goods was also improved. The amount of
debt that the contracting exporter had with customs was now reduced by the
percentage that his exports represented in his total sales.
2.68     Other measures  relating  to  export  promotion affected taxation.
There was a revision of Regulations and procedures were revised to reduce
delays in refunding the value-added tax (VAT) to exporters. In addition,
it became possible to recover the VAT within six months for capital equip-
ment incorporated in fixed assets.   Seal  and stamp taxes on export credit
operations were eliminated.
2.69     New legislation gave greater flexibility  to PROCHILE.  In the end
of 1985, the agency prepared a study of the goods that could be exported in
the short-term, and it assigned experts to some small- and medium-size
producers to assist their exports by improving quality control, marketing,
and access to market information.
2.70     Within the 1985-86 action program,  in March 1986 a public-private
committee was created to coordinate the major investments required in the
forestry sector.
2.71     Another measure to promote export performance was the reduction of
banks' reserve requirements for  credits  to  exporters.   The 52 margin on
capital and reserves that banks were allowed to lend to exporters was
raised to  1OX.    The  margin  of  guarantees  for  credit  operations was
increased from 252 to 30Z, allowing exporters to get more financial
resources. The Government also extended the period of repatriation of
receipts from exports that must be sent to free-port warehouses abroad in
order to be marketed properly.
2.72     The export promotion  policy was  constantly  enhanced with other
devices such as realistic exchange rates and low tariff levels. An excep-
tion was the system of surcharges established to protect domestic industry
from unfair competition from abroad, which followed the definitions of the
General Agreement on Tariffs and Trade (GATT).
2.73     There were several measures the  authorities had to take to ensure
the success and sustainability of the program, since one of the risks that
both the Chilean authorities and World Bank staff acknowledged was the
possible discontinuation of the program as a consequence of external
shocks. These measures were:



- 28 -
*    to maintain  an  exchange  rate  compatible with normineral
exports;
*    to develop the public-private forestry program;
*    to  expand  CODELCO's   capacity  consistently  with  other
investment prioritiest
*    to readjust cereal and sugar  support prices in accord with
the general tariff level and to define the sugar policy.
2.74     The Bank monitored the  ratification  of  the CSF in January 1986.
The public-private forestry program was approved in 1986. Bank monitoring
was also directed to follow noncopper export performance and to review the
price bands for support prices of sugar and cereals.
2.75     The CSF was to operate  on  an  experimental base during its first
year. The copper reference price was agreed in December 1985, and the fund
was put into effect the  next month.    The  CSF mechanism applied only to
CODELCO's exports, which amounted to 792 of total copper exports in 1985.
CSF funds were deposited in the Central Bank and incorporated in its inter-
national reserves.
2.76     The CSF would have a quarterly balance determined as follows:
*    a reference path for the price of copper was set by averag-
ing the price of each of the four quarters; this path was
considered in the budget and in the balance of payments;
?    the variation in the balance  of the CSF equaled the physi-
cal volume exported by CODELCO times the average difference
during that period between the market price (f.o.b.)
received by CODELCO and the reference price. The differ-
ence was adjusted  by  different weightst    zero within
tUS$0.50 per pound from the reference price, and 502 within
tUS$0.12 and tUS$0.05  per pound.   Outside the preceding
band, a factor of 1OOZ was applied;
*    The balance of the CSF in a year had to correspond with the
yearly average of prices and quantities;
*    The quarterly balance  of  the  CSF was reviewed every six
months in the context of the SAL program in order to avoid
destabilizing effects caused by the automatic operation of
the formula in a context of fluctuating noncopper prices,
import prices, and interest rates.
2.77     Public Finance.  The main issue  in the area of public finance was
the need to increase public savings. One of the effects of falling primary
export prices was the massive loss of fiscal revenues as a result of the



-29_
reduction in the value of exports.    To attain sustainability of the poli-
cies underlying the SAP, emphasis therefore had to be placed on strengthen-
ing the State's finances. This had to be accompanied by greater consisten-
cy between public sector management and investment. The increase of fiscal
savings had to be achieved with a parallel increase in private savings.
2.78     Public expenditures in 1985 had  to  be adjusted to reflect relief
and reconstruction requirements of the March earthquake and the need for
fiscal austerity. The fiscal program for the year included contingency
investments of 0.5Z of GDP to be activated if net private spending was
lower then foreseen or if emergency financing could be obtained to repair
the earthquake damage. A PIP was elaborated for 1986 and was ratified by
April 1986. Some projects in transportation infrastructure were reduced or
eliminated from the 1985-87 PIP.    Construction  of Line 3 of the Santiago
Metro was suspended indefinitely.    The  annual investment on the southern
highway was not increased in real terms. Other projects, such as new irri-
gation works, were also postponed. These cutbacks were made to give prior-
ity to investments of high return in agriculture and mining.
2.79     In the area  of public  administration  the programs of technical
assistance financed by the Bank were to produce studies for the design and
implementation of organization and information systems to improve the con-
trol and management  of public  enterprises.    In  EMPORCHI (Chilean Port
Enterprise), EMPREMAR (Chilean Maritime Enterprise), LAN (Chilean Airlines)
and the Rail Authority, for example, the technical assistance program
strengthened planning and management capabilities, study of which began in
April 1986.
2.80     In 1985 a series  of  studies  on  the  labor market was begun to
review the efficiency  of  the  Government's  EEPs.    The authorities took
actions aimed to promote the use of labor in production and to increase the
efficiency of public employment programs.
2.81     The overall nonfinancial  public  sector  deficit was reduced from
4.4: of GDP in 1984 to 2.2Z of GDP in 1986. Expanded exports and rising
copper prices meant larger revenues.   The increase of public savings (4.4Z
of GDP in 1986, up from 0.61 in 1984) was the consequence of spending
restraint.    Wages,  salaries,  and  transfer payments  for unemployment
declined in real terms.    So  the  decrease  in  the public sector deficit
reflected the net effect of current expenditure restraint and expanded
public investment. In 1986 the latter reached 7.72 of GDP, over 1 point
higher than in 1984. The increment of public savings and investment was
accompanied by increases in private savings and investment. Total invest-
ment rose from 1.6Z of GDP in 1984 to 14.62 in 1986.
2.82     Financial Rehabilitation Policy.   The government intended to con-
clude normalization of the financial sector in 1986. The measures taken
were to end Central Bank subsidies to borrowers.   It would not set up new
lines of concessional credit to refinance loans already outstanding. This
activity was left to the private financial institutions. The process of
normalization of the bank system was very expensive, and the Treasury and



- 30 -
international banks would have to  become  suppliers  of finance In case of
need. In 1985 the authorities launched a program for recapitalizing the
Banco de Chile and the Banco de Santiago. Before the start of the SAP, the
Central Bank was engaged in rescuing banks and firms seriously affected by
the crisis. This action implied subsidies and debt rescheduling. The
transfers of funds to the private sector associated with the rehabilitation
and recapitalization Was estimated to be about US$6 billion in 1983 and
1984. Part of these transfers were recovered from the private banks and
firms through compulsory repurchases of their portfolios from the Central
Bank.
2.83     In 1985 the financial system  began  to make profits.  In June of
that year the government announced the gradual phasing out, by the end of
1986. of the preferential exchange rate program for debtors owing more than
US$50,000, of dollar swaps, and of dollar deposit subsidies by the begin-
ning of 1987.
2.84     Finally the  authorities  complemented  the  policies to diversify
exports, promote growth, and rehabilitate firms and banks near collapse
with a restrained wage policy.  Wage indexation was ended.  In 1985 employ-
ment increased by 6.6?, the rate of open unemployment was 8.8Z, and the
rate of unemployment prevented by the EEPs was 5.1Z.
Implementation of SAL II
2.85     It was recognized from the  beginning  that most of the structural
problems could not be resolved in one year, and then it was realized that a
longer period for adjustment--say, three to five years--was necessary. In
February 1986 a Bank mission visited Chile to review progress on SAL I and
was asked to consider a second SAL. Disbursement of SAL 1I would be front-
loaded, with the first tranche (US$150 million) released upon effectiveness
November 1986 and the second one (US$100 million) after a performance
review to be held six months later (as it happened, in June 1987). The
project was first presented to the Bank's Board in February 1986. Negotia-
tions were completed by mid-August 1986, and the date for effectiveness was
December 1986.    SAL  II's  conditionalities  were *as  described earlier.
Despite SAL I, the adjustment process was not yet solid. Chile could pay
only 50? of the interest on its foreign debt by itself. Insufficiency of
savings and investment persisted, which made it doubtful that steady growth
of 52 per year would be maintained. The conditions emphasized the need for
consolidating the results of SAL I through the continuation of the policies
agreed in the first loan, with more concern for the social sectors. The
government complied with almost all requirements for the release of the
US$100 million of the second  tranche.   The good economic performance per-
mitted not only the disbursement of the loan but also the consideration of
a third SAL.
2.86     The goals of  economic  policy  stated  by  the government were as
follows. Once adjustment in the external accounts was achieved, a reacti-
vation of the economy was expected--but under a reduction in foreign bor-
rowing and hand in hand with efforts to bring down inflation. The govern-
ment expected a 31 GDP growth rate and an inflation rate of 202 a year.



- 31 -
Progress in the normalization, recapitalization and reprivatization of
firms and banks rescued by the Central Bank had a positive effect on the
reactivation. The improvement in savings and Investments attracted foreign
capital and this made feasible the resumption and completion of important
projects.   Almost all the  objectives  had been achieved within the frame-
work of SAL I, but inflation was higher than programmed and in 1987 prices
grew 21.52 instead of  the  expected  152.   The Government raised interest
rates between March and October 1987, and the rate of inflation slowed down
in the following months.
2.87     Export Promotion.  The fulfillment  of the BANK's export promotion
conditionalities was centered on the adjustment of exchange rates for non-
mineral exports. Under SAL II no large devaluations took place; the ex-
change rate moved with mini-devaluations.   The purpose was to maintain the
exchange rate stable in real terms, but in fact there was a depreciation of
the peso. In January 1987 the execution of a plan for forestry develop-
ment, including an investment program for 1988-90, was started. The study
on PROCHILE's efficiency continued under a Bank's TAL. The CSF was renewed
for another year in January  1987.    Export incentives were sufficient to
exceed the 62 targeted increase in real noncopper exports, which grew at
more than double that rate.
2.88     Public Finances.  Measures to achieve public savings equivalent to
4.7Z of projected GDP were put into effect in March 1987. The satisfactory
execution of the 1986 PIP was verified, and during the second half of 1986
a PIP for 1987-89 was prepared. There was also substantial progress in the
studies on the efficiency of public firm management. The fiscal program
assumed that current expenditures would decline slightly in real terms.
The increase in public investment from 7.12 of GDP in 1985 to 7.62 of GDP
in 1986 was concentrated in infrastructure: works already begun because of
the March 1985 earthquake; subsidized housing; construction of public
morks; and the  investment  projects  of  CODELCO,  ENDESA (Santiago Energy
Company),  CHILECTRA  (Chilean   Electric   Company),  CAP  (Pacific  Steel
Company), and other productive  public  enterprises.   The 1986 budget pro-
jected a deficit amounting to 2.22 of GDP, which would be financed mainly
by external credit. Public savings were 31 of GDP.
2.89     Financial Rehabilitation Policy.    The  process of recapitalizing
banks was continuing, and a substantial advance was verified at the end of
1986.   The  preferential  exchange  rate  program  for  debtors  with over
US$50,000 in obligations was phased out by the end of this year. In April
1987 the subsidy implicit in dollar deposit and swap schemes for the re-
structured foreign debts of the financial system were eliminated. The
government had capitalized the five major intervened banks, required share-
holders of other weakened institutions to subscribe to new capital,
achieved the sale of bad portfolios to the Central Bank, and completed
provisions for loan losses.    The  costs  incurred  by the Central Bank in
recapitalization were determined as were the absorption conditions accord-
ing to the 1987-90 monetary and financial program. The normalization of
intervened banks and firms was completed in 1987; it constituted a major
element of SAL II.   The  intervened banks were satisfactorily capitalized,



- 32 -
with leverage ratios from 9? to 14? (after adjustments for double counting
on balance sheets). The rescued institutions were prepared to make opera-
tional profits and then repurchase their nonperforming portfolios from the
Central Bazk. The World Bank saw the state divestments as having high
costs, since the shares of the enterprises were sold at very low prices.
It therefore seemed convenient to slow down the process of reprivatization.
In any case, the recapitalization supported by SAL IT was completed.
2.90     The new banking law 18,576 of November 1986 was designed to reduce
risk and speculation. Bank managers were made legally responsible for the
operation of their new portfolios.   A  supervision mechanism was set up by
the SBIF to ensure against any direct and indirect related-party loans. In
September 1986 law 18,519 put into effect a new system for bank deposit
insurance that conditioned such protection on specific increases in equity.
The governmeat established tests for risk classification of paper issues
and the recording of five-year earnings. Privatized state enterprises were
excepted.
2.91     A problem in connection with  channeling  private savings was the
restrictions on investment Imposed on AlPs. Pension funds represented over
20? of domestic savings. To avoid risks, AFPs could invest their pension
funds only in the most  highly  qualified  instruments.   Only 5? could be
invested in equities on the stock  market.   (By 1991 this percentage would
be increased to 30S). The companies where these funds could be invested
could have no single shareholder controlling any more than 20? of the
stocks. In addition, APPs could place no more than 20? of their funds in
any single investment. The excessive care to avoid risks represented not
only a limitation on broadening the investment channels for pension funds,
but also a constant threat of expanded consumption from public expenditures
financed by bond issues bought by the APPs.
2.92     Social Sectors.  The government  decided to improve the efficiency
of public and private health care systems, but there were no additional
resources to invest in this sector.    The policy was to maintain the level
of public expenditures but to modify the composition of speading in favor
of capital and against current  expenditures.   Priority was given to main-
tain existing facilities and then to replacing outmoded equipment. A com-
pulsory payroll deduction for health care was increased from 6S to 7Z.
2.93     In aidition, minor  adjustments  were made  in educational sector
reform with respect to student loan administration and school municipaliza-
tion.
2.94     The social security system was  modified to improve its efficiency
and coverage. Voluntary savings accounts, to be financed through payroll
deductions and managed by the AFPs, were introduced. The Bank mission had
reservations on the scheme because it could lead to open loans to pension
plan members and could raise the APPs' operational costs. The mission
agrsed that, so long as the voluntary accounts did not receive tax prefer-
ences relative to the banking system, they might be an acceptable instru-
ment for inducing smallholder savings.    In another area, the labor market
studies about the relative efficiencies of the EEPs continued as scheduled.



- 33 -
Implementation of SAL III
2.95     The Bank's financial assistance to support Chile's ongoing SAP was
extended by approval of a third SAL. SAL II successfully met its macroeco-
nomic targets, but there were some areas that still required consolidation,
such as mobilization of domestic savings and investment and more emphasis
on the social sectors.   SAL  III was  another  US$250 million loan, to be
disbursed in two equal tranches.    The  program was first presented to the
Bank's Board in May 1987.   The  negotiations  were completed at the end of
October 1987, and the date for effectiveness was December 1987. The loan,
similar to SALs I and II, was intended to finance imports, thus continuing
relief for Chile's balance of payments.  Conditionalities for SAL III were
described above. The application of SAL III was accompanied by monitoring
of economic performance by both the Bank and the InF. The economy went on
expanding at about 6.72 in 1988; unemployment declined to 8.8Z from 9.6 in
the previous year, and real wages increased by 5.32. The inflation rate
slackened to 12.3Z, down from 21.51  in  1987.   There have been periods in
which the money supply  expanded  significantly.   The authorities and Bank
officials were concerned about the impact on the improved situation already
achieved through the  SAP.    The  Government  imposed restrictive monetary
policies whenever credit expansion could jeopardize the adjustment process.
These attempts were successful, and monetary policy remained tight.
2.96     Exnort Promotion.   Export  promotion  policies  were kept in line
with those of the previous SALs.   The main  features in SAL III were the
strengthening of the CSF in 1988 by narrowing the price bands that activate
the fund (from US$0.05 - US$0.125 per pound to US$0.04 - US$0.10 per pound,
around the reference price of US$0.75 per pound) and by substituting quar-
terly for annual averaging.   There  had  been increases in the reserves of
the CSF during 1988.   The  Chilean  authorities expected that CS? reserves
could reach US$400 million by September 1988 and Bank officials thought
those reserves could exceed US$500 million by the end of 1988, provided
that international copper prices did not decline sharply. Such levels of
reserves were higher than those expected when the SAL III agreement was
signed. Copper prices were supposed to be US$0.75 per pound when the cur-
rent estimate in 1988 was US$1  per  pound.   On these grounds, the Chilean
Government requested Bank authorization to use US$200 million from the CSF
for debt repurchases. This request was accepted. An agreement on restruc-
turing debt was scheduled to take place between Chile and commercial banks
whereby Chile could use international reserves for debt buybacks. This use
of CSF funds was also consistent with the objectives of SAL III: the re-
duction of Chile's debt bought at a discount would increase public savings.
At the same time, Chile had improved its creditworthiness and its position
to re-enter international financial markets.
2.97     The maintenance of  the  exchange  rate  policy was implemented by
daily devaluations of the peso, made by taking into account the difference
between domestic and external Inflation. This systematic currency depreci-
ation was corrected in 1988.



- 34 -
2.98     The policy of export promotion was maintained.  To offset the 202
uniform nominal tariff and to stimulate export production, the administra-
tion permitted (through a revision of decrees 224 and 409) the automatic
duty-free importation of inputs destined for such production. Another
measure was to replace the existing rebate system by expanding the duty
suspension provisions of the drawback regime. Noncopper exports were 34.11
higher in 1988 than in the year before. The rise in copper prices contrib-
uted to an overall export increase of 31.82 in 1987. Imports grew at 20.41
(capital goods imports increased 25.21 in the first semester of 1988, com-
pared with the same period in  1987).    In 1988 the trade balance improved
further. The surplus reached US$2.075 million, 69Z higher than in 1987.
Exports rose by 31.81, with mineral exports accounting for only 28.8S of
the increase.   Industrial exports  advanced  39.42 in the first six months
of 1988, compared with the same period in 1987.
2.99     The implementation of statutory measures  was designed to improve
the drawback system for small-scale exporters, to include indirect ex-
porters under the system of customs tariff refunding on imported inputs and
capital goods, and to establish the legal framework for an export credit
insurance scheme. Additional legislation was to set up a partial guarantee
fund for small- and medium-scale exporters.
2.100    In the first six months  of  1988, the balance of payments surplus
was US$224 million. The surplus covered the loss in international reserves
that occurred in 1987. In the first seven months of 1988, the trade sur-
plus and debt conversion contributed to a net reduction of external debt by
US$984 million.
2.101    Public Finances.  Fiscal policy  continued to stress larger fiscal
savings and a high rate of public investment. Continuous improvements in
its finances led the Government to lower the VAT from 201 to 161. The aim
of reducing the State's role in the economy has prevailed, and the adminis-
tration has continued with its program of reprivatization. The rate of
public saving was expected to grow from 4.81 of GDP in 1987 to 52 of GDP in
1988. This target was pursued through a policy of current expenditure
restraint and of repricing public enterprises' services. The targets were
surpassed, since public savings amounted to 5.41 of GDP in 1987 and were
7.71 of GDP a year  later.    Public  investment was expected to remain at
about 7.51 of GDP. The contingency investments included in previous fiscal
budgets were activated in the amount of 0.11 of GDP when emergency loans
were obtained from the Bank and the  IDB.   The choice of projects was made
after rigorous evaluations.   The  program of public expenditures respected
the target that limited the overall deficit of the nonfinancial public
sector to less than 1.62  of  GDP  in  1988.    As it happened, the deficit
shrank to 0.41 of GDP in 1987 and became a surplus of 2.41 of GDP in 1988.
2.102    The policies  designed  to  increase  private  sector  savings and
investment remained as before, creating a macroeconomic framework consis-
tent with stable rules of the game and relative prices and with prudent
management of aggregate demand.  The  tax  system was supposed to cause the
smallest possible disincentives for private saving and investment, the
medium- and long-term aspects of which were stressed.



_ 35 -
2.103    Financial Rehabilitation.    The measures  to  be  applied to the
financial system were designed to devote special attention to the institu-
tional aspects of the capital market.    Specifically, it was intended that
provisions contained in the recent banking law reforms, particularly, in
connection with the SBXD and the regulations affecting the services pro-
vided by financial intermediaries, would be fully applied in order to
induce competition in the capital markets. The continuity of the financial
system's rehabilitation was ensured by the maintenance of loan collections
and the repurchase of substandard portfolios from the Central Bank.
2.104    Certain tax initiatives were designed to accelerate development of
the capital markets. For instance, the stamp duty on loans from 2.42 to
1.22 was reduced. Initiatives were taken to revise the income tax law in
order to achieve a more symmetrical treatment of capital gains and losses.
Margins for tax exceptions on income derived from capital gains were wid-
ened. Tax exemptions were introduced to include incomes derived from secu-
rities issued by open corporations. Other initiatives implied revisions in
the tax treatment of investments in mutual funds.
2.105    Measures designed to  improve  the  regulations governing institu-
tional investors were considered.   These studies included plans to promote
private systems for rating the risks associated with publicly offered secu-
rities and shares.   These  mechanisms  would  operate in parallel with the
existing AFP regulations concerning investments by insurance companies and
mutual funds. In the case of insurance, rules about reserve accounting
were to be revised. The application of new rules relating to AFPs' invest-
ments in shares, from recent changes in decree law 3,500, will also be
analyzed.
2.106    The measures taken in accord with  the SAP had a positive impact.
The financial system first made an overall profit in 1985. Its rentability
has continued to improve since then.   In the first five months of 1988 the
annualized rate of return on equity reached 32.8Z compared with yields of
19.4X and 13.52 In 1987 and 1986, respectively.
2.107    Social Policies.   Through  its  conditionalities,  SAL III placed
more explicit emphasis on the social aspects of adjustment. In 1987 social
outlays remained practically unchanged, with only marginal increases in
health, housing and regional development. For the SAP, health expenditures
and the publicly funded portion of the pension system were important: they
represented 302 of noninterest current Government expenditures in 1987.
The social security system was  modified.   The administration intended to
increase real nonpersonnel outlays for health, by 152 in 1988, to extend
preventive care and to invest in the rehabilitation of equipment over two
years.
2.108    For maternal and child health,  135 primary health care facilities
were transferred to municipalities in order to improve the quantity and
quality of services. For chronic diseases and adult health, the Government
made efforts to standardize norms for diagnosis and treatment by organizing
workshops and studies, some for training purposes. Infant mortality de-
clined from 65.82 of live births in 1973 to'19.4Z in 1986. Improvements in



- 36 -
preventive medicine and incroased provision of safe water and sewerage
services helped to raise the life expectancy at birth form 85.6 years in
1975-80 to 71 years in 1986.
2.109    Chile's housing program provided 120,000 homes to low-income fami-
lies during  1984-86.   According  to plans  submitted  for Bank support,
500,000 houses will be made available to people below the urban poverty
threshold in the next three years.
2.110    Government SEPs engaged 500,000 persons by 1983.  In the following
years economic recovery and low wages increased employment significantly.
In the period from October 1987 to January 1988, the official unemployment
rate averaged 7.72 of the labor force.   It was 9.6Z in the last quarter of
1986. The EEPs reduced their client rolls by over 100,000 persons in 1987,
leaving fever than 90,000 in  those  programs.    It vas a significant fall
from the 1983 peak.

trading partners, a 101 increase in copper prices by 1990, and a decline of

1984 to 2.52 of GDP in 1990, and the resource balance would change from a
negative US$204 million in 1984 to US$1.1 billion in 1990. The 1985-86

a 4.6S annual growth in 1984-90; mining, with 32 a year; industry, at 42
annually (including a low in 1985). Progress would be faster in the 1990s.
2.113    To attain those  objectives.  the  investment ratio would increase
from 14Z in 1985 to 202 In 1990. Although terms of trade improvements were
expected to bring income relief of 22 of 1990 GDP, a dramatic increase in
national savings from 7Z to 182 of GDP and an even faster rise in domestic
savings would be required (public savings were to go from 4.52 of GDP in
1986 to 6.52 in 1990; household savings were to provide a similar amount by
1990). Interest payments abroad would drop from 102 to 62 of GDP by 1990,
and foreign savings would be only 2.5Z of GDP that year.
2.114.  Unemployment would be slowly  reduced  despite cuts in social pro-
grams and EEPs, and assets' property would be more widely distributed.
SAL It
2.115    Many of the assumptions  underlying  the  expected impact of SAL I
were kept to assess that of SAL II, but in the second SAL more emphasis was
placed on the need for moderate increases in consumption and unemployment
to make the SAP sustainable and on a reduction of the debt burden to make
it viable (Table 2.8).

SAL III
2.118    Although the appraisal of SAL III's impact retained the assumption
of moderate growth in the OECD countries, it foresaw an increase of nominal
international interest rates of 1.2? by 1989 (and stabilization thereafter)
and copper price increases of 3.3? yearly (after an upward bubble). On
these grounds, GDP was to grow at 4.9Z yearly through 1992, consumption at
32. The faster increase in national savings would fund debt service and
investment. Growth would be fueled by exports. Noncopper exports would
rise 62 annually until 1992, but copper exports would keep their share (due
to expanded capacity). This export expansion would lower the interest on
the debtlexports ratio to 18? by  1992.    It would also support imports at
the level of 29? of GDP until 1990 (with increases later, due to exhaustion
of domestic oil supplies).   But  slightly  improved terms of trade and the
lower debt burden would allow a fall of the current account deficit to 2.8?
of GDP by 1992 (from 6.5? in 1986; Table 2.10).

2.121    The SAP would pay  large  social  dividends  in increased wages,
employment, and soclal expenditures by the Government. A reduction of 8X
ln infant and 20S in national mortality was expected over five years; a
drop in school-age malnutrition of 121 to 8S was foreseen by 1990.
A#praisal of the Impact
2.122    The aims of the Chilean SAP  since 1985 were to produce the accel-
eration of exports and expand domestic savings in order to permit a recov-
ery of economic growth and employment while the country continued to
service its external debt. By improving the performance of the balance of
payments Chile would strengthen its creditworthiness and attract a continu-
ous flow of foreign capital. The expansLon of exports was supposed to take
place by applying sound incentives to noncopper exports, thus diversifying
exports in order to reduce the strong dependence Chile traditionally had on
copper and nitrates.   Another  important  objective was to increase public
savings and achieve more efficiency in investment programs. A third goal
was to improve the condition of banking and corporate finances by means of
recapitalization and reprivatization schemes.   A  fourth objective was to
improve the  pension  program,  health  care  system,  and  education.  The
approach in the social area has been to direct public spending to satisfy
needs of the sectors in which poverty is extreme.
2.123    To analyze the impact of institutional changes and other modifica-
tions produced by implementation of the SAP is very difflcult. Benefits
spread throughout the economy, accruing to diverse economic agents. More-
over, to evaluate certain effects in isolation, sophistLcated models should
be constructed to evaluate the empirical evidence (see, for example, the
Technical Appendix). Instead, a vlew of economic performance--in particu-
lar, of the specific areas in which the SAP applied--may provide a useful
assessment of fulfillment of objectives.
2.124    In short, the SAP produced positive  results.  In the years of its
application, starting from an extremely difficult situation, there was a
significant recovery in economic growth and in employment caused by an
important expansion of exports and an increase in domestlc savings. This
performance was compatible with the country's servicing its external debt
in a consistent way.
2.125    The macroeconomic  approach  used  implied  a  conservative fiscal
policy designed to improve public finances and consolidate the balance of
payments, even with the negative external shock of deterioration in the
terms of trade that  started  in  1985.    The debt management strategy was
complemented by one of the first (and most) innovative programs in the
regions to induce conversion of discounted foreign debt into equity and
domestic liabilities. Between mid-1985 and mid-1988 the amount of con-
verted debt was about US$4.6 billion.    The government has also managed to
recapitalize and reprivatize the corporate and financial sectors through
strong monitoring and pressure to make institutions repurchase their debts
in order to complete the program as soon as possible and to maintain withxin
limits the strong lossei from changes in ownership. The rate of inflation
was maintained roughly constant at annual leveis close to 202. The follow-
ing subsections give a more detailed account of economic performance.



- 41 -
Economic Growth
2.126    The macroeconmic Impact of the  SAP wae  very favorable.  At the
beginning, economic policy was designed mainly to deal with stability and
balance of payments disequilibrium in a short-term contest. Most of the
actions directed to that end suffered from frequent setbacks and from the
different views of changing economic authorities. The burden of the finan-
cial crisis aggravated the situation. In 1983 the Government was overopti-
mistic about economic recovery while servicing the external debt, and its
concern about recession produced the relaxation of adjustment measures in
1984 (Table 2.12).

2.127    In 1982-84 there were  wide  deviations  between the Government's
ex.*ected values for target variables and the actual figures. The 1982 fall
in GDP was underestimated; the prospects a year later ware 4Z growth, but
GDP continued to decline (although at a reduced rate of 0.72). In 1984
actual growth exceeded  the  programed  rate.    Results for the remaining



- 42 -
variables results were mixed.   In  1985 the situation changed with respect
to the design and implementation of economlc policy. The stabilization
effort was supplemented by a SAP with financial backing. Soon e"ogenous
factors moved favorably for Chile's economy.   An upward trend in the terms
of trade began in  1986,  mainly  influenced by export prices, particularly
for. copper, which in 1988 practically doubled its 1985-86 levels. Import
prices also went up, but  at  a  slower  pace.   The trade volume increased
significantly.  With the exception of  1986, import volume grew faster than
export volume. The measures adopted to free markets had positive effects
in expanding trade and in stabilizing Chile's balance of payments, which
also benefited from a decline in the international interest rates.
2.128    The administration elaborated its program  in 1985 by relying on a
set of assumptions about the future evolution of foreign trade prices and
quantities. They underestimated export prices and overestimated import
prices (Table 2.13).    The  authorities  also  overestimated the levels of
international  rates  of   interest   (Table   2.14).    These  assumptions
contributed to the design of policies stressing graduality and prudence.
In this conservative context, the results looked favorable.

2.131    The public saving ratio was  another  target variable that showed
the viability of the Government's economic policies carried out after 1985.
The current expenditure restraint, the more efficient allocation of invest-
ible resources, and the rehabilitation and reprivatization of state enter-
prises allowed considerable change in the fiscal situation. By 1988 the
public sector balance became positive and slightly exceeded the program
goals of 2.5S  of  GDP.    During  1985-88  there  were minor readjustments
required by activation of part of the contingency investment plans.
2.132    Thus, economic performance was  excellent.    But  how much of the
success can be attributed to the macroeconomic framework of stabilization
and trade liberalization, institutional changes promoting export diversifi-
cation, measures to rehabilitate corporate and financial institutions, and
other factors? It is difficult to separate the effects without recourse to
general equilibrium models, but a clear picture emerges from the figures.
In 1985-86 the stabilization policies and a consistent and sound SAP,
backed by an important financial package from abroad, played the leading

the financial crisis. While policies were kept on focus, from 1986 on much
of the economic expansion was due to positive, unforeseen external shocks,
through prices and interest rates (Table 2.17).

2.133    The external shock effect  was  actually  nil  in 1985t  the gains
from international interest rate reductions were offset by losses from the
terms of trade. In 1986 there was a net benefit produced by improvements
in the terms of trade and interest cuts, amounting to more than US$170
million. In 1987 both factors contributed to generate a gain of over
US$700 million. A year later, the gains increased by nearly 30?, to more
than US$900 million.   The most  important  factor was improvement in the
rerms of trade. By 1988 the cumulative effects of better terms of trade
and lower world interest rates had added up to 10.41 of GDP in four years.
More than 602 of the gains were attributable to the improved terms of
trade. Part of those gains were expected by the Government when it de-
signed the SAP. Actual  figures  surpassed  planned magnitudes.  The lower
part of Table 2.17 shows that the direct impact of positive external shocks
accounted for 40.7?  of  GDP  growth  in  1985-88s    better terms of trade
explain 28.11; and lower interest rates 40.72. It also points out that the
unexpected part of those shocks  (they were partly expected) explains 97.62
of the excess of effective over programmed GDP growth (terms of trade,
77.52; interest rates, 21.32).
Z.134    After the critical years  1982  and  1983, when GDP declined 14.2?
and 0.7? with respect to  previous  years,  the new trade and exchange rate
policies and financial measures put forward by the Government restored a
positive rate of growth in an atmosphere of increased competitiveness.  The
experience of the economic crisis  induced  the authorities to state that a
longer-term program was a  necessary precondition for attaining sustainable
economic growth. Early in 1984, a  three-year PIP was produced, and a year
later the SAP was started.
2.135    A short-lived GDP expansion in  1984 proved unsustainable.  A more
thorough and decisive attempt at stabilization with adjustment was launched
in early 1985. The expansion of economic activity slowed its pace to an
annual 2.4?, as a consequence of reductions in consumption and gross
domestic investment (Table  2.18).    The  following  years showed rates of
growth over 5X, higher but close  to  those expected by the three SALs (4S;
4.4?, and 4.9Z).

2.136    Recovery from the 1982-83 crisis was taking place with a signifi-
cant role played by mining, although this sector would gradually lose its
pre-eminence. An important comeback was also happening in agriculture.
manufacturing, and construction.   If  one  compares the perfor-nance of the
Chilean economy with aggregate perforrance of developing countries in simi-
lar periods, it imediately becomes apparent that the economic growth
attained in Chile can be attributed to the country's efforts to implement
structural adjustments within a  consistent framework of macroeconomic pol-
icy. During 1980-87 the economic growth of developing countries averaged
2.32 a year, far lower than the 5.62 the group achieved during 1970-79. In
Chile, GDP expanded at the rate of 22 annually in the 19709 and at 2.62 a
year in the 1980s.   But  the  evolution  of  Chilean GDP since 1975 can be
separated into an initial expansion. (1975-81), at 4.12 annual growth; a
crisis period (1982-85), with -1.92 yearly decline; and a second recovery,
at 5.12 increase per year--all while Chile was carrying a heavy foreign
debt burden.
2.137    Sr. figures in Table  2.18  show  that  the SAP, launched in 1985,
produced drsmatic effects on both the demand and supply sides. On the
demand side, exports in real terms acceierated to a 72 annual growth, from
4.72 in 1982-85. Consumption reverted from -3.52 annual change during the
crisis to a quite moderate 2.22 rise after 1985. The turnaround was most
pronounced in domestic investment, which since 1985 moved from a -17.42
yearly fall to a sustained 102 growth rate. On the supply side, GDP, which
declined 1.52 yearly in 1982-85, recovered to a growth rate of over 52,
with moderate acceleration. The drastic drop in imports (in real terms)
durlhg the crisis years was followed by 72 yearly increases, roughly in
line with exports. Both expanded faster than GDP, reflecting the opening
of the economy. The dramatic turnaround of domestic investment was over-
shadowed by an even more spectacular change in the behavior of both na-
tional and domestic savings, which since 1985 grew 17.42 and 35.52 yearly,
respectively.
The Forei_n Sector
2.138    In the first  half  of  1985,  the  Government reduced its uniform
import tariff from 352 to 202, as a consequence of bad price prospects for
primary exports and the increasing need to expand noncopper exports. In
addition, the exchange rate was devalued 251, bringing the peso to its
lowest value with respect to the dollar in ten years. The devaluation was
destined to provide incentives to exporters and was maintained with fre-
quent periodic adjustments  to  the  exchange  rate.    In fact, additional
devaluations were observed in 1985-87 (Table 2.19). The reduction in the
import tariff was done in steps:    first  it was lowered from 352 to 302;
four months later it attained the level of 202. In 1988 the uniform import
tariff was further reduced to  152.    The authorities considered that free
trade and a low uniform import tariff would be sufficient incentives to
expand noncopper exports. They also imposed antidumping surcharges on milk
and sugar imports, to avoid unfair trade practices.

2.140    Export incentives were also  strengthened  by  the creation of the
CSF, designed to avoid future overvaluations of the currency because of
international copper prices.    The  CSF  ensured  that revenues of CODELCO
derived from higher prices would be sterilized in a reserve fund. Estab-
lished in 1985, by September 1988 these reserves had reached US$300 million
after withdrawal of US$200 million for foreign debt buybacks (Table 2.20).
2.141    The increase of  noncopper  exports  was  accompanied by efficient
import substitution. Private investment was channeled into activities that
replaced imports without creating antiexport  bias.   The share of consumer
and intermediate goods in imports declined. In contrast, import of capital
goods continued at a faster pace. This p1lies that future growth will not
be hampered by capacity obstacles.
2.142    To strengthen confidence in the private sector and attract foreign
capital, legislative changes were made to promote exports from small and
medium-size producers and to facilitate joint ventures in the energy sector
involving private firms and the SNAP.   Specific measures were taken by the
authorities to offset some effects of the uniform tariff through improved
drawback facilities. They revised decree law 1226 to allow for a 15? fis-
cal credit for domestic purchases of capital goods on equal conditions with
foreign suppliers, since these goods can be imported duty free when used in
export production.   Indirect  exporters  have  access  to the benefits for
capital goods purchases  under  decree  law  1226.   Exporters were granted
rebates for an amount equal to tariffs paid on imported inputs used for
export production, regardless of who actually imported the product. As far
as these measures designed to construct a consistent system of incentives
without interfering with the price signals go on, and provided no external
shocks occur in the following years, non traditional exports should be able
to grow faster than 10X a year in real terms. In due time this will con-
solidate the diversification of exports (Table 2.21).

2.143    The current account deficit fell by two-thirds between 1984 and 1987,
from US$2 billion to US$1.1  billion.    In 1986 Chile's reserves increased by
US$227 million. The current account deficit was 10.7? of GDP in 1984, was
reduced to 6.52 in 1986, and vent still lower to 52 in 1987. This strong
trade performance showed that  the  Government  succeeded in Implementing this
aspect of the SAP. The objectives of accelerating exports through diversifi-
cation were fulfilled, but great emphasis should be put in the future regard-
ing the sustainability of the export growth policy.
2.144    The successive negotiations of  the  external  debt helped to release
pressures on the balance  of  payments.    This  impact was complemented by an
increase in primary export values as a consequence of rising copper prices
and, particularly,  the  decline  in  international  interest  rates.   During
1983-85 the administration was forced to guarantee previously unguaranteed
external  obligations  of  private  banks   in  order  to  get  a  significant
rescheduling of payments due and US$3.2 billion in new medium-term loans.
Thus the share of public debt in total foreign debt was 39Z in 1982, 712 in
1985, and 762 in 1987.
2.145    By guaranteeing external private debt, the government linked its fate
to that of private borrowers.    The  state  guarantee on most of the external
debt eliminated the possibility of write-downs in individual cases and set a
new constraint on public finance, affecting the prospects of economic growth
because of reduced public investments.    Investment suffered as a consequence
of the shrinkage in national savings and of limited access to external sav-
ings. Therefore, the economic authorities attempted to defer the burden of
some private external interest payments by converting certain foreign bank
loans into equity (Table 2.22).

2.146    Chile's debt negotiations was  allowed  the conversion of external
into domestic obligations--by residents and nonresidents--and the purchase
of external debt by foreign investors for conversion into direct investment
in Chile.   This  mechanism  permitted  taking  advantage of the difference
between the market value of the external obligations in secondary markets
and their face value. Chile's debt was priced at 652-702 of face value in
those markets. By 1986 major foreign investors were using this conversion
scheme, and the arrangement was very useful in moderating the increase of
external debt.
2.147    Management of external debt  proved  difficult, but the efforts of
the authorities to increase creditworthiness allowed them to introduce
mechanisms that could moderate the growth of debt over through time. Their
prudence shaped performance in the capital account of the balance of
payments. The annual growth rate of external debt was 4.62 in 1982-85 and*
-0.8S in 1985-88. The external debtlGDP ratio had been going up steadily
since 1980. In 1985 it reached a maximum of 1192 and later reversed its
trend. The ratio was 832 in 1988. Analogous performance was achieved by
interest on the debtlexport  ratio:    its  peak was  482  in 1984, and it
declined to 23Z in 1988.
2.148    SAL I, the IMP's  ElF,  and  the  partial World Bank guarantee of
US$300 million in B-loan cofinancing eased conditions for a favorable
agreement with commercial banks on the 1985-86 package. The Bank guaran-
teed US$150 million of the restructuring of US$5.7 billion in maturities
due in 1985-87; the banks then provided fresh money in the amount of US$1.1
billion for 1985-86.   Some  creditor  countries rescheduled another US$145
million, representing 652 of principal payments due from July 1, 1985 to
December 31, 1986.
2.149    The authorities  agreed with  the  banks" advisory  committee in
February 1987 on the financial program for 1987-88, effective in June 1987.
For 1988, maturities amounting to US$12.4 billion were rescheduled, leaving
a financial gap of  US$650 million  for  1987-88.   Interest payments were
consolidated by this agreement into one annual payment, which deferred the
July 1988 commitment of US$450 million to January 1989. Current spreads on
past fresh money loans were reduced to 1-1182 over the London Interbank
Offered Rate (LIBOR); on those credits based on restructured maturities
(1988-91), spreads were trimmed to 12.   In April 1987 creditor governments
rescheduled 852 of maturities falling due between July 1987 and December
1988.
2.150    In April 1988 the  Government  agreed with its commercial creditor
banks' advisory coumittee to reduce the spread of their outstanding debt.
They accepted LIBOR plus 13/16ths of a point for old debt and 7/8ths of a
point for fresh money. They also agreed to use reserves accumulated in the
CSF for debt buybacks.  Debt conversions continued, and in seven months of
1988 they amounted to US$1.290  billion.   The cumulative amount since 1985
was US$4.60 billion. Conversions exceeded the additional borrowing, so the
external debt decreased to below US$20 billion.



- 52 -
The Public Sector
2.151    Implementation of the  SAP was preceded  by financial assistance
obtained from the IMF.  During  1983  Chile  abided by a two-year Stand-by
agreement.  It managed to  overcome  some earlier difficulties and complied
with the program's goals. The IMP continued fmnancing Chile, with an SDR
#750 EFF.  The goals of the programs to stabilize the economy under the DNF
agreement were highly coincident with those of the World Bank. This situa-
tion facilitated coordinated work between the government, the IMP, and the
Bank to harmonize the requirements of stability and structural adjustment.
The WFF stressed the significance of phasing out the preferential excwange
rate and currency swap operations of the Central Sank, import tariff reduc-
tion, a lower public deficit, and a cut In the country's demand for new
commercial funds.
2.152    Both the nfM and  the  Bank  shared  the  goal of a more efficient
public sector with better budgetary and institutional controls. The poli-
cies to reduce fiscal deficits became the basis of both stabilization and
adjustment programs. In the first case they contributed to controlling
inflation, and in the second they released funds required to susta,.n
measures for the medium-term reallocation of resources. This coordination
is always one of the difficult tasks a country faces when it engages in
stabilization and development policies, because it requires reducing the
budget deficit while at the same time raising private savings.
2.153    Chile's performance in this area proved successful.  In 1984 a tax
reform was initiated that provided fiscal incentives for reinvestment of
corporate profits. It also induced individuals to increase contributions
to the private pension fund system. That reform took place in January 1984
and was designed to stimulate the reinvestment of benefits by the gradual
elimination of a 401 surtax on corporate profits to 151 by 1986, with the
aim of cancelling  the  surtax  completely  in  1987.   Savings stimuli for
households consisted of deductions of 202 of funds invested in approved
shares, bank deposits, and other financial securities. Households could
also subtract--up to a 202 of their income--contributions to the private
pension funds in excess the minimum required by law. The yearly fiscal
costs from lost revenues were 0.61 of GDP for the period 1985-86. The
scheduled corporate surtax reduction was implemented, double taxation was
eliminated, and the applicability of the tax surcharge to public enter-
prises was confirmed. The fall In international prices, notably in 1986,
affected the revenues of ENAP (the National Petroleum Company) and CODELCO.
The adverse effect was in part counteracted by an improvement in collection
methods, (particularly in the VAT), by a postponement of personal tax re-
ductions, and by a new tax on the domestic consumption of gasoline. Be-
cause copper prices continued to climb and fiscal revenues expanded, the
government decided to release the tax pressure on the economy. Taxes on
credits and fuels were reduced, as well as those on corporate profits. The
VAT rate was lowered from 201 to 162 in 1988. These measures implied an
annual sacrifice of fiscal revenues equivalent to 31 of GDP from 1988 on-
wards (Table 2.23).

2.154    The government wanted to  Increase  public savings through current
expenditure restraint rather than through tax increases in order to avoid
hampering private savings. In addltion, measures introduced to ralse funds
could not create  an  anti-export blas.    The authorities reduced current
expenditures as a proportLon of GDP from slightly over 302 in 1984 and
previous years to 272 since 1986.   The cuts In current public outlays were
produced by restraint in wages and salaries, freezing nominal expenditures
ln unemployment programs, and reductions in the nominal amounts dedicated
to social programs. The emphasLs In social policies shlfted toward housing
and regLonal development programs.  In  1986  those outlays were raised to
252 of the total  social  budget.    Publlc  sector  savings durlng the SAP
increased dramaticallys  public savings,  whLch were negatlve in 1982, rose
to 0.6Z of GDP with the  1984  expansion  and, despite lower GDP growth, in
1985 were 3.8Z  of GDP.   FLscal  deficlts  declined  systematLcally as a
proportion of GDP from a peak of 4.41 in 1984 to less than 1S in 1987
(Table 2.24).

2.155    Public investment (Table 2.25) averaged 4.7S of GDP in 1982-83; it
rose steadily to 7.5Z in 1986 and was 7.31 in 1987. Investment was mainly
concentrated in productive sectors and infrastructure. In 1985-87, 291 was
oriented to mining  activities.  22S  to  electricity  and water,  202 to
transport, and 19X  to  housing.    CODELCO's  investment program of US$300
million was devoted to upgrading processing facilities for maintaining ore
grades in the Chuquicamata and El Teniente mines. In the energy sector, a
US$120 million investment initiated works in the Pehuenche and finalized
works in Camitillar, Alfalfar, and Colbun-Nachicura power plants. These
investments belong to a program destined to satisfy domestic energy demand
in the 1990s. Another US$100 million exploration and production investment
program may permit ENAP  to  counteract  the  decline  in oil production in
offshore facilities. Road, port, railroad, and agricultural infrastructure
investments are planned with financial assistance from the Bank and IDB.
To increase output in agriculture, the Government deferred large and low-
return irrigation projects and favored those projects that were small and
efficient. The SAP foresees that the PIP will amount to 7.51 of GDP up to
1992, thus helping to sustain expected annual GDP growth rates of between
4.5? and 5.

2.156    In January 1983 the  Government substantially increased its influ-
ence on Chile's productive resources.   It had to take over the administra-
tion of the largest private firms and banks to prevent their collapse.
Over a two-year period it returned them to private ownership and started to
divest itself of many state enterprises. Thus a program of reprivatization
was designed to increase the equity base of intervened debt-ridden institu-
tions and to distribute these equities among the largest possible number of
shareholders (Table 2.26). After legal settlement of the affairs of inter-
vened groups, which took place by the end of 1985, the sale of productive
firms proceeded at a rapid pace. By December 1986, over 902 of the produc-
tive assets of intervened groups had been sold to private investors. For-
eign investors started negotiations to purchase the largest brewery, as
well as INFORSA and COPEC, holding companies with interests in forestry and
energy.

2.158    The reprivatization of Intervened  and  public enterprises has met
the Government's goals and had a good influence on the capital market. The
reallocation of resources from the public to the private sector was

directed to improve the management of these firms and to set new and trans-
parent rules of the game that would enhance competitiveness and help to
develop capital markets.
Financial Rehabilitation
2.159    The main features of the  financial sector were the Central Bank's
supporting credit lines, which were  designed  to reduce the debt burden of
the private sector and to improve the liquidity of the financial institu-
tions. Before the start of the SAP, the Central Bank moved to prevent the
collapse of  private  firm   and  banks  through  general  subsidized debt-
rescheduling programs, rates of exchange, and other measures to dissuade
the flight of domestic dollar deposits.    In June 1985 the Government com-
mitted itself to the phasing out, in six months, of the subsidized exchange
rate for debtors owing more than US$50,000.   In 1987 the remaining subsi-
dies--applied to dollar swaps and deposits and credit lines for reschedul-
ing purposes--would end. These measures were taken to reduce the Central
Bank's operational losses and to increase monetary flexibility. Both the
IMF and the Bank concurred on ending subsidies in order to consolidate the
stabilization policy.
2.160    In mid-1985 a program for reprivatization of two major banks under
state management was started. All these measures had a positive impact on
the economy. Financial profits reappeared, and lending was channeled to
viable firms. thus consolidating a healthy interaction between financial
and productive resources.    Nevertheless,  the  problem of absorbing heavy
financial losses produced by the recapitalization and reprivatization still
remains. These losses were reflected in the Central Bank's results and
could affect future growth by acting as a strong restraint on public ex-
penditure and expansion of consumption (Table 2.27).

2.161    The process of reprivatization  of  intervened banks was rapid and
is still in progress. By the end of 1986 the capital of the Banco de Chile
and the Banco de Santiago was sold to 25,000 shareholders. The Banco
Continental was returned to its  former  owners.   The Banco Concepcion and
the B8nco Internacional were sold to domestic investors after the state
absorbed these banks' losses. There were also mergers of institutions that
gradually passed on to private  hands.    The divestment scheme is still on
target. Intervened APPs are also being reprivatized. In some cases the
majority of capital was sold to multinational companies; in others only the
minority was transferred to foreign banks and insurance companies, and the
majority of shares remain widely dispersed.
2.162    The emergency loans to banks were capitalized, and the shares were
sold to the public, under a *popular capitalisml program involving heavy
losses and limits to the share of control that any one person could hold.
To improve their solvency, the banks were allowed to sell their nonperform-
ing portfolios to the Central Bank.    By August 1986, private ownership of
nearly 602 was achieved for the Banco de Chile and the Banco de Santiago.
2.163    Nevertheless, the  costs  of  financial  rehabilitation  have been
high. The efforts have been consistent with economic growth and controlled
inflation, but the banking system remains vulnerable to external shocks. A
fall in international prices for the main traded goods implies a sharp
reduction in public revenues. The CSF could initially dampen such effects,
but only for a limited time and to limited extent. This is a serious con-
straint for sustaining the pace of growth and necessarily affects consump-
tion and living standards.
2.164    To continue the  financial  reconstruction, the Government refused
demands for new rescheduling, and restricted paying dividends to pre-crisis
owners who had not fully repurchased obligations sold to the Central Bank.
As long as the Central Bank exerts a monitoring role on the purchase of
debt and continues its policy in the direction already mentioned, greater
efficiency will permit banks to accelerate their absorption of debts.
2.165    As the program reaches completion the administration will be freer
to pay more attention to medium-term financial issues, of which the most
pressing will be the channeling of private savings. Largely mobilized in
private pension funds, private savings are now almost solely dependent on
the indexed securities of  the  Government and  commercial banks.  In this
sense, private savings portfolios are undiversified.
2.166    The Government also strengthened  its corporate and banking legis-
lation by reinforcing institutions that pursue legal action against compa-
nies, administrators, or shareholders or that function legally against
third parties.
Sectoral Performance
2.167    Even with unfavorable terms of  trade and persistent high interest
rates, the Chilean economy recovered in 1984. After a consistent SAP begun
in 1985, the pace of growth was quickened to 52 in 1986-87 and rose to over



- 59 -
62 in 1988. Per capita consumption grew slightly, and investment grew at
nearly 202 annually in 1986-87.    Unemployment was reduced to below 92 in
1988.
2.168    Minns.  Chile is one  of  the  lowest-cost producers of copper in
the world. On the average, mining activity represents nearly 9X of GDP.
Total Chilean exports of goods amount to US$6.9 billion per year; nearly
half of these correspond to mining, and 43X of that to copper (Table 2.28).
Over 902 of copper exports are carried out by. CODELCO and ENAMI (National
Mining Company); more than 902 of Chile's coal is produced by ENACAR
(National Coal Company); 100X of nitrates and iodine are produced by
SOQUIMICH; and 1002 of Chilean iron originates from CAP. All these are
public enterprises. In 1986, the Treasury received US$0.7 billion in taxes
and dividends from public mining companies.   Except for ENAGAR, these com-
panies are profitable, enjoy a good financial condition, and have a low
level of indebtedness.

2.169    In 1970 the State took  over  the Schwager coal mines, operated by
ENACAR, to prevent their closure. Other smller mines were integrated into
ENACAR. The ENACAR group is now recovering from prolonged financial prob-
lems, and partial privatization is envisaged. Nitrate production was stag-
nant during the period, and the mines owned by the State worked at a loss.
Efficiency and price improvements reversed SOQUIMICH performance, and the
company has generated profits since 1984.

2.170    Chile's strategy in  copper  production  is deterained by CODELCO.
In 1984 CODELCO planned an investment program averaging just over US$400
million a year during 1986-90.    Fiscal constraints forced the corporation
to cut back its investment plan by Imposing an annual upper limit of US$300
million. The Ministry of Finance suggested that CODELCO to sell off some
of its assets to Increase its capital expenditures above the yearly limit.
Something similar may bappen with the expansion plans of RAMI . Investment
restraint may prevent the company's cost decreases and the achievement of
more production efficiency in small- and medium-scale mines.
2.171    Agriculture.  To stimulate  production,  the financial measures of
1983-84 permitted renegotiation of the debts of almost half of all agricul-
tural borrowers.   In  1984,  the  authorities  provided a one-year special
credit line to finance working capital with future crops as the only col-
lateral. This financial assistance--with exchange rate adjustments and a
support price--generated investment In the sector and more efficient import
substitution in 1984-86. The impact of .rade and exchange rate policies
was to induce increased production of export-oriented crops. Production of
principal crops grew annually as follows: cereals, 7.32; legumes and pota-
toes, -3.22; Industrial crops, 14.32.   As more investment was channeled to
the sector, the area under cultivation increased. Production also showed a
significant expansions in 1982 fruit output was 917,960 tons, whereas in
1985 it reached 1,164,100 tons (Table 2.29).

2.172    The Government established a  reference  price mechanism for wheat
and oils and supported sugar producers through a variable import tariff
system. A price band was established that was based on the experience of
the previous five years and eliminated extremely high or low prices. When
international prices exceed the maximum limit of the band, the import
tariff decreases proportionately until prices reach the international
level. This reference method was applied to only two coonodities, so the
market vas kept quasi-free.
2.173    IANSA (the National  Sugar  Industry,  Inc.)  was considered to be
efficient. Nevertheless, external obligations and subsidized international
prices forced the Government to impose a tariff surcharge on sugar imports,
imposed since 1984. The resulting price was supposed to reflect estimated
long-run international  costs,  based  on  ten-year  Caribbean  prices.  At
present, however, the sugar reference prices are below current long-run
Caribbean costs and prices. This discrepancy could threaten the allocation
of resources in sugar production and lead to inefficient utilization of
land in the future.
2.174    During the recovery  period,  farmers'  incomes were significantly
increased. The improved agricultural environment favored traditional crops
such as cereals, vegetables, and industrial crops. and cultivated area
increased (Table 2.30).
