Agricola, World Bank and CEPAL.
2.175    The experience of  1982-86  shows  that  farmers  reacted to price
signals. In the future, care must be exercised to prevent special purchas-
ing agencies from causing price distortions that cause inefficient alloca-
tion of resources.    Certain  buying  schemes may not reflect opportunity
costs and may distort resource allocation. Policies favoring traditional
activities may also impede diversification and the expansion of activities
with higher profits.    The  Government  initiated  a program for promoting
exports, and the role of PROCHILE in this regard is very important. Export



- 62 _
promotion can be supported by the creation of marketing companies able to
penetrate markets in the Pacific basin, North America, and Europe.
2.176    Export diversification was le  by  agricultural goods, foods, and
forestry products, and it reacted positively to exchange rate conditions
and drawback schemes. This process requires great attention so that diver-
sification consolidates in the long run. In this sense, long-term programs
are vulnerable to deterioration in the terms of trade and world interest
rates.
2.177    Forestry.   Forestry  plantations  have  expanded  at  the pace of
70,000 hectares a year since 1974. The Government created a private/public
forestry commission in 1985 to coordinate investment in the sector. Sus-
tained growth of radiata  pine  plantations  requires industrial and infra-
structure  investments.    Technological  improvements  are  already taking
place, and the larger companies  have  begun using heavy thinning and high
pruning as a normal practice.    The  commission projected an annual growth
rate of forestry output of 8.91  for  the period 1985-1990.  Growth in sawn
logs was expected to be 4.9Z a year, and pulpwood 162. Market studies have
shown that the quality of Chile's wood is low, and therefore price pros-
pects are not  optimistic.    The  country must  improve lumber quality by
establishing better processing facilities. Private investments toward this
goal have already started, initiated by domestic and foreign firms. The
infrastructure requirements imply modernization of road, rail, and port
facilities in order to handle the output volumes expected after 1990.
2.178    Manufacturing.  The  industrial  sector,  adapted  to sharp tariff
reductions in 1975-79, suffered from the overvaluation of the peso in
1979-82, and output  levels  declined  significantly.   The economic growth
that took place after 1983, with export-oriented features and the import
substitution accompanying that expansion meant that by the end of 1986
manufacturing output had reached levels achieved during the peak years
before 1983. The reduction of the uniform import tariff and the favorable
and stable exchange rate had a healthy effect on production. The only
problem that may restrict future growth is the condition under which new
investments are financed.
2.179    The impact of manufacturing incentives  has been to foster produc-
tion, and this has been achieved by increased utilization of idle capacity.
The conditions for obtaining finance for plant expansion require the future
stability of the economic program.  As  long as demand expands as a conse-
quence of the overall growth of  the economy, profit prospects will make it
easier to face market interest rates and to invest in medium-term projects.
This applies also to foreign capital that could be attracted by an expand-
ing domestic market.  Nevertheless,  when  general growth is interrupted by
an external shock affecting the economy, idle capacity reappears, and the
objective of consistent and efficient outward orientation may be blocked.
2.180    Social Sectors.  The impact  of  the  economic crisis was that the
government spent relatively more on social security reform and on UEPs.
Wages dropped significantly, and other social programs suffered cuts and



- 63 -
postponements. By 1987, real expenditures in health, education, and hous-
ing had not yet regained  1981  levels.    These trends began to reverse in
1988.
2.181    The Government's  main  achievements  in  the  social sectors also
included the followingt
*    The  government  decided  to  increase  the  efficiency  of
equipment rather than to raise personnel outlays in the
health area.
*    Emphasis was  placed  on  preventive  health  care.  Infant
mortality rates continued to declines in 1973 the rate was
652 of live births, and in 1986 it fell to 19.4Z. In
addition,  provisions  of  clean   water  and  of  sewerage
services  contributed  to  improving   the  health  of  the
population.
*    In the area of housing  there was important progress.  The
authorities provided 120,000 houses to low-income families
in 1984-86 and the program was continued and expanded with
the Bank's support.
III. EVALUATION OF THE SAP
3.01     This chapter supplements the appraisal  of Chile's SAP with a more
comprehensive evaluation than that given In Chapter II. A country's eco-
nomic performance under a SAP can be assessed from different anglles:
*    by comparison with  economic  behavior  during the previous
period;
*    by comparison with  the  economic  behavior  of other coun-
tries;
*    by comparison with the programmed behavior of the economy.
Although these comparisons would  enrich  one's  vision of economic perfor-
mance under the Chilean SAP, it cannot be ignored that they are essentially
counterfactual historical ezercises:    in  one  case  the standard is past
history; in another it is a different economy; in the third it is an imagi-
nary scenario.. The last approach, however, offers the advantage of repli-
crting the decision-making process and is amenable to a disaggregation of
t.e causes of differences between program and reality (see the Technical
Appendix). After an overview of Chilean economic policy and an analysis of
the policy components of the Bank's SALs, the evaluation utilizes elements
of all three approaches.
3.02     The SALs initiated in 1985 in support of the Government's SAP were
directed to modify severe balance of payments disequilibria and to create a
spontaneous reallocation of resources induced by a new process of market



- 64 -
liberalization ia order to help reverse the economic crisis of 1982-83.
The program almed to diversify exports away from the traditional dependence
on coppert to achieve sustained growth in output, domestic savings; and
investment; and, finally, to rehabilitate the financial system which was
seriously affected by the crash.    The  loans were used to finance imports
and to release pressure on the balance of payments, thus enabling implemen-
tation of the adjustment program.
3.03     The SAP differed from  the  libe:alization policies carried out in
1973-81, which followed the idea that distortions in the economy could be
eliminated by  first  removing  controls  on  capital  markets.   Financial
liberalization thus became the most important component of domestic policy.
Increases in real interest rates were expected to stimulate demand for
money and other financial assets, so that output could expand and--provided
money demand increased more than money supply--inflationary expectations
would gradually disappear. That view relied particularly on the ease of
capital flows and took for granted that productive structure would respond
quickly and efficiently to  financial  movements.   The permissive rules of
the game allowed firms to relate  among  themselves and to control banks in
order to collect funds  for  their  own  investment outlets.  High interest
rates, both charged to borrowers and paid to depositors stimulated external
borrowing. This context facilitated the channeling of funds to high-risk
investment ventures.   That  period  coincided with  growth in output, and
exports also responded positively.   In  the meantime, the vulnerability of
the economy was increasing and the peso appreciated; falling copper prices,
and higher costs of importing petroleum precipitated the crisis in 1982.
3.04     The consequences of the crisis  implied  going back on the process
of liberalization.   Controls  and  subsidies  reappeareds   in 1983 impost
tariffs went from 101 to 20X; in March 235 import items were subjected to a
152 surtaz; in September 1984 the peso was devalued (232), and import
tariffs were carried to a uniform 35? rate. The Government was in need of
fiscal revenues, but it was compelled to rescue firms and banks that had
been considerably weakened. In 1982 GDP declined by 142, and a year later
it fell by 0.72.
3.05     The impact on employment was tremendous.    By the end of 1982 un-
employment reached 24Z of the labor force. Difficulties derived from the
crisis required Stand-by loans agreements with the rMF and forced the pro-
vision of subsidies to avoid widespread collapse of enterprises and banks
and capital flight.   At  this  point  it was clear that the stabilization
program by itself could not resolve the major problems. A new approach to
adjustment was required. Its search evolved finally into the SAP.
3.06     The SAP included  a  set  of measures, particularly institutional,
the most important of which affected export promotion and the public and
financial sectors. Before analyzing its content, it can be said that in
general the SAP was directed to restore liberalization but in a more grad-
ual and thorough-going way, in a context of an outward-oriented policy.
The treatment of the financial  sector,  in particular, was to complete its
rehabilitation and to provide new rules of the game that would avoid the
troubles experienced during the previous experience.



_65.-
A. ComDonents of Chilean Economic Policy
3.07     The economic programs lmplemented by the Chilean authorities since
1973 has experienced several modifications in shape and content. The ex-
perience can be characterized as a search for medium-term policies, at
least in certain time intervals, in order to achieve growth and eliminate
several structural constraints.
3.08     In evaluating the economic policies  included in the programs car.
ried out, one can  visualize  three main  components.   The first and most
important is the Government's role, in policy desipn and implementation.
The administration defines its program and provides the main framework of
measures. Within the framework, objectives are related to a set of instru-
mental variables on which the authorities rely to implement economic poli-
cy. According to the Government role and the type of programs implemented,
ezperience can be classified into three periods:
*    first liberalization, 1973-81;
*    economic crisis and transition, 1982-84;
*    the SAP, 1985-88.
A detailed analysis of the features of each general framewcrk and its
results is presented later In the section.
3.09     The second component of the programs is the role of the  INf.  The
need to stabilize the economy by improving the current account deficit and
reducing inflationary pressures was present during the whole period. The
I_N's financial assistance  implied  agreement with  the authorities with
respect to basic stabilization objectives and the means to achieve them.
The financial requirements necessitated definition of fiscal and monetary
policies consistent with the aims  adopted,  which could be monitored regu-
larly to determine the results of the programmed actions. in this sense
many of the structural objectives present in the Government's approach to
economic pclicy were shared by the IMF and monitoring was restricted to
ensuring that the policy instruments activated were the right ones. Here
the DIMF's conceptual framework of economic policy was important. Goals
were usually the reduction of the current account deficit and the infla-
tionary rhythm.    Policy  instruments,  however,  were  the exchange rate,
reduction in import protection, and the control of total domestic credit,
(particularly to the public .sector).  Monitoring was centered mainly on net
domestic assets of the financial system and on the size of public expendi-
ture.
3.10     The third  component  of  Chilean  economic  policy was the World
Bank's financial assistance and policy advice, notably in the adjustment
program since 1985 onward, once the SAP was recognized by the Government as
a pressing need. The general scheme of economic policy required to obtain
financial assistance from the Bank considered as goals the growth of GDP
and improvement in the balance  of payments.  Usually, the policy instru-
ments were fiscal' revenues and current Government expenditure, through



- 66 -
which public savings and the incremental access to foreign loans, both
public and private, could  be  manipulated.    This  approach cares for the
investment process and the achievement of sustained growth by combining
improvements in domestic savings and defining the role of foreign finance.
In the case of Chile, the Bank's contribution was to complement IMF support
once the administration had decided to implement a consistent SAP.
3.11     The new liberalization program  implied  a different strategy from
that applied after the catastrophic consequences of the 1982-83 crisis and
a return to the 1975_79 approach.   The growth supported by the Bank should
be based on the diversification of exports and on expansion of domestic
capacity to save and invest, and should be complemented by measures to
rehabilitate and reprivatize the financial  system.   The Bank's loans were
given to finance imports, thus to release pressures on the balance of pay-
ments, and this created sufficient flexibility to implement the SAP and
successfully manage the external  debt.    In  short,  the usual growth and
balance of payments goals considered by the Bank were maintained, but
emphasis put on the creation of conditions to ensure favorable evolution of
the economy in the medium term.
3.12     The three periods mentioned  before  showed  different uses of the
three policy components.  In  1973-81,  the main component in determining
economic policy was the Government's strategy of liberalization, strongly
complemented by foreign resources rather than by assistance from the IMF or
the Bank. The economic crisis and transition of 1982-84 brought redefini-
tions of economic policy on the part of the administration and an increas-
ing IMF role. The efforts of the authorities were not strong enough to
produce a consistent medium-term program during this period. Monetary
adjustments made to restore short-term improvements in the balance of pay-
ments and to reduce the inflation rate were clashed with attempts to follow
expansionary actions and with the reintroduction of protective fiscal
measures. The period 1985-88 showed a better balance among the three pol-
icy components, and this meant that the adjustment program could Include
different sets of goals and instruments in a consistent way. Perhaps
adjustment policies can or must be readapted in the future to ensure the
sustainability of stabilization and adjustment, but it is clear that the
policy package of 1985-88 was a good medium-term program that could harmo-
nize the stabilization and macroeconomic policies with structural modifica-
tions in the institutional and economic context.
First Liberalization, 1973-81
3.13     The economic situation of Chile  in  September 1973 was critical.
Output and employment were  falling while  the  annual  rate of inflation
soared to 1,000X. The Government and public sector enterprises were gener-
ating huge deficits. International reserves were negative and the country
was unable to service its external debt. The exchange rate was overvalued
and was supported by prevailing high levels of protection, both tariff and
nontariff. Ceilings in the monetary sector produced negative real interest
rates. There were many price controls, particularly on food and urban wage
goods. Wage adjustments were legislated, and a high level of social bene-
fits was financed by monetary expansion and foreign loans. Fiscal revenues



- 67 -
were weakened by many tax exemptions, tariffs, quantitative restrictions.
direct subsidies, and the acceleration of inflation.
3.14     The policies carried out since September 1973 were directed toward
producing rapid liberalization of the economy. The measures put the stress
on private initiatives and free market forces. The authorities emphasized
the subsidiary role of Government in the economic process. State interven-
tion was resitricted to cases in which divergences between social and pri-
vate benefits and  costs  prevailed.    Those  policies  aimed to eliminate
quickly distortions in price mechanisms.    The  economy was expected to be
opened to foreign trade, thus reducing to anti-export bias and tending to
improve resource allocation. A reform was conceived to establish a compet-
itive financial system in which the allocation of resources would be deter-
mined by forces of demand and supply and in which interest rates would
reflect this interaction.    The program  implemented  since 1973 also had
short-term stabilization objectives: to reduce inflation, to limit public
expenditures, and to achieve a rapid increase in international reserves.
3.15     The measures utilized to meet  the objectives were to free prices,
complemented by the massive adjustments of public enterprise prices and
fares and of other controlled prices.   The peso was devalued, the exchange
rate regime was simplified, and a crawling-peg system was adopted. Import
tariffs were sharply reduced. Interventions and requisitions of firms were
annulled, and the return of socialized enterprises to private owners was
implemented. The attack on public finances was based on substantial tax
Increases and long-term actions to reduce the size of expenditures and to
transfer responsibility for most resource allocation to private hands.
White- and blue-collar minimum wages and family allowances were unified.
Measures to mitigate heavy unemployment were taken; minimum employment
programs consisted of public works, wage subsidies to stimulate capacity
utilization, reforms of unemployment insurance, and manpower and training
programs.
3.16     A cornerstone of the  liberalization policy was the reconstruction
of the financial  system.    The  existing  legal  ceiling  on  the rate of
interest was progressively raised and ultimately eliminated. Monetary
correction clauses for financial obligations were legalized and exempted
from income  tax.    Central Bank  authority  over  all financial entities
receiving deposits from the public was strengthened. The Central Bank was
allowed to lend only to the  Government, commercial banks, and other finan-
cial institutions. The Superintendency of Banks coverage was extended to
supervise all financial entities (to become the Superintendency of Banks
and Financial Institutions, SlIP).    Development of multipurpose financial
institutions was encouraged through the relaxation of operating restric-
tions previously granted only  to  specialized  agents.   This measure gave
tremendous flexibility to the behavior of the financial system, the under-
lying idea being to increase competition and to take advantage of economies
of scale. Banking shares previously acquired by the State were sold back
to the private sector.   The  freeing  of financial activity was ensured by
the elimination of quantitative credit limits on commercial bank's lending
operations. Special credit lines from the Central Bank were progressively
eliminated.



- 68 -
3.17     Short-term instruments were  consistent with an austerity program
relying on orthodox monetary and fiscal policies and on depreciation of the
currency. Public expenditures were cut back, taxes were increased, and
credit was tightened.
3.18     The liberalization program can be viewed  as a move toward struc-
tural economic  change.    Policy modifications were  tremendous in their
effects, reflected in the Chilean authorities' inability to project the
future of the economy in  any  precise  terms.   The policy parameters that
gave shape to main behavioral relationships had changed drastically. The
liberalization efforic was strong and had impact on import elasticities,
capital output ratios, Input-output coefficients, tax collection percent-
ages, and so on.
3.19     A distinctive feature of  the  economlc  policy  of those days was
financial liberalization, which would facilitate the elimination of distor-
tions in the economy. Free interest rates were to result in positive real
rates and to stimulate savings.    Demand  for money was also regulated by
real interest movements, and so other financial assets would follow suit.
An increased and flexible set of financial assets permitted economic agents
to reverse the negative effects of the long-run excess demand for credit
produced by previous negative real rates of interest.
3.20     The effects of  the  program were  positive,  particularly in the
short term. Output and noncopper exports expanded, and the whole economy
seemed to move toward the goals pursued.   There was a growth process, con-
stantly linked to a short-run austerity policy, that caused the alternation
of recessionary situations. This was also the result of deep changes in
resource allocation. Restrictions to entry into financial activities were
eliminated in the hope of creating more competition for deposits from the
public. These measures, however, led to a situation in which money was
channelled not only according to healthy banking principles, but also to
the requirements of firms that controlled the banks. The mechanism pro-
vided many enterprises with means of obtaining finance for their own in-
vestment outlets. This permissiveness created availability of funds that
could be invested  in very  risky  projects.    A  main  deviation of this
approach was that the difference between the cost of credits in the domes-
tic market and abroad created a strong bias in favor of external borrowing.
There was an important leverage effect in obtaining external finance from
abroad. The rapid expansion of the domestic financial sector also inter-
acted with the increase in borrowing from external markets. The official
expectation that domestic Inflation had to converge to the pace of foreign
inflation--to the extent that demand for money kept rates of interest high
and that the exchange rate pressed Internal prices to slow their growth-
-resulted in an appreciation of the peso.
3.21     Thus, the Chilean economy was  slipping  into  a higher degree of
vulnerability. Quite apart from the traditional volatility of the external
sector--due to its dependence on international copper prices--there were
evolving two additional types of vulnerability.   One, domestic in nature,
derived from the Increased weakness of the financial sector. Credits were



-'69 -
allocated In a way that eroded banks' solvency. Economlc fluctuations made
their impacts felt through the expanded net of borrowing. It became clear
that many resources had been misallocated because of a sense of adventure,
overoptimistic  expectations,  and--mainly--rules  of  the  Same  that were
excessively permlssive.   This potential  vulnerability became actual vith
the devastating effects of the 1982 crisis. The second vulnerability was
the accumulation of external debt, whose service set an important con-
straint on the balance of payments and on growth. The liberalization pro-
gram clearly had positive effects in several areas, but it also had strong
social costs. The Government tried to reduce unemployment directly, in an
attempt to release some of the pressures on those sectors without abandon-
ing orthodox demand restraints to reduce inflation. However, the vulner-
abilities depicted above were so Important as to jeopardize the sustain-
ability of growth.
Economic Crisis and Transition, 1982-84
3.22     The appreciation of the  peso,  the  fall of copper prices, higher
costs of imported oil--these were important factors that contributed to
precipitation of the crisis in  1982.    The fixed exchange rate with tight
domestic credit did not produce the expected convergence of prices to
external inflation. Moreover, wage and cost indexations were applied in
the same belief. In June 1982 wage indexation was abolished, the peso was
devalued, and the depreciation schedule for the following 12 months was
announced. The devaluation provoked important capital losses because of
the existing indebtedness. Banks were weakened and the authorities had to
liquidate some and intervene others.  Unemployment rebounded to 24Z of the
labor force by the end of 1982. The foreign debt of the private sector was
US$1.6 billion in 1978 and USS8.1 billion in 1982. A fixed exchange rate,
combined with free capital movements and high domestic interest rates gene-
rated a systematic appreciation of the peso. Although copper prices were
falling, interest payments on the external debt amounted to nearly 40Z of
exports. This situation affected Chill's creditworthiness, and finally its
access to foreign resources wat closed.
3.23     The consequences of the crisis  were  a  retreat in the process of
liberalization. That process was interrupted more because of circumstances
than because of a modified mentality in the Government's approach to eco-
nomic policy. The economic and financial crisis forced the authorities to
intervene to avoid a general collapse of firms and financial institutions.
At the same time the country was experiencing serious disequilibria in the
balance of payments.  That is why  in this period there Was some confusion
in the economic approach as far as the Government was concerned, and the
IDF played an important role both in supplying financial assistance and in
helping to clarify the main lines of current economic policy. The two-year
US$500 million Stand-by agreement with the IMF at the beginning of 1983
opened a new stage in which stabilization measures had to continue and
played an important role in the evolution toward the Chilean SAP. The
IMF's net credit to the country rose from US$6 million in 1982 to US$600
million in 1983. In the transition period there were external debt negoti-
ations between the Government and international banks. Amortization pay-
ments for 1983-84 were rescheduled over eight years, with four years'



- 70-
grace, and new loans were obtained. The main problems then were the conse-
quences of a major economic crisis expressed, among other ways, by disequi-
librium in the balance of payments. The IMF'o fimancial assistance, guided
by its mandate to provide finance to eliminate such short-term disequilib-
ria, implied a definite stance of economic policy to restore payments equi-
librium and stop the general rise in prices.
3.24     The policy model implicit  in  the  DMF approach works on domestic
monetary equilibrium and its connections with the foreign sector of the
economy. It takes as its goals an increase in international reserve,
(usually produced by improvements in current account deficits), the slowing
down of the inflation rate, and the expansion of domestic credit to the
private sector. These goals are conceptually linked to policy instruments
that the responsible authority has to manage in order to achieve the pro-
posed-objectives. Usually these are the ceiling to domestic credit, the
subceiling determined by domestic credit from the Central Bank to the pub-
lic sector, and the exchange rate.    In certain cases, the IMF sets floors
to the amount of foreign exchange reserves that the Central Bank can hold
and limits to the short-term external borrowing of the public sector.
These limits are supposed to function in the following way. First, the
exchange rate is manipulated to provide stimuli to exports and disincen-
tives to imports in a context of liberalization. The IMF is more concerned
with the exchange rate than with import tariffs and export tax reductions
for budgetary reasons. The control of domestic credit affects conditions
in the money market. As internal credit hits the ceiling, increases in
international reserves derived from current account corrections will have
to meet the increased demand for money. If increased money demand does not
keep pace with the expected rise in money supply, then domestic credit
should decline in order to permit equilibrium in the money market without
fueling inflationary pressures.
3.25     In the case of Chile, the situation can be depicted in the follow-
ing terms. The current account deficit as a proportion of GDP was 14.51 in
1981 and 9.52 in 1982. For 1983, the policy goals were 7.22 for 1983 and
4.72 a year later.   These  percentages  implied a negative current account
balance of US$1,602 million in 1983 and no precise figure (a range between
negative US$1,088 million and negative US$1098 million) for 1984. Pro-
grammed net international reserves of the Central Bank were negative US$485
million in 1983 and zero in 1984.   The targeted rate of inflation for 1983
was 25S and was to be less than that in 1984. The ceiling to internal
credit was fixed at an increase of 332--measured as net domestic assets of
the Central Bank--for 1983. The limits on domestic credit to the Govern-
ment were expected to bring the overall deficit of the nonfinancial public
sector down to 1.7Z of GDP in 1983 and to eliminate it the following year.
This fiscal policy was centered on achieving improvements mainly through
cuts in current expenditures and increases in the operating surpluses of
public enterprises. The devaluation of the peso was ongoing since mid-
1982, and this meant moving from a crawling peg to a floating exchange rate
and then going back to a crawling peg.



- 71-
3.26     The framework of  economic  policy was restricted to implementing
measures aimed to achieve short-tetm goals.    There was a lack of definite
medium-term objectives, and complications from the financial crisis that
detonated in early 1983 confused even more the consistency of actions in
pursuit of stabilization.    The  economic  program  did not anticipate the
financial crisis of 1983. Therefore the adoption of comprehensive measures
was delayed. Later, different views within the Government about how to
cope with the problems confused matters  further.   The crisis made it more
difficult to maintain a policy of  demand  restraint.   The handling of the
exchange rate did not prove adequate and ended in further appreciation of
the pesos more forceful adjustment of that variable could have aggravated
the financial crisis. The process of liberalization was set back. Con-
trols and subsidies reappeareds in 1983 Import tariffs went up from 102 to
202; in March, 235 import items were subjected to a 152 surtax; in
September the peso was devalued, and import tariffs were again raised, this
time to a uniform level of 35?. The government was in need of revenues but
was compelled to rescue firms and banks on the verge of collapse. In 1984
the current account deficit increased because of negative external shocks
and slippage in fiscal and exchange rate policies. Fiscal policy became
expansionary in mid-1983 and continued so a year later. The fiscal deficit
as a proportion of GDP rose to almost double the targeted value for 1983,
and in 1984 it  reached  4.4S.    As  mentioned elsewhere, the quasi-fiscal
deficit exceeded the fiscal deficit in those years. GDP fell 0.7Z in 1983
and rose 6.3? in 1984.   Inflation was  slower than programmed in 1983 and
maintained its pace in 1984.
3.27     The general outcome of the  economic  program  can be summed up as
follows.
-   Success in growth and  in  the  current bal;i .e of payments
adjustment, particularly in 1983 (5.4? of GDP versus a
target of 7.22) was attributable to the residual effects of
structural reform policies pursued in the 1970s.
*   The main  result  of  the  1982-84  experience was official
recognition of the main features of the adjustment problems
and the need for a medium-term strategy: at this point it
was clear that the stabilization program alone could not
resolve major economic problems.
*   The stabilization program was  flawed  in design and imple-
mentation. The monitoring function was incomplete. Limits
were not set for private short-term debt and for the opera-
ting deficit of the Central Bank (Table 3.1).

The lack of conditionality and monitoring permitted the inflow of short-
term foreign capital, which was attracted by the fixed exchange rate and
tight financial policies. This type of capital also financed the rising
current account deficit in 1984.   In  addition, the Central Bank directly
attracted short-term capital through subsidies on forward and swap opera-
tions and through a premium on foreign exchange deposits.
The SAP, 1985-88
3.26     The SAP was initiated in 1985. The G6kernment decided to confront
the medium-term problems and implemented a program that included both IMP
and World Bank lending. The three components of economic policy performed
their complementary role with regard to the stabilization and structural
changes required. The IM provided a US$750 million EBY, later extended by
US$75 million; the Bank granted three successive SALs, for a total of
another US$750 million. The  13W-type stabilization policy alone presented
as its goals an improvement in the current account deficit, a decline in
the inflation rate, and a given level of domestic credit for the private
sector; its instrumental variables were total domestic credit (as a ceil-
ing), credit directed to the public sector, and an exchange rate adjust-
ment. The Bank's polLcy, in contrast, stressed medium-teml changes to
consolidate sustained growth.   Bank policy  presented  as  its goals GDP
growth and improvement in international reserves, derived from a better
current account situation.  These  goals were  to  be attained though the
following instrument variabless current expenditures of the public sector,
fiscal revenues, and foreign savings (which were intended to fill the gap
produced by insufficient domestic savings). Given behavioral relationships
such as the savings rate, the marginal propensity to import, and incremen-
tal capital-output ratios, the Bank's financial assistance would generate
the required additional investment and raise the level of economic activ-
ity. This is an extremely simplified version of the basic variables taken
into account under economic policy guidelines of the multilateral agencies.
3.29    Nevertheless, the combination of these two approaches to economic
policy does not produce a simple sum of both recommendations. In fact,



- 73 -
they interact with each other and generate a third approach in which sta-
bilization and structural measures are intertwined. In theoretical terms,
that given economic models produce Independently certain numbers for target
and lnstrumental variables In the IMF  and  in the Bank approaches does not
imply that these numbers persist when both economic programs are merged.
It may happen that the number of instrument variables exceeds the number of
targets, thus allowing more flexibility in the choice of strategies. For
Instance, targets include a reduction In inflation and an Increase in
international reserves, on the one hand, but GDP growth was also incorpo-
rated. To put into operation such a policy, which now regards objectives
of the kind In the Bank's approach, implies the use of instrumental vari-
ables such as fiscal revenues, current public expenditures, total domestic
credit and its distribution between public and private sectors, and ex-
change rate manipulation.    The  economic  policy  problem  usually has a
limited number of targets aad a larger number of instruments. To avoid
indeterminacy and incompatibilities in the implementation of policies, the
authorities treat a number of instruments as targets. The number of re-
maining instruments equals the number of previous targets and new targeted
instruments, and therefore it is possible to approach consistently the
solution of the economic policy problem.
3.30     This design closely resembles that  of Chile's economic policy for
1985-88. The SAP had as goals (see Table 3.2) real GDP growth (32 in the
first three years and 3.52 in 1988) and a declining expected rate of infla-
tion (starting with 25Z in 1985 and reaching 122 in 1988). The current
account deficit as a percentage of GDP was expected to change from 7.12 in
1985 to 1.8 in 1988. The exposition of main targets in Table 3.2 explains
only part of the interconnection between the DMP and the Bank approaches.
Table 3.2s Chiles Goals of the SAP, 1985-88

- 74 -
3.31     The role of the Bank was to facilitate and support the application
of structural adjustment measures.    In most  cases these objectives were
flexible, since they could not be targeted precisely in advance. The new
adjustment program was directed to modify severe disequilibria and to
creace spontaneous reallocations of resources by a new process of market
liberalization that could modify, in the medium-term factors causing pay-
ments imbalances. The program aimed to diversify exports to break the
dependence on copper. GDP growth had to be founded on increased domestic
.savings and investment and on the rehabilitation of firms and banks and new
rules of the game. This approach was more cautious then the one carried
out between 1973 and 1981. It was more gradual and much more careful about
consistency. The Bank's SALs provided finance to facilitate imports and to
release pressures on the balance of payments.
3.32     The authorities showed a  strong  commitment  to  the SAP and they
utilized the instruments of economic policy in a consistent way. Wage
increases were moderated, and the exchange rate was managed flexibly.
Tariff reductions and other export incentives contributed to creating an
atmosphere of growth. In the public sector. investment increased both in
quantity and quality, and confidence in management was restored. The pol-
icy of reducing fiscal deficits continued in the form of restraint in
current public expenditures and a more appropriate pricing policy for state
enterprises. Care was also given to preventing sudden falls in demand, in
the form of PIPs that could be activated if needed. Monetary policy was
consistent with reducing inflation and with balance of payments objectives.
The I)F continued to monitor net domestic assets of the Central Bank and,
quarterly, official international  reserves.    Among the medium-term meas-
ures, the Central Bank curtailed quasi-fiscal subsidies, and this contrib-
uted to reducing its operational deficit.   In what follows, the main areas
of the program are analyzed.
B.  Policy Components of the SALs
3.33     This section evaluates  policies  and progress  in the three main
areas of the SAP supported by the Bank's SALs: export promotion, public
sector savings and investment management, and rehabilitation of the finan-
cial sector.
Export Promotion Policies
3.34     These policies were  designed  to  create incentives for exporters
and to eliminate controls and subsidies connected with the exchange rate so
as to induce and sustain a medium-term supply response. The main stimulus
was bringing the exchange rate to a realistic level and periodically ad-
justing it in correspondence with domestic and external inflation. Tariff
reduction was brought about in steps.   This time the movement intended not
to affect suddenly important sources of fiscal revenues and not to have an
impact in a context of restrictive macroeconomic policy.
3.35     There were collateral incentives to promote noncopper exports, and
improvements in taxation schemes, such as regulations and procedures to



- 75 -
return to exporters the VAT, were also made to favor exporters. Reductions
in the relative inconvenience of using imported inputs subject to tariffs
became operational when drawbacks of  import tariff payments were permitted
to exporters. The revision of decree law 1,226 and 409 expanded the tariff
rebate regime to include indirect exports in the drawback system. Another
type of collateral action to promote nontraditional exports was focused on
the financial sector. A legal framework for export credit insurance and
partial guarantees for small exporters was established.
3.36     A measure agreed with  the  Bank  was  the  creation of the Cooper
Stabilization Fund (CSF) to stabilize exchange rate movements and dampen
the monetary effects of fluctuations in international copper prices. In
1988 reserves for that purpose accumulated, and part of them were used for
foreign debt buybacks. In other areas of production, there were policies
to restrict the use of support prices to a minimum number of products
(sugar, cereals). New legislation gave flexibility to the export promotion
agency, PROCHILE, in assisting exporters in their efforts to identify and
consolidate foreign markets.
3.37     Export promotion  policies  were  implemented  consistently.   The
measures in most cases were complementary, making for exemplary experience.
The economy underwent a period of steady growth, and the expansion of non-
copper exports was  considerably  higher  than  expected.  When evaluating
results, however, one must take into account the fact that the previous
liberalization program (1973-81) also produced growth in nontraditional
exports and in import substitution. The present issue is to know how much
of the economic expansion depended on still important primary exporting
activity and on foreign capital inflows.    Put in other words, how vulner-
able is a steady-growth economy such as Chile's if world copper prices fall
and the balance of payments  situation  worsens?    The SAP has to create a
basis for reducing this vulnerability, and when this occurs the conditions
for sustainable economic growth will  be  ensured.    It is obvious that it
takes time and concerted activity in terms of economic policy to reach
market size and permanency, implying long-term modifications of comparative
advantages. This qualification opens the debate to further considerations
of medium-term economic policy that would create safeguards against vulner-
ability. The scope should include future resource allocations to produce
permanent alterations in the  productive  structure.   This means much more
than using idle capacity. It is concerned with the expansion of new, addi-
tional, diversified, and more efficient capacity.
3.38     At this point it is  important  to consider the role of incentives
and tariff protection in the long-run allocation of resources. Exports
develop together with some import-substituting  activities.   As far as the
latter do not generate an antiexport bias, they can be considered effi-
cient. However, those activities compete with other production in the use
of scarce resources and then influence prices and quantities. It follows
that, in order not to create distortions in production for local and for-
eign markets, under competitive conditions export subsidies must equal
import tariffs. This excludes cases in which an export activity faces a
less than infinitely elastic demand for its commodities and there is an



- 76 -
optimal tariff determination. Under competitive conditions, in which Indi-
vidual producere have no power to Influence prices, the treatment of export
subsidies and tariffs  is  Important  in  detenrmaing  future allocation of
resources in production and consumption.    The neutrality of subsidies and
tariffs ensures efficient allocation; it equates at the margin the cost of
export promotion with the cost of import substitution In order to create
additional external revenues.
3.39     So far the incentives introduced  to  release the market from pre-
existing controls have had a positive effect on export growth. But to
maintain consistency in the medium-term there must be a defined program of
incentives and tariff adjustments that is consistent with efficient
resource allocation in production and with consolidation of new comparative
advantage.   Reducing  the  relative weight  of  primary comodity exports
requires a tremendous effort in expanding nontraditional exports. Other-
wise diversification may suffer in the face of contingent negative external
shocks.  Chile has  done well with    removing quantitative restraints to
trade and with the imposition of a uniform tariff that tends to distribute
effective protection evenly. A movement toward free trade can be seen here
and in the introduction  of  export  subsidies.   This movement corresponds
with a depreciation of the exchange rate in order to reach equilibrium in
the balance of payments.   Subsidies  can be represented by credit and tax
preferences. The issue is that, in order to maintain a consistent set of
policies in the medium-term, greater attention should be given to the way
in which the exchange rate is adjusted and its interrelation with the
existing protective structure.   Future import-substitution activities will
emerge, and these should not develop antiexport biases. A long-run problem
is  to  determine  hoc  to  modify  incentives  and  introduce  additional
institutional changes consistent with a rapid but smooth reallocation of
resources that will alter comparative advantage in the "right' way. The
SAP as it affects export promotion should be maintained in order to create
defenses against external shocks to the balance of payments. A policy
strategy should be designed because the interconnection between this
economic area and public finance is substantial.
Public Sector Management
3.40     During the 1985-88 SAP  the main  objectives of fiscal management
were two-fold: to achieve public savings equivalent to 4.8? of GDP and to
sustain public investment at a level of 7.5? of GDP. The policy of mone-
tary stabilization required increasing the operational surpluses of public
enterprises and improving revenues; the main line of attack has been cur-
rent expenditure restraint.   The  stabilization measures implied agreement
with the DMF on views that the Bank also shared completely. In this case
parsimony meant to apply adjustments to salaries and pensions that were
lower than cost of living   and smaller transfer payments for unemployment.
Public investment rose, however, and since 1986 has surpassed 72 of GDP.
investment was concentrated on productive sectors and infrastructure; low-
return projects were postponed, and emphasis was placed on high-return
outlets. The National Planning Office, ODEPLAN, participated actively in
the evaluation and approval of profitable projects.



- 77 -
3.41     Although not an IMF or World Bank conditioaality, reprivatization
of state enterprises was another important item in the set of measures
taken by the goverament to put its finances in order. The reallocation of
resources from public to private sectors was expected to improve the man-
agement of those firms.
3.42     Another line of policy in public sector administration was changes
in th. tax system. The Government's aim was to step up public savings by
expenditure restraint rather than by hardening the tax burden. There were
improvements in VAT  collection.    The  additional fiscal revenues derived
from the expanded value of copper exports permitted relieving the tax
pressure. Import tariffs could be lowered successfully, and this was done
for taxes on  credits  and  fuels.    Tax  reductions  applied to corporate
profits and the VAT rate diminished from 202 to 162. The fiscal cost of
tax reform was at last 32 of GDP.
3.43     The positive effects of  these modifications on economic activity
contributed to reinforcing growth and stability, since inflation declined.
However, these results were also facilitated by the positive impact that
international prices had on the balance of payments and public finance.
since the main exporter of primary goods is CODELCO, a state enterprise.
Other lines of action were the initiation of studies to improve management
of state enterprises.   In  practice,  a  consistent policy of state firms'
pricing and improved management permitted higher operational profits from
these companies. The administration of public finance in the context of
the SAP was successful. Kost of the measures followed from requirements of
stabilization policies necessary to comply with INP conditionalities. In
this respect, short-term policies were directed to contain the public defi-
cit. The tools for influencing the general rise in prices and the current
account deficit were to increase revenues and decrease expenditures. This
action was directed toward the primary deficit, (that is, the noninterest
deficit).
3.44     According to definitions of deficits used by international insti-
tutions, the primary deficit plus real interest payments (real domestic and
nominal foreign) equal the operational deficit; the operational deficit
plus inflation correction equal public nonfinancial sector borrowing; and
public sector borrowing requirements equal expenditure less revenue. The
primary deficit measures how current actions affect public net indebted-
ness. To evaluate the sustainability of deficit reductions, it is impor-
tant to evaluate the behavior of the deficit. Chile's measures were mainly
directed to raise revenues by increasing the operational surpluses of state
enterprises and to expand tax collection by improving efficiency in the
process. Current expenditure restraint was the most important factor, and
this did not prevent the expansion of public investment. The Government's
policy was concentrated on avoiding financing the total deficit by money
creation; it was done by increased public and foreign savings. Since 1983
the quasi-fiscal deficit was 6Z of GDP.   In 1985 it climbed to 13Z, and in
1986 it declined to 10. The efforts at rehabilitation and reprivatization
generated severe disequilibria that had to be financed with external funds.
The foreign debt includes expenses that only could be repaid in the future



- 78 -
and the Government will have to make available the necessary revenue. From
a short-temn point of view, the evolution of the primary deficit is impor-
tant to foresee the trend in closing the gap, but it is not itself suffi-
cient to predict the sustainability of the program in connection with the
required relation between future revenues and expenditures.
3.45     The Bank concurred with the IMP in the observance of stabilization
measures. The SAP was directed to remove obstacles to medium- and long-
term growth, such as distortions in productive incentives, overvalued
currency, price ceilings and controlled interest rates and credit, import
tariffs, and subsidies. In this sense the Bank expected the program to
open a new liberalization period.    The positive impact of external shocks
allowed improvement in the public imbalance between expenditure and revenue
without increasing the tax burden  on  the  economy.  There was a harmonic
connection between structural adjustment and stabilization objectives in
the sense that many of the trade-offs between policy instruments did not
pose serious  problems.    Sometimes  over-reliance  on  domestic borrowing
implies high rates of interest with reductions of private investment,
whereas excessive dependence on foreign finance leads to currency apprecia-
tion, which worsens the current account deficits. Chile was able to avoid
money creation, and thus it could reduce the inflation rate. Good external
conditions--both in foreign trade and lower interest rates--released pres-
sures on public sector management, permitting the fulfillment of objectives
in that area by making less operative the obvious trade-offs between fis-
cal, monetary, and exchange rate policies.
3.46     However, the SAP still  lacks  a  structural  view of the deficit-
handling aspects that will persist in the long run unless additional cor-
rective measures are  taken.    Such  a  structural  view, should take into
account--through conservative estimates--the dependence of fiscal revenues
on copper exports that still exists.   A long-run policy should contemplate
reforms that would consolidate a permanent revenue and expenditure struc-
ture consistent with reduction of the structural deficit in the future. So
far the SAP has succeeded in the implementation of measures directed to
liberalize the economy and facilitate  growth.    This was done by means of
institutional changes that put the growth process in motion. It was evi-
dent to the Bank that sustainability had to be ensured. That task has to
be carried out by programming future revenues and expenditures and taking
due account of external shocks and their likely effects on public sector
management and on external debt. Laying the ground work for sustainability
involves more than considering primary fiscal deficit trends. It implies
the normalization of revenues and expenditures by relaxing the tension in
the trade-off between budgetary requirements and trade policies. On the
revenue side, it means moving gradually to a structure closer to the one
existing in industrial countries where income taxes play a more important
role. The economy must be prepared to meet and curb future difficulties
arising from eventual external  shocks.    The burden of rehabilitation and
reprivatization has to be explicitly managed through time, and its impact
adequately programmed. The Bank's strategy was based on the idea that
resumption of economic growth is a prerequisite to creditworthiness.
Financial support has been given in the form of fast disbursement and flex-
ible utilization of funds.



- 79 -
3.47     There were also social components  of the SAP that were emphasized
more in the later course of  implementation.   The Bank also catalyzed fur-
ther finance from commercial banks.    Perhaps  a  cost of introducing few
conditionalities has been to maintain the SAP within the boundaries of its
medium-term application. The Bank was, rightly, interested primarily in
putting the process in motion, action that in itself has enhanced long-term
sustainability. However, much of the work for sustainability will still
have to be performed by the Government, outside the period of the SALs.
Financial Sector Policies
3.48     The main financial policy  objective  in  the framework of the SAP
was to complete the rehabilitation of the corporate and banking system and
to provide sound rules of the game that could give transparency and normal-
ize financial activity in  free market  conditions.    The weakness of the
previous liberalization process, started in 1973, was the permissive condi-
tions prevailing in the financial system. Great efforts were required from
the Government to bring order back to that seriously affected market.
These included the return to subsidies which finally hampered the correct
functioning of capital markets.    The  SAP was  conceived to complete the
rehabilitation process and to remove the subsidy structure supported by the
Central Bank, in  its  preferential  exchange  rates  for  dollar swaps and
deposits.  The action was  to  stop  all  preferences and to accelerate the
recovery of losses by private banks' repurchases of their portfolios.
3.49     A second policy line was to increase the surveillance and monitor-
ing of financial institutions to ensure their healthy functioning. New
legislation was introduced to ensure transparent control by stockholders
and to limit the participation  of  related  firms.   In the past financial
crisis, the cross-participation of firms through channeling bank funds to
their own investment projects weakened financial stability by increasing
risks of insolvency. Therefore, the Superintendency of Banks was strength-
ened and its functions expanded to control the financial sector.
3.50     Third, a set of legal reforms was put in operation to consolidate
the rehabilitated financial  system.    The  banking  law  introduced a new
deposit guarantee scheme relating the responsibilities of the Central Bank,
bank shareholders, and depositors.   Decree  law. 3,500 was also amended to
permit enhanced participation of pension funds in equity markets. Insur-
ance company and mutual fund regulations were reinforced with prudential
portfolio guidelines about diversification and limits to related-party
investments.
3.51.   The measures taken in  the financial sector, mainly institutional,
were directed to achieve a normalization of the activity in the long run.
The efforts of rehabilitation and reprivatization have been successful,
since financial sector activity  has  been  normalized.    The costs of the
success have seriously affected the level of public indebtedness and are
financed with external savings. No detailed information is available about
these costs and their financing over the future.



- 80 -
3.52     chile's management of foreign debt has also been remarkable.  The
SAP was designed with the definite aim of controlling the growth of debt
and debt service. After a  period  of rescheduling and repricing, the Gov-
ernment succeeded in repurchasing part of its obligations. Chile, which is
considered as one of the highly indebted Latin American countries, managed
to bring its debt down to around the size of GDP and then to further reduce
it. The consisteany of the SAP and the handling of external debt permitted
the country to improve its creditworthiness significantly.
3.53     In the area of  short-term policy, the administration was careful
to increase the money supply only vhen growth in money demand required it.
Thus there was no room for stimulating inflation. Control was also exerted
on additional domestic credit to private and public sectors. Besides, I10
monitoring was centered on the management of net domestic assets of the
Central Bank and the supervision of quarterly targets for official interna-
tional reserves. The rehabilitation program ended in 1987, and it was at
various times complemented by a tight monetary policy in order not to lose
ground in pursuing of  stabilization  objectives.   Because the authorities
did not claim resources by issuing currency, inflation was controlleds it
came down from 26.41 in 1985 to 12.31 in 1988, with a short upward movement
in 1986.
C. Historical Comparisons
3.54     This section presents a  comparison of economic performance during
the last three periods of Chilean economic policys the first liberaliza-
tion, 1973-81;, economic crisis and transition, 1982-84; and the SAP,
1985-88.
First Liberalization, 1973-81
3.55     The liberalization period 1973-81 was characterized by three dif-
ferent phases of the evolution of economic activity: recession, recovery,
and boom (Table 3.3). The recessive situation prevailing in 1973 remained
until 1975. GDP attalned a depressed level in 1973; experienced a slight
increase (to 1.02) in 1974; and declined again (to 12.9Z) a year later. In
those years, private consumption and gross domestic investment decreased
significantly. This can be seen particularly in fixed investment, since
the increase in gross domestic investment in 1974 was affected by accumula-
tion of inventories. Exports went on expanding while the reduction in the
level of economic activity produced an important decline in imports. The
behavior of the trade balance prevented a larger fall in GDP. The liberal-
ization program intended to trim the role of the State, to lower inflation,
and to liberalize trade And financial activities. The start of liberaliza-
tion Implied keeping recessive conditions by demand restraint. However,
in 1976 the situation reversed and the fruits of economic policy showed in
a rebound of economic activity.

Lo At market primc.
3.56     The economic recovery took place between 1976 and 1979.  Financial
liberalization started in 1975, and since then credit was allocated as a
result of demand and supply. In 1975 the exchange rate was unified; first
a crawling peg was adopted, followed by a preannounced minidevaluation
system. Import tariffs were gradually cut down, to a uniform 102 rate in
1979. Copper prices were low, and restrictions restrained the growth of
trade. However, cheaper imported inputs and credits with low rates of
interest allowed the expansion of noncopper exports, so Import subntitution
and nontraditional exports took place in a significant way. Liberalization
stimulated exports of forestry, fishmeal, and fruits. Agricultural activi-
ties shlfted from traditional products such as cereals, oilseeds and sugar-
beet toward export-oriented crops such as fruits, timber, and vegetables.
Manufacturing shrunk relatively In the beginning of the program but later
regained its share, with a shift toward wood products, fishmeal. and non-
ferrous metals and away from textiles, chemical items, and nonmetal goods,
which suffered from competition with Imports. Improved efficiency in pro-
duction stimulated the recovery of 1976-79. Competition with foreign firms
induced domestic companies to modernize their old equipment through small
investments and adoption of modern management techniques.  The increase in
productivity fostered output. Fixed investment began to grow in 1977, and
in 1978 its level surpassed the amount attained In 1974. The utilization
of idle capacity and its slow expansion was taking place through higber
productivity. Thus, despite the recovery, the rate of unemployment re-
mained above previous historical standards for Chile. The rationalization
of labor costs implied that private and public sectors were laying workers
off. The rate of unemployment was above 10X. Real interest rates were
kept high, and this stimulated external borrowing. The Government's labor
policy went into effect In 1977, with automatic ex post indexation of
wages. The Chilean peso appreciated; Imports were stimulated; and the
competitiveness of exports was eroded.



- 82 -
3.57     The years 1980 and 1981 showed a  boom. The rate of growth of GDP
declined as the expansion was  reaching  its  end.   The pegging of the ex-
change rate with the opening of capital movements brought about important
trade deficits (Table 3.4). For example, the resource balance in 1979 was
-US$597 million; in 1980 it was US$1,056 million, and a year later it Was
-US$3,367 million.   In  addition,  interest  payments  abroad jumped from
US$583 million in 1979 to US$919 million in 1980 to US$1,423 million the
next year. Public savings declined in 1981, foreign resources financed
nearly half of gross domestic investment. In 1977-79 the latter was 141 of
GDP, while foreign savings  amounted  to  2.71  of  GDP.   In 1980-81 gross
domestic investment was 13.81 of GDP, and foreign savings climbed to 7Z of
GDP. The end of the boom showed the decline in the availability of foreign
resources to finance investment, particularly private investment. There-
fore, private savings were insufficient to finance investment and to sus-
tain growth. In the meantime, serious veaknesses were developing in the
financial system with the liberalization of the operations of financial
intermediaries since 1979-80.

Economic Crisis and Transition. 1982-84
3.58     This period corresponds with the economic crisis that followed the
previous period and a year of economic transition toward a new adjustment
program (Table 3.5). In 1984 there were several setbacks in economic pol-
icy design and implementation. The 1982-83 crisis was one of Chile's worst
economic disasters. GDP declined to -14.11 in 1982 and -0.71 in 1983.
Unemployment climbed to a fourth of the labor force. A large part (nearly
half) of the productive sector was insolvent, and the same fate befell the
biggest commercial banks. Total external debt rose from US$17.2 billion in
1982 to US$19.7 billion in 1984.   In 1982-83 total investment was entirely
financed by domestic savings.      External savings turned negative because
foreign banks were reluctant  to  lend more.  Economic depression created
public deficits as  revenues  decreased.    Current  expenditures could not
contract accordingly because of the high level of existing unemployment and
the  Government's  social  security  obligations.    Consequently, domestic
credit to the public sector had to Increase. The 1982-83 devaluations in
the midst of the recession had devastating effects on the corporate sector,



- 83 -
sitch was highly  Indebted  in  foreign  currency.    Bankruptcies spread
throughout the country.   Imports  declined  and contributed to improve the
resource balance, but the current account deficit was around US$2 billion.
Inflation showed down in 1982 because of the impact of the crisis: in 1982
consumer prices rose nearly lOXI in 1983 they grew 272, and in 1984 202.

3.59     The economic crisis was  the  result  of  external shocks, such as
declining copper prices, and of the application of incorrect economic poli-
cies both at the time and in the previous period. Economic growth was
expected to  arise  spontaneously  from  the  earlier  liberalization.   in
December 1982, Chile developed a short-term stabilization program, sup-
ported by the IMF, that was intended to reduce current account deficits,
increase international reserves, and slow down inflation. Nevertheless,
outbreak of the financial crisis at the beginning of 1983 forced the Gov-
ernment to take control of the largest financial banks in distress. The
recovery was weak because of the financial crash. The major problems con-
fronting the economy were unemployment, lack of foreign exchange, and the
need to increase savings and investment.    Tariffs were doubled in 1983 in
order to raise fiscal revenues.   There were also pressures to use tariffs
for the protection of domestic producers; therefore an anti-export bias was
generated.
The SAP. 1985-88
3.60     The need for  structural  adjustment  developed  In the transition
period, and it finally began in. 1985 with the program agreed with the InM
and the World Bank. It was a new liberalization attempt that would take
account of past policy mistakes. Graduality was introduced, and monitoring
and supervision were reinforced, notably in the financial sector. The
fragile recovery initiated in 1984 was sustained in the following years.
Some of the effects of external shocks were gradually reversed. In 1985
the GDP growth rate was 2.42.   The following two years it surpassed 52 and
it was above 62  in  1988.   Unemployment declined considerably, to levels
similar to those during the liberalization  period.   If these rates of GDP



- 84 -
growth are compared with those prevailing In the recovery stage of the
liberalization period 1973-81, they  look  a  little  lower.  owever, they
reflect a more solid SAP.
3.61     The main feature of this program continues to be the articulation
between stabilisation and structural adjustment,  as can be seen In efforts
to re-establish an export-oriented economy, a genuie expansion In public
and private savings, and the strengthening of a rehabilltated fif.Lncial
sector (Table 3.6).

3.62     The inflation rate vas  notably  reduced  to  122 and the current
balance of payments deficit contracted to little more than 12 of GDP.
Official gross reserves increased by more than one third with respect to
1981. These results speak well for the stabilization efforts carried out
by the the Government with the IHP's financial support and provided a good
basis for the SAP supported by the Bank. the medium-term goals were also
successfully attained. Gross domestic investment represented 16.92 of GDP



- 65 -
in 1987-88, and the ratio of gross national savings to GDP rose from 5.42
in 1985 to 15.42 in 1988.    The  ratio  of external debt to GDP grew until
1985 and then began to decline, to 832 in 1988. Exports kept growing, at
rates similar to general economic (GDP) growth, and continued their trend.
Imports, however, accelerated according to- the economic expansion. The
terms of trade evolved favorably for Chile from 1986 onwards. The rise in
the price of copper played an Important role in surpassing the program
targets.
3.63     As far as  the  stabilization  program  was  concerned, when minor
slippages in policy lmplementation occurred, corrective measures were taken
quickly, and this permitted the program to continue vith little deviations
from the targets. The structural actions were evaluated more by the meas-
ures taken than by  the  actual  results.    In --his sense, the results of
structural reforms were mixed with those coming from the stabilization
program. That is why stabilization sad structural policies merge in a
general and consistent program that has been successful in the medium-term,
but that can still be extended to consolidate new long-run targets for
resource,allocation and to make economic policy less dependent on copper
exports.
D. Resional and Country Comparisons
3.64     This section offers  comparisons  of  Chile's economic performance
with that of other Latin American countries during the SAP period. The
comparisons, although suggestive, must be interpreted with caution because
of data limitations with respect to varying quality and methodological
derivation. Nevertheless, the recovery of the Chilean economy that took
place after the 1982-83 crisis was remarkable, particularly after 1985, in
large part because the implementation of the SAP's medium-term goals was
accomplished and the process of liberalization was reset on more solid
grounds. Chilean expansion of economic activity looks interesting in the
Latin American context, since there are several regional similarities in
international trade and external  debt.    In  these comparisons the period
chosen will be restricted to the 1980s.
3.65     In terms of  average  annual  rates,  Chile  grew  at 2.22 between
1981-88, when the Latin American average for the period (including Chile)
was 1.62 (Table 3.7). Chile's expansion was above the average. Most of
Latin American countries experienced recessions in 1982 and 1983. Only
Colombia, Cuba, and the Dominican Republic showed increases of GDP in both
years. The decline of GDP in 1982-83 was larger in Chile (14Z) than in the
region as a whole  (4X).    The  tremendous  impact of the Chilean economic
crisis brought down  the  average  rate  of  growth.    If one compares the
expansion that took place in 1984 and in following years, the deviation of
Chile from the rest rises considerably.    Chile  grew at an annual rate of
5.12, while the region as a whole experienced 2.9Z growth in 1983-88.

3.66     Within Latin American,  economic  performance  in 1983-88 differed
according to whether a country was an oil exporter. Chile is not. During
the period, annual GDP growth for the region as a whole (excluding Cuba)
was 2.91. For oil-exporting countries it was 1.31, and for non-oil-export-
ing countries it was 3.92 Chile'. annual GDP growth during the period was
5.1?. In general, oil exporters grew less because of the depressive impact
of the years 1982-83.    However,  if  1982  and 1983 are excluded, non-oil
exporters are still above, and oil exporters, below the average. The
latter experienced slower progress because of falling petroleum prices.
Chile's performance was  excellent  in  this  context. *       hen compared with
other countries in the region, it had the most rapid annual GDP growth
(5.11) during 1983-88, followed by Brazil (5), Colombia (4.61), and Costa
Rica (4.21). Product per capita (Table 3.8) also increased more in Chile
than in the rest of the region.    For 1981-88, it grew 0.31 yearly--a slow
rate, but one that was exceeded only by Barbados (0.61). Most Latin
American countries suffered importment declines in GDP per capita. The
average variation for the whole group was -11 a year.

3.67     Chile experienced a significant growth  in the purchasing power of
its exports of goods  in  1983-88s    10.7?  annually on the average (Table
3.9). For the region as a whole, it was 2.5; oil-exporting countries lost
(-4.12 per year), and non-oil exporters Increased (7.9? per year), purchas-
ing power of their exports. Although magnitudes differ, the pattern of the
comparison persists in the evolution of the terms of trade. The region saw
its aggregate terms of trade deteriorate at -2.1? a year. Oil exporters
suffered a more severe loss, at an annual rate of -7.8?. On the contrary,
non-oil exporters experienced an improvement of 2.1Z per year. Chile's
terms of trade rose 3.7? yearly between 1983 and 1988. This performance
was particularly affected by the recovery in the price of copper, which
took place in 1986 and which was significantly accentuated in following
years.  The improvement  in  Chile's  terms  of  trade exceeded the average
performance of the region and  other major  countrier within it.  This was
one of the basic factors explaining the quick recent growth of the Chilean
economy.

program with its stabilization component succeeded in reducing the rise in
local prices.  In fact, Chile  has  controlled  its inflttion at a level of

experience led to an Iprovement In its creditworthiness.

3.70     To sum up, the Chilean  experience  in the years of implementation
of its SAP has  been  outstanding.    In  the  comparisons with other Latin
American countries, however, not all the characteristics of the Chilean
evolution during the period considered have been favorable. For example,
Chile was one of the countries with a permanently high level of unemploy-
ment, sharing that distinction with Colombia, Jam_ica, and Panama (Table
3.12).  Even when the  SAP  started  in  1985, allowing Chile to reduce its
unemployment, figures were still  high.   The most significant improvements
In this regard have taken place since 1986, but CEPAL's figures use data
from different sources and methodologies that make cross-country compari-
sons and the reconciliation with official national estimates extremely
difficult. Data on employment and real wages show that in Chile a great
part of the burden of adjustment was borne by laborers. The feature of the
economic policies implemented was that, in order to sustain employment,
wages were allowed to decline in real terms. This trade-off did not occur
ex post, since wages remained low and unemployment was controlled at a high
level. The policy actions were directed toward the emergency employment
programs, or EEPs, which prevented the unemployment rate from soaring.
When the economic expansion gained force, the EEPs could be ended. Around
1988 the evolution of wages and salaries changed, and important real
increases took place.
