E. Reality versus Programs
3.71     This section confronts the ex ante programs with the ex post real-
ity during the SAP period and also in the immediately preceding years. The
three periods show different experience. First, the liberalization program
was expected to bring the economy back to equilibrium both in production
and external accounts, based on deregulation and the search of more effi-
cient allocation of resources.   Some structural problems developed and the
boom of 1980-81 ended in the worst economic crisis in Chile in the past 50
years. Since then, economic policy looked for measures and programs to
stabilize and rehabilitate the seriously damaged economy and then to con-
solidate the achievements in the medium-term. It took the 1982-1983 crisis
and the 1984 transition to clear the way for an adjustment program that
could confront structural problems.   In the meantime, the policies carried
out in 1982-84 were mainly directed to price stabilization and balance of
payments corrections. At the beginning these results were not as close to
the programmed goals as expected, but the experience led the authorities,
first, to diagnose structural problems and, second, to devise a consistent
program.
3.72     The period of economic  crisis  and transition also showed greater
deviations between realization and programming. To illustrate this aspect,
main deviations between actual and programed variables can be averaged for
the complete period (Table 3.13).



- 92 -
Table 3.13, Chiles Average Deviations from Program Goals,
1982-84 and 1985-88
(percent)
Item                            Stabilization, 1982-84       SAP, 1985-88
Real GDP Growth                          -1.4                        2
Inflation                                -0.4                     1.4
Current Account of the
balance of payments/GDP                -1.2                    -0.4
Fiscal Balance/GDP                       -0.4                     -0.1
Average total Deviation                  -0.9                      0.8
Variance                                  6.8                      4.3
The four targeted variables have been compared year by year with actual
values. The averages have been calculated for the period. In 1982-84 the
average deviations were negative for all individual targets. This means
that for variables programed values  exceeded  actual values.  In 1985-88,
the four variables chosen showed  a  different picture.  Actual values sur-
passed programmed values  for  economic  growth  and  inflation.   That is,
results were better than expected. The opposite happened for the current
account and fiscal deficits; both these variables showed overoptimistic
prograuming. However, the magnitudes of the deviations can be compared
with the same items in 1982-84; even when results were not as good as
expected, the average deviations in the more recent period were smaller
than earlier. The last two rows of Table 3.13 show that the average total
deviation (for the four targets) was positive in 1985-88. In other words,
although there were positive and negative deviations, the dominant value
was lower than zero, and this points to the idea that the bias was to
underestimate the evolution of the variables. Perhaps this funding is also
the result of a more consistent set of policy measures in a definite direc-
tion. Soae of these virtues derived from the SAP were absent in the eco-
nomic policy of 1982-84. The smaller variance in the SAP experience gives
the idea of greater uniformity.
3.73     These figures by themselves  are  insufficient to prove the point,
but they are helpful in synthesizing a set of conclusion derived from a
more detailed and deep evaluation of the program. The superiority of the
SAP's performance with respect to that of 1982-84 is obvious, but it is
also superior to that of the period of liberalization policy that preceded
stabilization policies in 1982-84. The new program has stressed graduality
and has taken much more care that certain supervisory roles of the public
institutions were strengthened. There are two characteristics that led to
the SAP's success and deserve mention.   The first was when the administra-
tion changed the implementation of economic policy after recognizing past
mistakes, and tried not to repeat earlier errors. This was important in
ensuring consistency in the new  program.   The second was that not every-
thing was wrong with the earlier liberalization program; moreover, that
many of its achievements induced long-run effects, (for example, the elas-
ticity of response of nontraditional exports and of import substitution).
The search for more efficient and less costly ways of production and the
like produced external economies that benefited the SAP when it started in



- 93 -
1985. This is important because it shows that both experiences--that of
1973-81 and of 1985-88--were closely linked.
3.74     Another reason  for  the  SAP's  overshooting  its  goals was that
strong positive external shocks helped the economy to grow faster and the
Government to get a better  grip  on  public finance.  This external effect
must not be underrated.   Nearly  402  of the cumulative GDP growth between
1984 and 1988 was attributable to better terms of trade (281) and lower
international interest rates (121).    When  the policies were designed the
future evolution of these variables was not foreseen exactly. That is why
results were much better than  expected.   The windfall from improved terms
of trade and more favorable interest rates explains almost totally the
unexpected success in GDP.
3.75     Although the IM  monitored  the  program  according to its tradi-
tional approach--with stress on a given number of economic variables-- the
Bank =o'tored a few additional variables but, in general, placed its em-
phasis on the implementation of structural measures rather than on their
results. This was so because in many cases the effect could not be direct-
ly associated with the policy instrument. It is clear both for the Govern-
ment and the two international financial institutions, that the program
requires careful attention to ensure sustainability, which at present im-
plies the need to consolidate the achievements in the long run. The long-
term goals necessarily include reinforcement of export diversification and
promotion of nontraditional exports to reach a significant share of total
exports. A second long-run objective should be to make fiscal revenues
more independent of the current account of the balance of payments.  Third,
efforts should be intensified to distribute the fruits of development to a
larger proportion of the population.    If benefits reach larger quantities
of people, there will be more public support for continuing the structural
adjustment efforts.
F. Final Remarks
3.76     The Chilean SAP,  with  its  significant  SAL  component, has been
successful in the sense that its application is associated with rapid
growth, diversification of exports, and strong reduction in unemployment to
less than 101 of the labor force, in a context of better resource alloca-
tion. In other words, the SAP has intended to improve efficiency in both
the private  and  public  sectors.    The  program  constitutes a different
approach to a free market economy that the previous one (begun in 1973),
which started with permissive liberalization of financial markets. The SAP
has been more gradual and prudently directed, toward an outward-oriented
economy with diversified exports.
3.77     The main virtue  of  the  program  since  1985 is its consistency.
Most of the measures correspond to a unified strategy and interact coher-
ently. The short-run policies, both fiscal and monetary, have been applied
in accordance with the SAP.    This feature becomes significant because the
proceeds of the SALs were used to finance imports (excluding those for
luxury and military consumption).  To  the  extent that the funds have not
been tied to particular projects, the risk of incompatible applications
arises. However, Chile's Goverument managed to maintain consistency in its



- 94 -
policy measures: they took into account both stabilizption and adjustment
requirements. There was a strong commitment of the administration to reha-
bilitate the economy in a context of liberalization, and the international
financing institutions concurred. Proof of that determination has been the
continuity in policy design and instrumentation since 1973. This created
sufficient credibility in the Government's aims and permitted the Bank to
disburse the three SALs with relatively few conditionalities.
3.78     The quick response of  the  economy  both  to the previous and the
present policies of liberalization has been accompanied lately by favorable
export performance and rates of interest abroad. When external pressures
on the balance of payments are released, the macroeconomic framework
becomes favorable to the implementation of stabilization policies. This
fact may obscure the degree of vulnerability that still persists in the
program. Future external shocks may not only alter the depth of the meas-
ures but also the articulation of the different policy instruments,
affecting the heretofore beneficial consistency of the program. The artic-
ulation of trade policy, public budgetary policy, and external debt manage-
meut i8 complex and crucial for medium-term success. Such articulation
will affect the allocation of resources for production and consumption.
3.79     The components of the SAP  are  not symmetric in this respect. The
financial policy looks more solid in the long run. Trade policy requires
attention in terms of long-term effects on the exporting and import-substi-
tuting sectors for future years, on the one hand, and in terms of budget
constraints, on the  other.    Conversely,  public sector management should
become more independent of primary exports and build a future structure of
revenues on structural grounds, and future export and import-substituting
activities  must  develop  in   correspondence with  the  reallocation  of
resources that is taking place in the economy.
3.80     Another point to bear in mind  is  survival of the program in the
medium-term in the face of conflicting pressures from different sectors.
Some measures carried out by the authorities particularly affect in the
social sectors.  The  long  consumption restraint required by stabilization
and adjustment policies should be given some pressure release provided that
economic performance allows  it.    The  internal opposition that different
sectors have toward  certain  measures  may  create  inconsistencies in the
programs.
3.81     Trade policies should specify  the. roles  of export subsidies and
import tariffs and their interaction in the process of resource allocation.
Public finances require a long-run program for revenues and expenditures to
make public investment compatible with noninflationary resources. Another
important issue is future. foreign  debt management.    The three areas of
economic policy are interlinked in the present case, and provisions must be
made to curb adverse effects derived from eventual external shocks. So
far, the SALs have been a good instrument to initiate the SAP--but it is
evident that adjustment cannot stop after disbursement of these three
loans. The objective of achieving a permanent modification in economic
structure requires sustained efforts in the same direction. Further insti-
tutional changes will have to be brought about, and that conception of
long-run policy may consolidate the process initiated by the SALs.



_ 95 -
IV. ISSUES AND LESSONS
4.01     This chapter is divided Into  three sections.  The first discusses
subjects on which there are doubts or opposing views  the timing of the
World Bank's structural lending in Chile, the public cost of financial
rehabilitation, some social issues, and whether there are prospects for
sustainable adjustment. The second section draws lessons from the Chilean
SAP and its principal SAL components: export promotion, public savings and
investment administration, financial rehabilitation, and measures affecting
social and other  sectors.    The  final  section  derives from the Chilean
experience some notes on the Bank's structural lending activity; specifi-
cally, on preparation, conditionality, monitoring, phasing out, relations
with member country authorities, efficiency and costs, and evaluation of
SALS.
4.02     Many of the lessons seemingly add no  new knowledge or by now may
seem truisms. That old ideas tend to block new ones biases evaluations in
this direction; natural curiosity and the allure of potential new discov-
eries work in another. From the point of view of future Bank performance,
however, the evidence of a case, is equally important for proposing change
and for lending support to established operations.
A. Issues
4.03     The first question to  pose  about  the  Bank's active presence in
Chile is: Was it timely?    Answering  the question has prompted debate on
the basis of both Chile's and the Bank's circumstances and operations.
The Bank's Timing in Chile
4.04     In Chile the foreign exchange  crisis  detonated in June 1982.  It
came as an almost total surprise. With some lags it carried major business
groups and banks into insolvency, which was only partially realized at the
time, and the situation became unsustainable by early 1983. There was no
clear diagnosis, only confusion. Within about a year, there was succession
of four finance ministers. Economic statistics for 1982 indicate the mag-
nitude of the crisis. On the demand side, while exports recovered 10.9g,
consumption declined 13.52 and investment plummeted 65.42. On the supply
side, GDP fell 14.31, and imports contracted 32.91. Savings also went down
sharply, by 34.72. Although the current balance of payments deficit shrank
from US$4.6 billion to US$2.4 billion, international reserves still dropped
by US$1.3 billion because of the draining of direct private investment and
medium- and long-term loans and because of capital flight. The public
sector, which had posted overall fiscal surpluses in previous years, showed
in 1982 a deficit of 3.42 of GDP because of increased expenditure, caused
in large part by the transformation of the social security system.
4.05     In early 1983 a new finance minister, Caceres, took charge of the
economy. At that time, still in the opening months of the Latin American
debt crisis, the foreign debt situation was the most pressing issue. The



- 96 -
Minister went straight into a fast shadow' program, which became effective
on agreement with the IMF, and yielded to the creditor banks' demand that
the state guarantee the foreign debts of the private sector. It is worth
remembering that the IMP's practice was then to vait for agreement with the
creditors banks before making effective its own agreement. Figures for
1983 cover a large part of  Caceres's  tenure.   On the demand side exports
nearly  stagnated  (+1.4X),   consumption   declined  slowly  (-5.8X),  but
investment sharply (+15.39).   On  the  supply side, GDP remained depressed
(-0.81) while Imports experienced  another,  smaller drop (-9.4Z).  Savings
almost levelled off  (-1.7I) in  its  fall.    The  deficit in the current
account  of  the  balance   of   payments   was   cut  to  US$1.1  billion.
International reserves decreased by only US$541 million because, although
direct private investment and medium- and long-term loans diminished, other
capital movements became  slightly  positive.    The overall fiscal deficit
declined to 0.61 of GDP, mainly  because of expenditure restraint under the
unfavorable conditions. Whereas the external sector and investmentIsavings
performance improved and showed promise, the prolonged recession and the
social situation (20? open unemployment plus 152 of the population in
emergency employment programs or EEPs) weakened the political stability of
the  Government.    There were  disagreements  between  the  political and
economic authorities, and the President changed the finace minister in
March 1984.
4.06     The new finance minister,  Escobar Cerda, followed an expansionary
line and moved to assist insolvent firms and banks, but the 6.32 GDP growth
(with an explosion of investment) 1984 proved unsustainable because of
pressures on the balance of payments, the deteriorating fiscal situation,
and the acceleration  of  inflation.    Despite  closer  contacts with (and
missions by) the Bank, under the prevailing conditions an agreement with
the IMP was impossible, and this precluded any Bank SAL. The finance
minister tried to invoke protectionism, both for fiscal and terms of trade
reasons, but faced stern resistance.    He was finally replaced in February
1985 by BUchi, whose tenure lasted throughout the period of SALs 1-111.
4.07     The new finance minister had firm  political support.  By then the
need for a medium-term economic strategy was widely recognized. BUchi
addressed the economic agenda decisively and got early support from the IMP
and the Bank. The latter had renewed its knowledge and contacts with Chile
and moved swiftly, providing important services in the design and early
implementation of the SAP.
4.08     Thus, a more  precise  rephrasing  of  the  question  is:  Would a
Bank's SAL in 1983--by supporting Caceres' effort and reducing some of the
costs of his policies--have extended his program, and thus avoided the more
expensive adjustment later? Or would such a SAL have been insufficient to
sustain politically the program, making more difficult the Bank's later
reappearance on the scene?
4.09     During the 1970s and early  1980s  Chile showed little interest in
the Bank's assistance or in formulating structural reforms; it's foreign
funding came in the form of  commercial banks' loans to the private sector,



-97-
which then were abundantly available.    Until early 1983 the Chilean eco-
nomic team was so convinced that its own program was flawless, that it
could not be motivated to  enter  into  a meaningful policy discussion with
the Bank. The Bank submitted important memoranda to the authorities show-
ing that their present policies were untenable, but such presentations were
either ignored or rejected. The result was loss of contacts at Bank Head-
quarters, little Bank activLty in the country, an emptied loan pipeline,
and reduced staff time devoted to Chile by the Banks from 1981 to 1983
only one loan officerteconomist was in charge of monitoring economic
developments in the country. In any case, with four finance ministers in
little over a year, it would have been most difficult to enter into a SAL
in those years. Chile was not seen as creditworthy; nevertheless, while
the IMF was active, the Bank was passive--and this not by its own choice-
-with  regard  to  Chile  at  the  time.   AMother  aspect  of  the pollcy
environment then was the overall handling of the Latin American debt
crisis. The initial stage WaS "muddling through," led by the UV1 but with
the creditor  banks' agenda  at  the  forefront.    Although  SALs were in
existence and operating elsewhere, the IHF's standard coupling of financial
assistance with stabilization in debt-ridden countries was not really
launched until 1985. Finally, Chile's political climate did not make the
country a favorite within the Bank's Executive Board. Even as late as 1988
there was resistance to any lending to Chile on the part of some Board
members.
4.10     Nonetheless, the Bank assisted Chile  routinely and at low lending
levels in the early 19809s a US$32.4 million water supply loan in 1980, a
US$36 million agricultural credit, and a US$42 million highway reconstruc-
tion loan in 1981, another US$128 million highway loan, and a US$56 million
agricultural loan, and a US$80 million housing loan in 1983. In 1982 the
Bank also warned about, and made an early estimate of, the losses that
would ensue from the financial crisis and rehabilitation. The only time
when conditions may have been amenable for the Bank's adjustment lending to
Chile was in 1983, under  the  Caceres tenure, after the country's Stand-by
agreement with the IMF.   One  subject  blockilg the advance, besides those
mentioned, was the lack of knowledge  about the situation of the financial
sector. Another was the time required to start a SAL from scratch without
any international political support for the country's Government.
4.11     The turnaround in the Bank's attitude toward Chile occurred during
1984, and the Bank reinforced its staff charged with following the Chilean
economy. By mid-1984 collaboration with the IMP began. Although there was
no updated country report, the need for a medium-term joint effort was
accepted. The key element was the submission to, and the recognition by, a
World Bank Senior Vice-President of reasons supporting renewal of the
Bank's involvement in Chile.    Conditions  for  that involvement were met:
monetary and fiscal efforts supplemented by a drive to promote saving,
exports and other objectives.   That Senior Vice-President remembers that a
memorandum from the Bank Regional Vice-President brought up the subject,
and that the consistency of Chilean efforts over time, th.e principle of not
letting politics cloud technical judgments, and the prospect of intriguing
and innovative policy actions gained his personal commitment to the
proposal, which has generally been regarded as crucial. le authorized



- 98 -
bypassing some usual steps, such as the preparation of a country program
paper. The time for the SAL came when Btchi became finance minister and
took decisive action In early 1985, although the loan was not signed until
in October 1985.   There were  several  causes  for the delay--among them,
establishment of the CSF and negotiations with creditor banks--but from the
beginning it was agreed in the Bank to act *as if* the package was going
ahead. The Bank also moved forward in guaranteeing a syndicated B-loan in
co-financing, with full support from the US government (a few months later,
the Baker plan was launched) and under pressure from creditor banks.
4.12     Thus, a further refinement of  the  timing question iss  Would the
Bank have given Chile a SAL in 1983 if preparations at the technical level
had been ready? Or would it have waited until 1985 in any case, since the
international financlal climate and the political sensibilities were not
yet unfavorable? There il no consensus on an answer. There were technical
and operational aspects to surmount that made it difficult for the Bank to
come up wlth a SAL for  the  program.    Some  of  them might be due to the
diminished presence of the Bank in Chile in the previous years, some to the
lack of initiative on Chile's side.
4.13     Nevertheless, a majority--not all--of  observers feel that had the
Bank come forward with a SAL in 1983, the economic and the political envi-
ronments vere such that the SAL would not have had good chances of turning
the tide. The delay served to demonstrate the need for financial assis-
tance and the dangers of reversing the liberalization policies. Thus this
delay--despite its high social costs--may have been a necessary condition
for gathering solid support behind a new stabilization-plus-adjustment
effort.   By  the  same  token,  it  is not  clear  that the international
financial community or the Bank's Board were ready in 1983 to approach
renewed relations with Chile--with its political climate, its debt-ridden
economy, and the lack of a clear diagnosis of its economic situation-
-decisively and with a medium-term view.    That view started to evolve in
late 1983 but  gained  full  acceptance  only  during  1984.   Against this
background of doubtful counterfactual histories, there is the conclusive
evidence of the successful SAL operation begun in 1985, when the situation
was ripe for action and both Chile and the Bank seized the opportunity.
The Impact of the SAP on Public Finance
4.14     The most significant costs of  the  SAP to the public sector, came
from State intervention to avoid generalized bankruptcies of banks and
enterprises during the crisis of 1982-83. Intervention included transform-
ing private into public foreign debts, privatizing state enterprises (not
related to the Bank's SALs), and converting foreign debt into domestic
debts or claims on domestic assets (equity), which produced benefits whose
magnitude is contested.   The whole  question has important distributional
implications.
4.15     Since the  problem  originated  long  before  the  1985 SAP, (with
effects that continued through it and will continue to be felt in the
future), it is necessary to trace its origins and significance. The same



_ 99 _
is necessary for some of the mechanisms used to solve the problem. There
is no quantitative basis to support an appraisal of these questions--only
sparse data exist, thus far  impossible  to  consolidate.   The aim at this
point is to illustrate the magnitudes involved and to bring out some of the
implications. This is a far cry from drawing lessons from the Bank's SAL
experience, which covered only the years 1985-88 and did not have a bearing
on all aspects of the problem.
Evolution of Public Sector Finance
4.16      From the beginning the SAP had to cope with the burden left by the
1981-83 economic and financial crisis.   The efforts at financial rehabili-
tation, reprivatization, and management of external debt had generated
heavy losses to the public sector, that had to be distributed over a long
period, implying strong constraints on the budget. This burden of losses
underlay the evolution of operational and quasifiscal deficits and the
grovth of public external indebtedness (Table 4.1).

4.17     The public sector deteriorated after  1981. and the deficit became
important in  1982.    Capital  accounts  looked more  stable than current
accounts, but the intervention to rehabilitate corporate and financial
institutions produced changes in the capital accounts. The same happened
with the process of reprivatisation.   After 1981 current revenues showed a
declining trend produced by a reduction in direct taz collection, a short-
run fall in external trade taxes, and a sharp reduction in revenues derived
from the pension system. The evolution of public sector finances was con-
nected with efforts to stabilize the financial market and the privatized
public enterprises. The former operations implied lmportant commitments of
funds by the Government and implicit or explicit subsidies to the private
sector. On the one hand these created a rapid and spontaneous response
from economic agents--thus, also growth and revenues--and on the other they
added new constraints to the public budget in the future, through lower net
revenues from enterprises.
Financial Rehabilitation
CiS8    Another aspect of public cost  of the economic policy is connected
with the burden of quasi-fiscal operations of the Central Bank. There are
some actions performed by the monetary authority that are fiscal rather
than monetary.  In fact, all  actions Involving transfers of resources from
the Central Bank to other economic agents, such as various subsidies, are
quasi-fiscal operations.
4.19     The efforts to rehabilitate  financial institutions started before
the SAP was designed. The outbreak of the financial crisis in 1981, fol-
lowed by the 1982-83 economic crisis, required rapid intervention by the
Government to prevent a generalized collapse of enterprises and financial
institutions. The costs of rescue operations exerted a growing pressure on
the public sector in those years.   By 1987 the rescue operations were com-
pleted. The SAP played a significant role in this completion by limLting
the quasi-fiscal costs of subsidies given to the private sector--not only
to reduce the fiscal deficit but also to improve the incentive mechanisms
by increasing competition. The Central Bank supported the private sector
by purchasing nonperforming portfolios from the weakened banks; reschedul-
ing domestic debt from productive sectors, and real State-warranted loans;
making subsidies through interest rate differentials and preferential
exchange rates. The purchase of nonperforming portfolios from the private
financial sector was equivalent to 281 of the Central Bank's total place-
ments of funds and to 182 of GDP in 1985. The SAP started with a strong
determination to end these subsidies.
4.20     It is not e%sy to have  precise data on the impact of quasi-fiscal
operations and the losses in public finance derived from the process of
rehabilitation.   Available  information  is  insufficient  to evaluate the
costs. Some studies, however, have elaborated on current data and have
provided some  estimates  from  different angles, which  at  least give a
picture of the public costs of the rehabilitation process. Table 4.2 shows
an estimation of the amount of subsidies incurred by the Central lank to
support the private sector.  Alternatively, there are also figures showing
the amount of financial sector losses.

The changes in external debt show that public debt increased more than
private debt, reflecting transformation of the second into the first, by
the widening of public guarantees on existing and new operations. The 1985
SAP succeeded in limiting quasi-fiscal costs and the burden of about US$6
billion on public finance derived mainly from previous operations. The
burden was an inescapable consequence of the economic and financial crisis
of 1982-83. Whether it could have been lighter is another, open question.
But at least the SALs supported a move away from these subsidies and inter-
ventions.
Poreian Private Debt Transfers and Guarantees
4.23     The public cost of  debt management has  been substantial in the
substitution of public or publicly guaranteed debt for nonguaranteed pri-
vate external liabilities.   This  process  began  after 1982 and was rein-
forced by the fact that the State remained the accepted borrower from
creditors abroad, and these creditors required further public guarantees on
their existing credits. The state takeover of private liabilities implied
an important transfer of resources to the private sector, in the form of
preforential exchange rates (preferential dollars) or direct assumption of
responsibility whenever private debtors could not pay. Data om the public
costs of these operations are not available here, but the evolution of
public and private debt gives a rough idea of the size of transfers of
private debt to the Government that had to be refinanced along with the
total external debt. In 1985 the latter amounted to 1.26 times annual GDP,
the financial services represented nearly .10 of GDP, and net transfers to
coammercial creditor banks amounted to 51 of GDP (Table 4.5).

had grown to 842. Conversely, private debt not guaranteed by the State
evolved from 422 to 162 in those years. Before 1983 the guaranteed private
debt was practically Inexistent. At the end of 1987 it exceeded US$3,200

4.25     Between 1983 and 1986  the private  unguaranteed debt declined by
US$6,868 million vhile guaranteed private debt increased by US$3,346
million. Public debt grew by US$7,044 million, vhereas the total external
debt increased US$3,522 million.   It  is  clear  that net private debt de-
clined US$3,522 million as well. Therefore, half of the increase in public
debt was to replace private debt. On the average, this replacement vent on
at a rate of US$880 million a year during 1983-86, nearly a SZ of annual
GDP. The proportion of publicly guaranteed private debt in total debt
undet State responsibility was 1S on the average in the period 1975-82. In
the four years between 1983 and 1986, that proportion rose to 17.32. In
1987 and 1988 the trend was altered by conversions of debt into equity and
by a larger reimbursement of public external debt. Financial rehabilita-
tion permitted firm and banks to comply with their foreign obligations,
thus relieving the State of its contingent liabilities. The Government
benefited from part of the discount obtained in debt-equity swaps.
Debt Conversion
4.26     In the course of application  of the SAP the authorities attempted
to accelerate the reduction of external debt by means of its conversion
into claims  on  domestic  assets  (Table  4.7).    Foreign  investors were
attracted to purchase Chilean dollar notes in the secondary markets and
convert them into claims on domestic assets at a discount, and this pro-
duced, particularly in 1988, Important reductions of the outstanding debt.
Given the existing discount on Chilean debt notes In International capital
markets, the  debt-equity  swaps and  other  operations  meant an implicit
profit to foreign investors in terms of an exchange rate of up to 442 in
1987. This Incentive privileged debt-equity swaps over investments of
fresh money.

4.27     In the  SAP  years  the  total  of  Ch4lean  dollar  notes swapped
amounted to one-third of the total outstanding external debt. Conversion
In that period was US$6.2 billion.   However, the external debt was reduced
by US$1.1 billion because of a rapid increase in credit from the IDB, the
IHY and the World  Bank,  among  others.    The capitalized debt made under
Chapter XIX of the compendium of rules for international exchange In Chile
and Decree Law 600 on foreign investment amounted to US$2.1 billion in the
period considered.

of swaps corresponding to Chapter  XIX  operations.   Chilean debtors got a
12? discount. That Chilean debtors captured ouly a part of the discount in
the secondary markets its reasonable, since creditors were not payed cash
in dollar8 but instead got damestic Chilean paper. This can be measured as
a benefit of converting old into new debt.

4.29     The inducement for foreign investors  to buy debt notes at between
552 and 702 of face value in international markets and obtain in Chile 882
of that face value generated a preference for investment financed by debt
notes, and debt-financed investment in part substituted for other forms of
direct investment. The substitution of interest payments for profit remit-
tances released pressure on the balance of payments in the short run, since
dividends could be remitted abroad after the fourth year of the operation
and capital revaluations could be performed only after the tenth year.
4.30     Part of debt-equity conversions  were associated with the privati-
zation of public enterprises or firms under the transitory intervention of
the Government after the  1982-83  economic  crisis.   Besides, part of the
debt conversions were performed with Chilean dollar notes that originated
in the refinancing of private debts transferred to the Government. But it
would be improper to deduct the gains from debt conversions from the losses
in reprivatizating firms or from the losses in guaranteeing foreign private
debts. Those gains are benefits from a different, new operation and are
not assignable by origin or destination.



- 108 -
4.31     An open issue is whether Chile extracted all the benefits it could
have from debt conversion schemes--for instance, If public bids would have
been used In all cases. At the same time, it can be argued that, Insofar
as the only criteria to decide between offers is the discount offered, the
effect on investment tends to diminish (Dagnino Pastore, 1988).
Privatization of Public Enterprises
4.32     This program, an important part of the SAP, was neither negotiated
with the lank nor a part of  the  Bank's  SALs In Chile. It is, however, an
important issue for public sector finance. It is difficult to evaluate the
public cost of subsidies granted in the program of reprivatization during
1985-88. But there is at least one estimate of the transfers from the
public to private sectors (Marcel, 1989).
4.33     The process of privatization evolved through four different stages
after 1973. The first period covers 1974-75, when 241 public enterprises
intervened by the Allende Government were returned to the private sector.
In 1975-79 the process entered a new stage in which 110 firms with State
majority ownership were  privatized.    The  amount  privatized was US$664
million. The third period covers 1985-86 and involved the privatization of
previously intervened firms  as  a  consequence  of  the economic crisis of
1982-83. These enterprises were transferred to the private sector through
'popular capitalism.' The fourth round started in 1986 and is concerned
with the reprivatization of assets owned by or transferred through CORPO,
the Chilean Development Corporation (Table 4.9). The revenue expected from
the program was officially estimated at US$1,500 million (Table 4.10).

4.34i    The process  of  privatization  for  the  public  sector meant the
availability of proceeds of the sale of assets but the future loss of divi-
dends generated by those  enterprises..   The  estimations of the Impact of
privatization of public firms on the medium-term fiscal cash flow were made
on the assumption that future profitability and reinvestment of profits
would be similar. had  the  firms  remained  public.    On  the basis of the
expected behavior of firms  and  the  privatization program, three alterna-
tives of future fiscal cash flow were computed. Each alternative was based
on different assumptions about the cycle of reinvestment of profits and
distribution of dividends.
4.35     Between 1986 and 1989, assets were  sold to the private sector in
an amount of US$1,100 million. The cash impact (Table 4.11) was positive
until 1989.i Later on, the  loss  of  dividends would create a reduction' of
about US$360 million with respect to the previous year. Thus, the public
sector would lose annual revenues in the form of dividends in an amount



-110-
varying between US$100 million and US$165 million t(1987 dollars) between
1990 and 1997. Therefore, the positive flow of the first two years follow-
ing the asset sales would be exceeded by the dividend stream foregone. It
was also estimated that the loss of future dividends plus the deficit aris-
ing from the pension system reform of 1980 and tax reductions implemented
beginning 1984 would create a reinforced negative impact on the public
budget of more than US$1,000 million in the first years, reaching US$2,500
million in 1991. This represents over a 10Z of GDP if GDP were to keep
expanding annually at a steady SZ.

4.36     To evaluate the subsidies given  in  the purchase of public assets
(Table 4.12), the economic value of public enterprises was calculated as
their present value given Marcel's own prediction of future profits.
However, this approach--which relies on subjective judgments--may tend to
overestimate the fiscal losses, by assuming that the firms would have been
just as profitable had they remained part of the public sector, as they
were after  divestiture.    The  firms  being privatized  included  in the
estimatiot represented 802 of the total assets involved in the program and
the totality of sales in 1986-87.

4.37     The estimates in Table 4.12 show  that in 1986 and 1987 the subsi-
dies in the sales of shares were 502 and 352 of the privatized values in
the respective years. If rescue of CAP shares in 1986 and capitalization
of debts of ENDESA are taken into account, the total transfers to the pri-
vate sector as a percentage of traded values was 582 in 1986 and 552 in
1987. In 1986-87 the total transfer amounted to US$600 million. Reduction
In the subsidy margins showed improvements in the implementation of priva-
tization. If the rate of subsidy prevailing in 1987 were assumed to remain
in 1988, the state would have experienced a fiscal loss of US$350 million.
The cost of privatization is relatively higher whenever the functioning of
public enterprises was efficient and not led by politicized elites. In
that case, the motivations for widening privatization were mainly ideologi-
cal. In general, companies after privatization distributed most of their
profits in order to attract new stock purchasers (Table 4.13). Investment
was mainly covered by borrowing.

4.38     From a macroeconomic point of view,  the privatisation of public
enterprises is a simple transfer of physical assets from the State to the
private sector. Prom the angle of the public budget, the sale of assets
produces the availability of funds at the moment of collection, which
Increases the cash balance of the public sector.41 This effect will be com-
pensated in the future by the profits stream forgone by the State. Pro-
vided that the  enterprise  sold was originally efficient, public finance
will experience a negative impact in the course of time. This is a vay to
consider the public cost of the transfer of assets. Those costs affect
only the public budget.   In  general,  profits  lost by the State will be
earned by the private agents.   These costs correspond to the privatization
of efficient firms that made profits when they were owned by the public
sector.
4.39     In addition, the  public  sector  incurs  the  cost  of subsidies
granted to the private sector -that sometimes were directed to induce pur_
chase of assets. However, there is no proof that in the absence of subsi-
dies the privatization schem   would not work, or vice versa.  Subsidies
derived from low sales prices and covered between one-third and one-half of
privatized values. The assets sold into private hands between 1986 and
1989 are estimated at US$1.1  billion.    So  subsidies varied from US$330
mllion to US$550 million.   The  incidence  on  the annual budget was very
important. Nevertheless, the public cost of the privatization should be,
like the success of the program, considered in the medium and long term.
The final outcome of these operations can only be checked against the
41 Revenues derived from privatizations should be accounted for under the
line, as part of the finance of the public sector. A reduction in
assets is analogous to an increase in liabilities.



- 113 -
actual evolution of the privatized enterprises in the future, taking
account of profitability, the operational performance of the enterprises,
and the conditions of the products they supply to the market. This will be
the most important element in evaluating the costs--that is, the social
costs--for the Chilean economy of privatization of public enterprises.
4.40     Therefore, the outcome of  the  program  should be analyzed in the
context of resource allocation.   If more competitive behavior is achieved,
the means to economic efficiency will be justified; so, initial costs will
be matched by important benefits later.   Conversely, if competition is not
improved and efficiency results are not spread through the market mechanism
or imperfections remain or increase the costs to the economy, the success
of the program will be threatened. The idea of the State's role as subsid-
iary goes together with the expectation that privatization will improve
resource allocation. It is still very early to pronounce a verdict. So
far, it is true the privatization efforts have imposed new constraints on
the public budget. In the short and medium term, privatization may reduce
the availability of resources in the public sector, but these effects by
themselves do not impose strong conditions on public expenditure in the
future.
Some Social Issues
4.41     The evolution of the social  situation  over the course of the SAP
is one of the most hotly debated aspects of the administration's policy.
It is impossible to understand it without looking into the whole period of
the previous Government. It must be noted, however, that the SAP covered
only the last four years of that Government, when some programs and the
situation changed. The lessons drawn from these four years are stated in
Section B, below.   The  Government's  policies  in the social sectors were
mainly restricted by austerity in public expenditure. The Government's
observation of the rules of economic stabilization and structural adjust-
ment imposed a severe burden on wage earners, particularly on the lower-
income segments of the population.   Whereas other Government measures were
instituted to alleviate extreme poverty, it appears that the lower middle-
income groups felt more fully the impact of adjustment.
4.42     A study (Rodriguez Grossi,  1985)  concluded  that in Chile 402 of
the higher-income households captured over 50? of social subsidies. Many
of the State activities were directed toward social security, which repre-
sented over 40X of social expenditure and generated transfers accruing to
higher-income groups. The study argued that many programs did not have
redistributive criteria and were not particularly progressive. While the
author noted that the magnitude of social expenditure--about 201 of GDP-
-was considerable, it was not directed progressively to the more needy
sectors of the population. There were also serious problems of demand and
supply with respect to health, education, housing and basic services, which
made more difficult  the  access  from  the  poor  strata.   In housing, he
further noted that the programs requiring savings from the beneficiaries
were not directed at the poorest.   An important number of workers were not
affiliated to a social security system--nearly 502 of the labor force.
Moreover, he found that the hospital infrastructure rendered better
attention to urban than to rural sectors.



- 114 -
4.43     Another study. made in 1987  and published a year later (Arellano,
1988), found that the average standard of living iA Chile--as in many other
countries--had deteriorated between  1981  and  1987.    GDP per capita had
fallen (Table 4.14).

4.44     The Impact of adjustment  on unemployment was strong, particularly
after the economic crisis of 1982-83.   In the course of the period 1984-87
the unemployment rate, including persons employed in EPs, fluctuated at
around 16Z of the labor force. The economic recovery brought a significant
reduction in the amount of open unemployment. This was gradually compen-
sated by a reduction In the emergency programs. The Government considered
it a successful move toward absorbing unemployed and underemployed factors
into productive activity. However, critics of the program contended that
the increase in employment took place predominantly in activities of low
productivity and with Incomes much  lower  than those during the preceding

4.46     The evolution of public  expenditure  directed  to fulfill social
policies is a matter of  controversy.   Cabezas (1988) explains the distor-
tions introduced by considering fiscal expenditure as an indicator of total
social expenditure. The former does not include flows financed with funds
of decentralized public entities, and there are problem in the accounting
of financial flows between central and decentralised sectors. In fact, he
argues, fiscal social expenditures represented only about balf of public
social expenditure.



- 117 _
4.47     According to recent  government  information on social expenditure
(net of social security contributions) In Chile in 1980-87, some conclu-
sions can be obtained regarding the composition and destination of subsi-
dies. Direct social expenditure consists mainly of subsidies granted by
the Government, sometimes in monetary form, to education, health, housing,
etc. Total direct social expenditure as a proportion of GDP was 8.8? in
1985; 7.671 in 1986, and 6.552 In  1987.    In this last year the ratio was
slightly below that corresponding to 1980.   In the three years of the SAP,
on the average 462 of direct social expenditure corresponded to subsidies
to education, 30X was in monetary subsidies (employment programs, dismiss-
als, assistance pension, maternal subsidies, etc. (132 was directed to
health, 9? to housing subsidies, and the remaining 2? covered other subsi-
dies. The rest of outlays, called *other social expenditure, corresponded
to General Pensions, Employment Programs (inputs for those programs and
intensive employment projects), and other social programs. Other social
expenditure amounted to 6.352 of GDP in 1985, 6.741 in 1986, and 6.6? in
1987. Both broad categories (direct and *other') carry the total fiscal
expenditure to 15.15? of GDP In 1985, 14.41? in 1986, and 13.05? in 1987.
4.48     The destination of direct social expenditure was mainly the lower-
income deciles of the population.   This was not uniform, however, and some
types of subsidies benefited middle-income groups. Generally speaking, 502
of the poorest population received 64? of subsidies granted in 1987. The
most progressive subsidies were those for health, (72X) and monetary subsi-
dies (71?), which were distributed in the give lower deciles. A different
picture was evident for housing, where 5O0 of the poorer population got
only 59? of the subsidies for that end; in education they received 57Z of
the funds granted.
4.49     The main conclusion is that, though aggregate fiscal social spend-
ing in 1988 was higher in absolute amount than that of 1970, overall fiscal
social spending has been concentrated heavily over the 19809 in two major
areas, social security and  *social welfare.'   Social security outlays in
1988 were fourfold higher in real (1988 dollar) term than that of 1970
(i.e., US$1,902 million versus US$512.6 million), while their relative
share of total fiscal social spending had almost doubled, i.e., 49.1 per-
cent compared to 26.9 percent.    The large increase in fiscal expenditures
on social security is the product of extensive reforms to the system which
include not only expansion of coverage to heretofore ineligible workers
but, most importantly, modification of its financing mode from pay-as-you-
go to individualized,  vested  accounts.    The  principal objective of the
latter reform was to strengthen the longer-term capacity of the system to
meet the pension requirements of a rapidly aging population. Its most
immediate effect, however, has been to strain significantly the public
budget.   Moreover,  though  government  outlays  for  social  security are
expected to diminish consistently year-to-year, until all obligations to
the beneficiaries covered ur' er the previous pension system are met, the
medium-term transition costi of the social security reforms will continue
to be substantial.    Further,  any  decision  to  increase social security



- 118 _
benefits prior to the Government's fulfilling all its obligations under the
old pension system obviously would increase the fiscal cost commensurately.51
4.50     In general, social  expenditure  has  indeed been directed to the
more needy sectors of the  population.   For example, special programs were
launched to alleviate the effects of unemployment (SEP subsidies--see Table
4.17). Whereas the impact of this type of expenditure was to alleviate the
hardships of some groups in extreme poverty, the next group up the income
ladder was made worse off.

4.51     In 1981 the functioning  of  the ISAPREs (Institutes of Preventive
Health) was established by  law.    Individuals  were allowed to choose the
institutions that would administer their contributions. Two options were
left to salaried workers, for payment of their 71 mandatory payroll
deductions to join either an ISAPRE or FONASA (the National Health Fund).

ISAPREs are primarily financial intermediaries acting between the user and
the supplier of the service. At present, ISAPREs are highly selective, and
the size of ISAPREs was nearly 112 of the total health system in mid-1988
(Aninat, Mendez y Asociados, 1989).    The  amount of the mandatory payroll
deduction has increased from 4Z of salaries in 1981 to 62 in 1983 and to 7X
commencing in 1985. The ISAPREs do not constitute a safety net. Notable
holes are the limited coverage for chronic diseases, and the plight of
those who lose their jobs and must stop their contributions.
4.52     Fiscal health spending in 1988 was  markedly below that of 1970 in
real absolute amount, as a share of GDP and in per capita.terms. At the
peak of fiscal health spending in 1973, outlays were US$554 million equiva-
lent (in 1988 dollars), or 22Z of Central Government social expenditures.
By 1981 the health sector's share in fiscal social spending had declined to
one-half (112), and by 1988 to just one-third (72) its 1973 level, albeit
in actual amount public health outlays increased slightly in 1988 dollars
between 1987 and 1988  (Table  4.18).    Moreover, per capita fiscal health
expenditures declined from US$56 in 1973 to US$21 in 1988 (both in 1988
dollars). Total fiscal health expenditures in 1987 of C$93.1 billion (in
1988 pesos) represented just 1.8 percent of GDP--a level far below that of
other countries at a similar level of economic development.6/ Further,
according to Ministry of Finance reporting to the International Monetary
Fund, more than 90Z of fiscal health recurrent budget allocations over the
past decade have been to hospitals despite emphasis on primary care. More-
over, the health investment budget has been negligible over much of the
1980s, contributing to deterioration of plant and equipment, particularly
in the hospital sector.7/

Has Chile Really Succeeded?
4.53     The  Bank's  structural  adjustment  lending  covered  four  years
1985-88;  the  first   year  was   devoted   to  discussion  and  advanced
implementation). Since then, the foreign finance component of the SALs has
been replaced by disbursements of World Bank loans totaling US$230 million
in 1989 and by similar amounts into the 1990., and by new sources, such as
the Bank of Japan.   The  question  is whether the three-year financial and
policy package of 1985 pulled together by Chile, the DMF, and the Bank has
effectively placed the country on the path of sustained growth while
keeping unemployment at acceptable rates and managing to service the
foreign debt.
4.54     Progress since the crisis  has been  eloquent.   GDP rose 7.4Z in
1988--the third consecutive year above 5.5Z--and in per capita terms has
reached the peak level of 1981.    Open urban unemployment was reduced from
202 in 1982 to 11.2Z In 1988 (excluding people in the Government's EEPs).
Although consumption is still 16.3Z below its 1982 peak, investment recov-
ered significantly, from 9.82 of GDP (probably less than the depreciation
of the capital stock) to 16.82 in 1988, supported by an explosive surge in
domestic savings, from 9.42 of GDP In 1982 to 242 now. Inflation was
brought down from over 202 during several years to 122 in 1988 (a threat of
the revival of high inflation is being handled currently). The trade bal-
ance moved from a deficit of US$2.7 billion In 1981 to a surplus of US$2.2
billion in 1988, with growth in noncopper exports outperforming growth in



- 121 -
copper exports every year, even after the sharp recovery of the copper
price In 1987. The terms of trade  are  5  off their decade peak of 100 in
1980 (the trough was at 76  in  1986).   The current account deficit of the
balance of payments was  still  US$200  million  in  1988, down from US$4.7
billion In 1981 and US$2.1  billion  in  1984.   The external debt declined
from a maximm of US$20.7  billion  in  1986  to  US$19.0 billion in 1988.
Consequently, all ratios Improveds
Worst (Tear)               1988
Debt/Export GNPS                    457 (1985)                 230

Interest/GDP                         12 (1985)                   8
The worst fiscal year was 1982, with a current deficit of 4.4Z of GDP; in
1988 it was a 7.61 surplus, mostly because of a decline In expenditures
from 30.71 to 24.51 of GDP.   The  overall  deficit of 4.41 of GDP became a
0.52 surplus. The Central Bank deficit has also lost significance lately.
Thus, at a first glance a picture of the Chilean economy today suggests
that It is well on the path to self-sustained growth--although in the long
run not as fast as at present--and able to remain in compliance with its
foreign obligations, although this will be difficult.
4.55     A closer look at figures and likely trends, however, tends to con-
dition such definite statements and to raise a few doubts. The qualifica-
tions stem from the facts that a significant part (412) of GDP expansion In
the past two years is attributable to the windfall from improved interna-
tional copper prices, that the excess of domestic savings over Investment
is still not enough to cover fully interest payments on the foreign debt
(particularly in the public sector) and, thus, that additional foreign
finance and renegotiations will be needed, as well as continued efforts to
mobilize domestic savings.   Other  questions  come from envisioning future
trends and developments. The Chilean economy remains vulnerable to nega-
tive external shocks--a recession in industrial countries, swings in copper
prices, higher international interest rates--and to the accumulation of
pressures in the political, social and economic fields. There are uncer-
tainties surrounding the political transition and its effects on economic
policies (for example, the new administration's policy toward labor unions)
and public expectations (for example, the level of investment). There are
social needs in health and education whose satisfaction has been long
delayed. Finally, there are lnvestments In economic infrastructure and
production--which will increase both because of past delays and because the
economy is moving from recovery to previous peaks and into expansion
beyond--that will require additional productive capacity. In particular,
and despite a very good performance in the management of Its foreign debt,
Chile still has one of the largest per capita debts (US$1,638), and recent
projections show external gaps of 82 (US$380 million) in 1990, 1,578



- 122 -
(US$1,963 million) In 1991, and 1,181 (US$1,652 million) in 1992,81 after
considering (so far) net Bank disbursements of US$159,91 US$80 million, and
US$35 million, respectively. While the challenge to fill the gaps is less,
both in size and in difficulty, than that during the early SAP years, the
outcome still depends on continuous good economic performance--in turn
affected by external events.   Besides,  part  of the stabilization and ad-
justment effort was financed by postponement of public investment and
social outlays. While there are no exactly defined time limits to these
delays, several estimates place the necessary transfers of resources to
those ends in the order of 52 of GDP and 'coming due' in the near 19909.
Against this assessment of the current situation, as it resulted from a
medium-term evolution, the first  question  ist   What are the foreseeable
future changes -- external and internal--that will significantly affect
Chile's economic development?
The External Front
4.56     At a more specific level  than  the  future  rate of growth of the
world economy and the prospective behavior of international interest rates,
the price of copper has passed its peak and many experts predict another
decline, maybe to historical  levels.    This  would mean  a  loss of the
windfall terms of trade improvement that catapulted Chile's CDP growth from
the 5.52 per year that was programed to the rate of over 72 experienced in
1988--still a fast, but normal growth, with less slack in the external and
fiscal sectors. Tnitially, the fiscal impact of lower copper prices would
be cushioned by resorting to the Bank-supported CSF, which has accumulated
US$800 million (after deducting withdrawals for debt buybacks). This
course of events would not allow for further buybacks from this source of
funds. in any case, the boom is finishing for the leading fruit exports,
although it still goes on  for  some  specialties and new products (such as
salmon and berries, which are becoming hot export prospects). As an oil
importer, Chile is sensitive  to  International  fuel prices, but there has
been an expansion in Chile's productive capacity in copper and gold, as
well as in forestry, roads, and efficient import-substituting activities-
-an outcome of the SALs export policies.  To the earlier co ments about the
need to refinance the foreign debt when amortizations begin in 1991, it
must be added that some limitations on dividend remittances will be over at
roughly the same time.
4.57     Several Chilean observers point out that the country's good behav-
ior in servicing the foreign debt will be penalized by its exclusion from
the Brady plan. Excluded or not, some spillovers from the plan--if imple-
mented--will favor Chile's debt renegotiations.   Although the net flow of
funds with the IMP is already negative for the country, the World Bank will
be sustaining a positive net flow at a lower but still significant level in
1990, and there are already some signs of increasing commercial and project
lending and new foreign investment.
81 The figures reflects high (low) copper price hypotheses.
91 Including the recently approved Financial Sector Adjustment Loan.



- 123 _
The Internal Front
4.58     The dominant issue is the  impact  of the recent change of Govern-
ment on economic policy and public  expectations.  The relevant question is
how economic policy would change.   Opinions are divided on this point, but
the mainstream view  is  that  the  practical  continuity of liberalization
policies since the 19708 and their success in more recent years (faster
growth, much lower unemployment, lower inflation) have given them wide-
spread support and created an anti-inflationary conscience. The reasons
are:
*    Positive perception  of  the  policies  by  the population,
despite some shortcomings (notably, in the social sector);
*    Job generation through  increased  export activities, which
gives the export-promoting policies political strength;
*    The  existence  of  powerful   interest  groups  that  have
developed (and live) by rules of the current economic
policies;
*    Competition from abroad  in  the  opened economy which sets
limits to the monopoli$tic pressures of business and labor.
4.59     So, many observers  expect  that  the  economic  policy of the new
Government will change  little  in  its main  lines.    It will be more a
question of different emphasis.
4.60     Other issues on the domestic front are as follows:
*    With political opening  and more  employment, labor unions
will become stronger and push for more power or cause re-
ductions in productivity in very sensitive areas: in the
ports, for instance, the key to the export success. As
seen by experts, there are countervailing forces to the
union's increased power.
*    Some relatively  easy  productivity gains--from corrections
of inefficiencies, more intensive capacity utilization, and
the use of comparative advantage may have been exhausted.
*    In general the foregoing points to slower growth or stepped
up investment, but capacity bottlenecks may be specific,
not   general--often   ICORs   (incremental  capital-output
ratios) are volatile and misleading indicators- (for ex-
ample, in ports and in some, but not all, of the highway
network), and the liberalization of the economy may permit
easy spotting of new *niches, for comparative advantage and
enhanced productivity.   In particular. Chilean businessmen
have mastered export skills, which are transferable with
relative ease from one trade to another.



- 124 -
*    There may be pressures  to  raise the investment ratio from
172 to 232. which vill  require  domestic savings of about
282 of GDP--not a very  realistic prospect.  An alternative
solution is more  investment  or  financing from abroad, or
some sacrifices in the rate of growth of investment or in
the recovery of social expenditures.
*    The situation of the public  sector may not be as solid as
it seems. Central Bank statements do not portray the full
extent  of  contingent  liabilities.    The  burden  of the
revised social security system on the budget already is
diminlshing, but will remain significant into the 1990s.
There will be stronger demands for social expenditure--for
health, education, housing, and investment in infrastruc-
ture. And the reduction in the VAT by 202 has narrowed the
room for maneuver.    Yet  the  privatization of previously
public firms and services in several fields, requiring
large investment in the medium-term, has released the
Government from those conmitments in the future, and the
authorities are consequently free to choose a different tax
package.
4.61     Even for many stable, efficient, growing economies, sustainability
is a daily battle. For Chile, it will be tested by the political economy
of a change in Government, by pressures to shift the emphasis to the social
sectors, by the move from recovery to expansion, and by the likely end of
the terms of trade windfall of the last two years. With all the uncertain-
ties about how the political economy will evolve, the continuity and recent
success of the adjustment policies has created a consensus around their
main tenets and strong interest in preserving them. How much the domestic
power structure--for instance, the role of labor unions--will change is not
yet clear. If the new political economy does not break with the main path
of the adjustment, in 1990 Chile will face its current problems--lesser in
magnitude than those of the 1980s--from a stronger internal position and
with favorable momentum. Although the external sector windfall of the last
years is likely to  end  and  some pressures  &re  likely to heighten, the
country is better equipped to handle them now than it was before. Many
observers think that Chile is more likely to return to a slower--but still
healthy--rate of  growth  (say  42  per  year)  than  to  retreat  from the
adjustment process.
B. Lessons from the Chilean SAP and the Bank's SAL Comonent
The Overall SAP
4.62     To draw lessons  from  experience  is a different--more demanding,
yet complementary--exercise from that of evaluating performance. To apply
such lessons in other places and moments requires considerations of repli-
cability. Lessons from experience are also learned experientially, and,
with all the richness of life, that mode of learning defies easy schemes
and classifications. The transfer of experience, in turn, requires sound
judgment and careful adaptation.



125 -
Backsround
4.63     What are the lessons  that  the  Chilean  SAP  can teach about the
background and rationale for such programs? Chile had followed liberaliza-
tion policies since the mid-1970s with fair success until the end of the
decade, and then they came to an abrupt and very painful end in early 1982.
4.64     There are several lessons  from  this  experience, which have been
reviewed at length elsewhere.101 Among the main ones are: the appreciation
of the exchange rate, permitted by the faster reaction of funds than goods
movements to the opening of the economy, and fueled by the recycling of
petrodollars; the persistence of unemployment, in part explained by wage
indexation; and very high real rates of interest, reflecting insufficient
fiscal policies, losing battles against exchange rate expectations, or
both. An important misconception at the time--accepted by the IMF, among
others--was that private foreign debt  did not  count.   It did count, and
Chile was hit hard when credits were not renewed and the exchange transfers
became a macroeconomic problem that detonated the exchange crisis. Another
mistake was to assume that firms would adapt rapidly to high real interest
rates--the rates were above the average return on assets--by selling and
reducing assets and cancelling debt. In fact, many of them were not able,
under heavy import competition, to generate funds. When interest rates are
that high, it is difficult to sell assets--impossible at normal prices--and
to cut down receivables. A specific, very costly error was the confusion
between market freedom and lack of prudential rules in the financial
sector, where funds of other people  are  managed.   This led to collusion
between banks and enterprises and facilitated their later collapse. But
despite its dramatic end, the first liberalization period left some posi-
tive effects in the economy:   relatively well-ordered fiscal management, a
productive structure more prepared for the opening of the economy, and
respect for price stability. It is widely recognized that the liberaliza-
tion policy of the 1970. had a significant influence In the positive supply
response to the SAP in the mid-1980s. It also led the productive sector to
reject a neoprotectionist assault in late 1984.
4.65     The 1982-84 crisis--with all  its suffering and confusion--and the
transition from it--unsustainable relief and a failed attempt to revive
protectionism--forced a sounder analysis of the situation. The lessons
were the need for a medium-term view to avoid the successive failures of
short-term approaches and the need to sustain confidence. The tough deci-
sions of setting and keeping a high exchange rate, in order to raise public
savings significantly and swiftly and to let real wages fall, both allowed
and were sustained by foreign finance (the concept of a "policy and finan-
cial package*).  The medium-ternm horizon  implied working with multiyear
projections that helped to trace the consequences of alternative courses of
action--for instance, the Mexican and Brazilian models of adjustment were
considered unsuitable.
101 See, for example, Ardito Barletta, Blejer, and Landau (1984).



- 126 -
DesiRn
4.66     What are the lessons  taught by  the  Chilean  SAP as regards the
design of such programs?    The  crucial concepts  of  the SAP were that a
medium-term approach was a necessary condition for short-term stabiliza-
tion, and that the policy and financial elements of the package, by mutual
reinforcement, provided the consistency and continuity of the program. In
the SAP, the BUchi economic team blended the approach of the 1970s--a free,
open, privately operated economy with fiscal equilibrium -- with a realis-
tic handling of specific problems    reasonable price bands for traditional
crops; some protection; freedom with SJIP-enforced prudential rules for
banking; moderate, stable export stmuli; wage restrictions to avoid unem-
ployment; debt conversion  schemes.    The moral  of  the story commends a
sound, simple conceptual framework and its adaptation to particular aspects
of reality.
4.67     Chile "owned" the programs    the  design was Chilean. The INF,
active since the early 1980s, provided support and policy advice. The
World Bank helped in warning about, estimating the size of, and discussing
the financial crisis with the SBIP as early as the second part of 1982. By
mid-1984 the Bank was active and preparing for future action in Chile.
'hen the program was conceived Chile had the worst ratio of foreign debt to
GDP in Latin America, with a current balance of payments deficit of US$2.1
billion. The open urban unemployment rate was 18.5t. Investment was 13.6Z
of GDP and domestic savings 12.6Z--not enough to finance investment; less
adequate to service the foreign debt. The overall fiscal deficit was, at a
maximum, 4.4Z of GDP--mostly because of near-peak expenditures of 30.71 of
GDP. The current fiscal surplus was far from being able to finance public
investments of 6.41 of GDP. The program design was clears
*   A high and stable  exchange  rate, to stimulate exports and
improve the trade balance;
*   A fiscal shock--initially on both supply and demand sides,
later with emphasis on expenditure restraint--to raise
public savings;
*   Au effort to  rehabilitate  the  financial  sector, thus to
increase private savings.
Macroeconomic projections made use of the Central Bank's econometric model.
This macro framework was directed at tackling the key vulnerabilities of
the Chilean economys   to  generate  the  external  and internal transfers
necessary to avoid the 'snowballing' of the foreign debt in the medium run
and to generate confidence in foreign and domestic lenders and investors so
as to fill the external gap and return the economy to the growth path.
This *core' was supplemented with mesures to develop noncopper exports-
-among them, the CSF--and efficient imrort substitution; later with volun-
tary debt reduction mechanisms; with other measures to reorder public
current expenditures and investment (later, to privatize public enterprise
activities); and with institutional changes in the financial markets.



-.127 -
Again, the SAP vas Chile's; if it incorporated ideas or suggestions from
the IMP or the Bank, in major respects this was done through coincidence or
persuasion and not by imposition.
4.68     MAother corollary is  a  clear  separation  of the essential--non-
negotiable--elements of the program, just  described, from other aspects or
effects. The latter (for example, some social effects) were either ignored
or palliated. Another element was that the SAP adopted fairly conservative
estimates of exogenous factors (for example, of copper prices--although in
this case conservatism may have not been purposeful). When it produced its
SAP, the Government projected the price of copper, which differed from
estimates made by the Bank (Table 4.19). Bank estimates were readjusted,
as the table shows. This prudent approach made the SAP look initially too
restrictive, but it worked well and left some leeway to respond to require-
ments later on.
