4.69     The SAP could not have  been  the same without the Bank's support.
It lent it consistency (through its participation at the design stage) and
continuity (since its financial engagement had catalytic effects) helped
build favorable medium-term expectations in the economic operators and
reinforced the position of the  economic  team.   In other words, the Bank
helped to "sell' the SAP abroad and bought time to see the program's first
results. Key Bank contributions to the SAP design were the CSF, discussed
elsewhere--which afterward proved useful in discouraging expenditure temp-
tations--an end to the continuous drain of public funds, rehabilitation of
the financial system, and careful screening of investment projects. Priva-
tization of state enterprises--except intervened banks--was never a Bank
condition for its SALs. The economic team, however, was keen on the sub-
ject, and in recent years launched the *popular capitalism" initiative to
advance it.



- 128 -
4.70     On a conventional checklist  of  SAP  design elements, Chile would
show an almost perfect score.    It has improved efficiency through export
expansion, import liberalization, free prices (except in some traditional
crops), and better public expenditure and management of state enterprises.
It has also raised public and private savings significantly. The success
that has been achieved adds one more example to the global case record
confirming the correct orientation of this adjustment approach.
Implementation
4.71     What are the lessons  taught  by  the  Chilean  SAP as regards the
implementation of such programs? Implementation of the SAP in Chile admits
division into two stagess the crucial year 1985, during which the finan-
cial package and some aspects of the policy package were not yet final (SAL
I was signed on October 10, 1985), and the three following years, under
successive, uninterrupted Bank SALs. The genesis of the SAP can be traced
to 1984s3, when it was evident that the foreign debt situation and capital
flight would doom the economic approach and team. In late 1984 and early
1985 the guiding concepts and main lines of a new policy design became
clear to informed opinion in Chile, the IMF, and the Bank, which had rein-
forced its Chilean staff.    Chile  had  effective  negotiators and a tacit
agreement was reached with the Bank by March 1985, permitting the authori-
ties to behave "as if' the financial and policy package had been signed.
The difficult points in the deal with the Bank, which delayed signing, were
the CSF, the extent of financial rehabilitation, and relations with credi-
tor banks. The Bank's guarantee for co-financing a B highway loan suffered
a two-month delay, and initially the IMF and the Bank advised Chile against
providing State guarantees for private debts to foreign banks. The delays
in the agreement with the Bank increased some dangers: the agreement with
commercial banks was postponed until August 1985, the terms of trade
deteriorated, and  the  external  debt  situation  became  unsustainable in
April-May 1985. But while these delays weakened Chile's external position,
they also gave the creditor banks cause for reflection about the conse-
quences of not coming forward in support of the package. In any case, the
Chilean authorities gave up and agreed to guarantee private debts to
foreign banks (there were precedents in 1983). The subsequent position of
the IMF and the Bank  on  this  subject  seems  to have been one of "hands
off"--maybe reflecting the ambiguous position of some important board
members of its Executive Board.    In  the  second  part of 1985, while the
government decisively addressed its economic agenda, the Bank was active in
gathering funds for the financial side of the package. After signature of
SAL I, Bank staff accompanied Chilean officials on a tour of several
countries to close the financing of the 1985-87 needs from private sources.
4.72     A reading of the  experience  underlines  the importance of strong
political support and cohesion behind a SAP--that the program be 'owned' by
the country and clearly defined--and of the quality of the economic team
and technocracy.  This poses  the  question  of  the replicability of the
Chilean experiences Would it have been possible under a different polit-
ical economy? There can be as many counterfactual histories as ideological
angles. History provides examples of successes and failures under both
democratic and authoritarian regimes.



- 129 -
4.73     Another reading is the  Important  catalytic  effect of the Bank's
intervention, not only because of its financial comitment but also because
of the concurrence it showed with regard to the program's success. This
tends to indicate that it is wise for the hank to bide its time so as to
intervene only when the Government has shown its determination--while
accowpanying the Government's efforts during the process of design and
early implementation.
4.74     The program's implementation  after  the  first  SAL agreement was
excellent--helped especially since 1987, by positive external shocks (a
fall in oil prices, a rise in copper prices, lower interest rates and
dollar  prices,  world  economic  recovery).   The  administration  showed
restraint at several junctures, stopping an inflationary revival in 1987
and restraining a 1988  boom.   Also  important  in 1986-88 was the supply
response of the private sector, probably rooted in the liberalization
experience of the 1970s--not only with respect to entrepreneurial atti-
tudes, but also to the institutional setup. For instance, the efficiency
of Chilean ports, essential for the export drive, was in large measure the
result of previous policy decisions with regard to the workers' union. The
main lessons here are the importance of abiding by the right priorities and
keeping an eye on not failing because of early successes.
4.75     On a standard  checklist  of  implementation elements, the Chilean
performance would score very  hight    there were no serious difficulties;
lnstitutional reforms were smooth and, after sound studies, rapidly per-
formed by an efficient technocracy.    The Government commitment was excep-
tional, the external environment favorable and economic policies correct,
with some questions about nonprice policies. The Bank provided good advice
on the whole, although there was  some  dispute about the extent of social
and financial measures and  about  proper  disbursements.   Here again, the
evident success validates the usual criteria for the implementation of
SAPs.
Impact
4.76     What are the lessons taught by the Chilean eAP about the impact of
such programs? In Table 4.20 the performance of Chile under the SAP in
terms of the usual goals is compared with previous periods, with Latin
American countries as a whole, and with the program's goals. The SAP pro-
voked sharp, favorable trend reversals in all major objectives, with marked
improvements within the  period.    Chile  also outperformed Latin American
countries as a whole in the majority of variables and complied with practi-
cally all program goals--many of  them  In  excess.   The more specific SAL
actions were mainly qualitatives   directed toward export promotion, flnan-
cial rehabilitation, and the increase of savings and investment. The tar-
geted variables, in contrast, corresponded to the Government program and
IMF participation.

4.77     Almost all of the  overshooting  of the  GDP  growth goal can be
attributed to the direct effects of unanticipated positive external shocks:
mainly, favorable terms of trade and  lover  interest rates. To the extent
that these shocks had indirect effects, it can be argued that performance
without them--in the last 2 years of the SAP--would have fallen short of
th6 goals. But this reasoning ignores the policy reactions of the Govern-
ment and poses conditions different from those that happened. In any case,
a reading of the experience is that the model underlying the projections
portrays adequately the principal interactions of the Chilean economy.
4.78     The evolution of the social situation  under the SAP is a disputed
matter, as already discussed. The lesson to draw here is that the line of
not trading-off the main thrust of the SAP against short-run social
improvements paid off in  terms  of economic  growth and adjustment. Non-
essential concessions to palliate extreme or focused problems enhanced the
political sustainability with little  economic  cost.   An exercise in the
social costs of slower growth and vulnerability might sustain this reading.
4.79     On a conventional checklist of SAP  impact, Chile achieved an
almost perfect score. The trade-off between the resource balance and GDP
growth improved dramatically:

The supply response to the SAP was fast, strong, and sustained. The fore-

good design and implementation  produced very  good  results.   It is also
apparent that the windfall success  from external shocks changed little, if
any, of the Government's detenrmnation to pursue adjustment policies.

Sustainability
4.80     lhat lessons can the Chilean  SAP  teach with respect to the sus-
tainability of such programs? Clearly, there is not yet sufficient histor-
ical perspective to be sure about them. Chile is still undergoing profound
political transition and digesting the phasing out of intensive quick-
disbursing financial backing from international institutions. Besides,
there is always the question of the extent to which particular experiences
are replicable in reference to sustainability, a subject so much Imbedded
in the political economy of the individual country and in the external
environment. With all these caveats, some observations may be ventured.
One is that structural adjustment, as a form of social change, initially
faces resistance--the fear of costs to some is more perceptible than the
incentive of benefits to others--but as it proceeds, if convenient, gains
adepts and loses opponents.   In  this  sense, continuity of the program in
itself enhances  sustainability.    Chile  benefited  from  a long previous
liberalization period, which  is  not  a  replicable  experience.   But the
importance of continuity advocates, for the Bank, the practice of providing
successive SAL support during three- to five-year spans.
4.81     A second lesson  regarding  sustainability  is  the convenience of
creating, during economic upswings, reserves for downswings. That was the
experience with the CSF. But this same example provides another interest-
ing insight. Since it accumulated more funds, it was decided to spare some
of the CSF for foreign debt  buybacks.    That is, part of the reserves for
downswings was devoted to help solving longer-term problems. It can be
argued that this action tends to drive the adjustment policies in the
medium run.
4.82     Another observation is  that  features  of  the adjustment program
that involved the population directly--such as private pension funds and
"popular capitalisma--created broad support, thus increasing the chances of
survival of the respective policy elements.
4.83     One more perception is that,  although  at the time of launching a
SAP profound policy  changes  (decisive  Government  actions) and financial
support (the Bank's) play the leading roles, in terms of future sustain-
ability the function of institutional reforms  (more sticky and not so easy
to reverse) acquire  importance.    In  Chile  the  CSF, export credits and
insurance  systems,  and  the  organization   of ports  (this  reform  was
undertaken before the 1985 SAP) are established institutional changes that
supported the export  drive  and  diversification.    The  same is valid in
reference to savings mobilization.
4.84     On a standard checklist of elements favoring the sustainability of
SAPs, Chile scores high.    The  adjustment  policy  did not fall victim to
sociopolitical overoptimism and followed closely its original design, with
some adequate changes in emphasis and few concessions. There were signifi-
cant reforms and a widely recognized ability in economic management.



- 132 -
The SAL Component
4.85     This section presents the lessons taught by the Chilean experience
in reference to the main aspects of the SAP supported by the Bank's SALs.
Export Promotion
4.86     Chile's opening of  its  economy  included  the  promotion of non-
copper exports, the expansion of copper output cspacity, and efficient
import substitution. The erphasis was on the first--to expand and divers-
ify exports and reduce vulnerability to external shocks. Main lessons from
this effort were the overall importance of a reallstic and stable exchange
rate, whose credibility is a prerequisite for economic agents to invest in
an intrinsically risky activity, such as exporting. In fact, if weighted
by trade, the exchange rate kept depreciating until 1988, because of the
fall of the US dollar against other currencies.
4.87     Another observation is the importance  of having exports achieve a
critical mass that confers on interest groups--business, labor, etc.--suf-
ficient political power to avoid advocating economic policies that penalize
exports to the point of interrupting their normal flow. A good example was
the healthy reaction against a call in 1984 to reinstitute protectionism.
4.88     The role of  the  institutional  infrastructure  is also relevant.
Chile is a country distant from major world markets. However, it was able
to modify the operation of its ports so that, with very little investment,
they now count among the most efficient in the world, with their lower
operating costs compensating a large portion of Chile's higher freights
bill. But other institutional developments--in export insurance, credit and
promotion--were also of significant help.
4.89     Another angle is to look at export promotion "as a whole' - as the
program indeed did.    In  this  context,  the  focusing  of the PIP toward
exports as a first priority seems to have been very effectives high short-
term "new agriculture' programs, export-serving roads, fishing developments
received funds (instead of, for instance, long-maturity, capital-intensive,
massive transportation projects).
4.90     The Chilean experience,  however,  may not  be easily replicable,
because of country-specific factors favoring Chilet
* The supply response was facilitated by the existence of a
fluid market for agricultural land--a late result of the
agrarian reform of-the 1960s--and by the previous adjust-
ment of industry to Chile's exit from the Andean Pact and
trade liberalization in the 1970s.
* The high level of education that Chile vas able to main-
tain, a significant element in export development.
* The favorable development of the markets for Chilean
fruits, part of which were planted in the late 1960s and
early 1970s.



- 133 -
4.91     on a  conventional  checklist  of export  promotion elements, the
Chilean experience ranks wells   the  opening  of export markets kept pace
with the rate of export growth; the sequencing was correct, although non-
tariff restrictions had been removed almost totally before the SAP; and the
assistance given to the adjustment of foreign trade was approached as a
whole, with support for institutional, labor, infrastructural, and exten-
sion aspects. Initially there were high interest rates, and the fiscal
shock had to absorb same revenue losses from reduced import tariff rates.
As trade grew, revenue effects reversed, and then came the copper boom.
The notable improvement in the copper price, however, opened the gate to
expansionary pressures which the Government was able to resist. In the
end, the terms of trade windfall permitted the Government to have more
reserves and less debt. Significant import substitution projects are still
under way, and this area is likely to draw increasing attention in the
long-term consolidation of the structural adjustment.
The CSF
4.92     A very important Bank initiative  incorporated  in the SAP was the
CSF. It had to be negotiated with the Chilean authorities, who felt that
to single out one product for such treatment could lead to distortions in
price signals and resource allocation. But copper represented over half of
the country's exports, and the example of 'Dutch disease' was as clear as
any.  In fact, there are  two problems  here.   The first is that, leaving
aside the wide fluctuations characteristic of copper prices, such speciali-
zation has as its counterpart the backwardnesi of other export activities
and a very high vulnerability to external shocks or technological changes.
The second is that violent cycles in world copper prices also cause violent
expansions and recessions of domestic activity and prices, which in turn
not only trigger social tensions and economic losses, but also blur price
signals for long-term resource allocation.
4.93     In Chile, as seen above, the  first  problem was tackled by a pro-
gram to promote noncopper exports and the second through the CSP. A fea-
ture that is not always present in countries with similar problems is that
801 of Chile's copper exports were in the hands of a state enterprise. If
the variable taxisubsidy implied by the CSF has been applied to private
firms, there would have been incentives to underinvoice sales abroad in
order to reap fiscal benefits.  There are alternative instruments to steri-
lize cycles in export earnings--such  as  the placement of Government bonds
denominated in foreign currency, which of course puts the burden of stabil-
ization on the entire economic commuilty instead of the industry in ques-
tion--but these work not through the goods' price mechanism but through
asset prices. The key lesson is not to let foreseeable sources of fluctua-
tion interfere with stabilization and adjustment goals. The CSF success-
fully complied with this rule and is a commendable mechanism for similar
cases.



_ 134 -
Public Sector Management
4.94     A feature of the Chilean  fiscal situation not replicated in other
large debtor countries is that it had surpluses in the years immediately
before the 1982 crisis, and the public debt was accordingly low. Even at
its worst, in 1984, the fiscal deficit did not reach 5Z of GDP, and it
already included the cost of transforming the social security system (which
will peak in the mid-1990s and then will gradually fade away). At the
start of the SAP, the main problems In public finances were, in addition to
the social security change, the costs of the E8Ps and the guarantees of
private foreign debts. The public cost of the financial rehabilitation is
not reflected so much in the fiscal account, as in Central Bank statements.
The main actions were current expenditure restraint (at the beginning,
lower real wages and no readjustment of old pensions) and better tax
collection (initially, of the VAT and, later, due to expanded activity).
Additional sources of relief were the sales of State enterprises which--in
contrast to the experience of other countries--were profitable. Thus, the
effects on the public cash flow were positive initially but negative after-
ward, when the surpluses of those companies stopped feeding the Treasury.
Thus, the improvement of public savings in recent years may underestimate
the effort expended. In the longer run, the sales of those enterprises
also transfer to the private sector the financing of the capacity expan-
sions required. About half of the proceeds from the sales were devoted to
reduce the fiscal deficit. Another, later relief to public sector finance
came from the rise in copper prices.
4.95     The main lessons from Chile's public sector management are differ-
ent expressions of centuries-old  sound  criteria:   austerity and sensible
administration. Additional sources of revenues have been reviewed, but the
backbone of the success was tough decisions on the expenditure side, some
firmly proposed and backed by  the  Bank.    The administration was able to
institute cuts in wages and pensionss to contain EEPs and health expendi-
tures, under severe pressure, by formulating creative and focused programs;
to streamline the PIP by increasing the return and quality of investments-
-for example, in agriculture and water supply; and, finally, to resist
demands of expenditure expansion during the copper bonanza.
4.96     As in other components of the SAL, Chile performed well when meas-
ured against a conventional checklist of public administration elements.
Decentralization was  organized,  though  not  synchronized.    The fiscal
effects of high interest rates and devaluations were compensated by those
of trade liberalization improvement of the tax system. Solutions to fiscal
problems did not suffer from delays. A debated aspect, already treated, is
whether social expenditure could have been kept at a higher level. But at
what alternative cost?  Here a recurrent theme again appears:  How replica-
ble is the experience of resisting pressures especially on the social bud-
get--in such a difficult time, under different political conditions? This
subject has already been addressed.



- 135 -
Financial Rehabilitation
4.97     To draw lessons from  the financial rehabilitation policies within
Chile' SAP requires a  conceptual  framework.    An  approach is to build a
typology of crises affecting business activities as a whole in developing
countriest
Balance of Payments Crisis           Domestic Financial Crisis
No          Yes
No                           Type I     Type III
Yes                           Type II     Type IV
4.98     Situation I is normal:  no  crisis  of any kind.  In situation II
there is no across-the-board crisis  internally.   Once the Government has
run out of credit and reserves, it can either establish exchange controls
or let the exchange rate explode.    In the first case, when their foreign
debts become due, enterprises will pay--or consign in deposit--to the
Central Bank the value of the debt in local currency at the official
exchange rate and thus transfer to the State the obligation with the for-
eign creditor. The State takes the exchange risk. In the second case,
the value of the debt in local currency will explode accordingly, and-
-given the basic assumption--there will also be a domestic financial
crisis. The situation moves to Type IV.
4.99     In situation III  there  is  an across-the-board internal crisis,
but the balance of payments is in good shape. Enterprises are unable to
comply either with their local or with their foreign debts. Since there
is a generalized crisis, the Government will bail out the firm with pub-
lic funds, trying to spread the monetary effects over time. This means
refinancing or reducing business debts, or any combination of both. The
problem for the authorities is how to make foreign creditors share (pro-
portionally) in the cost of  the  bail  out.   The bargaining situation is
that of a creditors' club without formal rules of the game. Since the
foreign creditors know that the State has a large stake in the game, they
will refuse to pay any cost--as is generally done by small creditors. So
it is up to the Government to pose a credible threat--that it is not dis-
posed to pay all the tab itself--so as to get some contributions from
foreign creditors. The situation is much more complicated because there
are thousands of firms, there is a race against time, and the State cannot
act discretionally.
4.100    In situation IV the two  crisis occur simultaneously.  Firms will
probably try to pay foreign rather than local debts, not only because they
are more difficult to reschedule, but because they will prefer to get rid
of the exchange risk.   But  if  the  crisis  is deep enough they will pay
neither--in which case the situation resembles that of type III. The
corollary is that widespread foreign debts of private firms do not commit
the Government as long as the economy is in a normal situation (type I).



- 136 -
If there is a balance of payments crisis, the Government bears the
exchange risk. If there is a domestic financial crisis, the State bears
the brunt of the cost, and it is unlikely that it will succeed in trans-
ferring to foreign creditors  their  proportional  share.    It is in this
light that the Chilean experience can be probed for future guidance.
4.101    The lesson here is not  to  confuse market freedomlll (which says
that banks will compete in rates to gather funds but will also be prudent
in their loans so as to attract depositors with low-risk portfolios) with
lack of prudential regulations  (which  sets  limits to risk concentration
and self-credits).  Setting  aside delinquent behavior, many entrepreneurs
and bankers will feel  tempted  to  pay  high interest rates in supposedly
high-yield ventures, thus  incurring  risk concentration and self-credits,
which are very vulnerable to  crisis.    The high rates lure depositors to
them, thus channeling a  larger  share  of  savings to risky destinations.
Furthermore, in the initial stage of the crisis, the banks with such port-
folios pay ever higher rates of interest in order to keep pumping money
into those uncreditworthy firms in order to avoid their bankruptcy which
would uncover their own  insolvency.    The prudential rules and financial
surveillance established in Chile under the SAP, with the Bank's assis-
tance, are now considered a model for other countries in similar difficul-
ties.
4.102    What are the benefits  and  costs  of Government intervention and
subsidies in that situation? The benefits are to avoid the collapse from
macroeconomic mismanagement, an ensuing financial crisis of companies and
banks otherwise economically viable in the long term, and the adomino
effect' of their bankruptcies.   Government measures in this regard should
not include bailing out businesses and financial institutions that are
insolvent because of their own poor management or long-run unviability.
The benefits cannot be completely separated from the particular rehabili-
tation mechanisms employed and their  costs.   Insofar as there are inter-
dependencies, the application of the methodology of cost effectiveness to
select the course of action is impaired.
4.103    The costs to the country  of  preventing  the collapse are of two
kinds. The first is that foreign creditors are likely to share less of
the burden in the case of rehabilitation than in the case of bankruptcies-
-but under the circumstances the total burden should be larger for bank-
ruptcies. The second relates to the consequences of how the costs of
rehabilitation incurred by the public sector are imposed on the community
as a whole and how they are financed. That the rehabilitation is largely
an internal transfer should not obscure the fact that it is costly in
terms of resources for those who pay, and it is reflected either in less
investment or consumption (or both), directly or through higher interest
rates or inflation (or both).   All these are difficult concepts to define
111 A costly curiosityt the 1002 deposit insurance survived through the
liberalization  period,  eliminating  for   the  depositors  the  risk
differentials between banks--thus favoring those which paid high
rates--and held the State liable in the case of banks' default.



-137-
and measure. Nevertheless, the cost of State guarantees for private for-
eign debts furnish a first indication of costs of the first kind, and the
amount of public subsidies to firms and banks give an idea of costs of the
second kind.
4.104    The moral here is  to  adopt  rehabilitation measures directed at
achieving the benefits as defined above, and thus to limit the costs.
This often calls for making the "stock' losses explicit rather than
extending *flow' losses, and then designing how these losses would be
supported and financed, trying to avoid their inflationary impact; to
strike a balance between present and future burden distribution; and to
distort price signals and resource allocation as little as possible.
Awareness of the effects of the balance of payments crisis on the domestic
financial situation began in 1982--with the Bank's help--as did some meas-
ures of relief. But the full extent of the effects became manifest only
in 1983 when, over bickering with the finance minister, international
banks discontinued their credits to Chile, and the main business groups
and local banks were intervened  by  the  Government.   The Bank played no
role beyond providing technical assistance at this stage.
4.105    Initially the salvage was expensive  and inefficient, since 1985
less so, and later on, the costs diminished under Bank pressure. Many
Chilean authorities tend still to underplay the consequences of the bail
out, just because they were able to avoid its inflationary impact by fis-
cal improvements and indebtedness.   In  this  sense, they charge that the
Bank did not fully understand the financial side of the rescue, it proba-
bly misjudged the size of the problem, and so it was unduly worried.
Chilean officials are also very keen at underscoring the public cost of
the bail out--not always with solid reasons--maybe as a reaction to criti-
cism. So the Government involvement had two faces. The first is that the
public cost was very high--some estimates place it at 502 of a year's GDP
(cumulative total until it disappears); others in a lower range. The
second is that the burden was allocated and financed with relatively
little macroeconomic--especially  monetary--disturbances,   partly  as  a
result of good overall economic policy (that is, strong Inducements for
saving).
4.106    The costs of rehabilitation  and  reconstruction of the financial
sector included the losses for liquidation of financial entities; the
difference between the disbursements to capitalize banks and the reim-
bursements from the sales of those stocks, in present value terms; and the
assistance to debtors (portfolio purchase and sale-back swap agreements,
subsidies on dollar deposits, preferential exchange rates, and so on).
The exchange rate losses from the transfer of private foreign debt were
sizable--albeit an internal, not external cost.   The purchase by the pub-
lic of intervened financial entities was heavily subsidized--at prices
discounted up to 30Z, with interest-free (but indexed) financing by CORFO,
and with tax incentives.   The  AFPs took shares, but recapitalization had
multiple objectivess to do it without estatization", a usual temptation,
to make old stockholders bear a cost (though not full), and to avoid con-
centration of ownership. Portfolio swaps amounted to 3.5 times the equity



- 138 -
of the financial institutions and the subsidy was about 2Z a year. Some
entities have not been able to reduce this portfolio at an acceptable
rate, and the situation may snowball.
4.107    The Bank accompanied  the  process  by  shifting  the emphasis in
successive SAL*t In SAL I it was to stabilize the financial sector; SAL
II aimed to stop the Government's involvement in the sector; and SAL III
sought to capitalize financial entities (their 1987188 profits were satis-
factory) and to develop capital  markets.    The Bsank played an especially
useful role in providing guidance and support in Chile's developing an
exemplary system of prudential rules and surveillance--with the help of
outside consultants--penalizing nonproductive risks and trying to improve
the management of State banks, even though these were the most affected by
the crisis.
4.108    Each financial crisis  is  a unique,  at least partly unexpected
experience (otherwise it would not have occurred). Thus, the replicabil-
ity of the lessons is  limited.    Observations  from the Chilean case are
that the initial panic and disorder tend to induce inefficient and costly
salvage measures, and that it is best--as soon as the situation is stabi-
lized--to make the stock losses and stop the bail-out as fast as possible.
The benefits of a rapid recapitalization, without statization, of the
system in terms of confidence, fluidity, and saving-investment behavior
will justify incorporating a subsidy component in the stock sales to the
public.
Social and Other Sectors
4.109    Assessing the social costs of the 1985 Chilean SAP is a different
exercise than appraising such costs of liberalization-stabilization-
adjustment for the whole period of the previous Government, starting in
1973. Assessment of the social costs of the 1985 SAP can resort to com-
parisons with either counterfactual histories (which could be successful
or unsuccessful policy approaches tried in other Latin American countries)
or with the situation in 1984, with cases of failure In the design, lmple-
mentation, or management of economic adjustment programs.
4.110    In comparative terms, Chile  has traditionally shown higher rela-
tive social expenditure thas the rest of Latin America.  This may reflect,
at least partially, that Chile is not a federal nation; consequently, the
budget also includes claims that in federal countries would pertain to the
states. It is difficult to draw conclusions from intercountry compari-
sons, since the public coverage of social services varies from one to
another. During the early years of the SAL period, the behavior of public
social expenditure was as shown in Table 4.15. Some social services were
increasingly provided by the private sector (for example, the growth of
ISAPREs). As for unemployment, the policy was to reduce the expenditure
in EEPs, keeping pace with the evolution of economic recovery and employ-
ment while streamlining the EEPs to gain efficiency. The most controver-
sial of the social sectors has been health.



- 139 -
4.111    The key charge of  the  critics  is that health expenditures were
too low; that this led to a marked deterioration of social services and
the population's well-being; that special programs to palliate these
effects did not change the overall trend in the sector, were devoted to
easy and superficial goals, and created many situations of inequitytl2t
and that reductions in the VAT and progressive taxes could have been
replaced by more social  expenditures.   Past Government officials argued,
in contrast, that the containment of current expenditures was compensated
by  better  management  and   by   programs   focused  on  acute  problems
(malnutrition  and maternal-child  health),  and  that  social indicators
showed consistent progress in 1985-87.   Greater social expenditures would
certainly  have  modified  the main  thrust  of  economic  policy.    One
observation is that a large share of containment in social expenditures
came from a fall in real wages of civil servants, including doctors and
teachers. Another is that focused efforts to alleviate narrowly defined-
-often  pressing--problems  are  no   substitute  for  long-term  sectoral
solutions and in some cases create imbalances that increase tensions
later.13/ In both cases, the corollary is that the effort extracted from
and the palliatives given to, the social sectors, although congruent with
the economic policy approach, are short-term measures that will need to be
re-examined. Future projections should therefore take into account the
need  for  some   further   adjustments--for  example,  include  selective
corrections of real wages and increased attention to heretofore neglected
current expenditures such  as maintenance.    Several estimates place the
public cost of these adjustments in the range of 32 to 4Z of GDP. Another
corollary is that the adjustment years should be used to design and begin
structural changes in the social sectors, too, and that the phasing out of
multilateral structural adjustment assistance may contemplate some loans
for the social sectors from the Bank or other official sources.
4.112    On a conventional checklist  of  social  components of a SAP, the
Chilean program added only temporary--in its last year if any--strains.
The poverty impact of the economic crisis had reached its peak in 1982.
Calorie intake, however, behaved as shown in Table 4.21. The most impor-
tant special programs, the EEPs, were launched before the SAP, and un-
employment had already passed 'ts  peak.   Targeted programs shoved varied
success, with nutrition programs ranking among the highest. On the whole,
such efforts were oriented toward the poor.
12/ For instance, financing housing for better-off groups.
13/ For example, lower-middle-class residents, who do not qualify for some
social programs, are worse off than poor people who do qualify.

C. Lessons from the Bank's Structural Lending Activity
4.113    This section presents the  lessons  offered by the Chilean experi-
ence with reference to the Bank's related SAL activity.
Preparation
4.114    What are the lessons  from  the  Chilean  case with respect to the
Bank's preparation for a  sequence  of  SALs?    In  the late 1970s and the
beginning of the 1980s developing countries found--through their public and
private sectors--abundant and easy financing from international commercial
banks that imposed no ties and conditions on their loans (as the IMP and
the Bank do). So Chile, like some other countries, showed little interest
in the Bank loans. At the sae time, some members of the Bank's Executive
Board showed concern  about political  conditions  in  Chile.   The Bank's
activity in the country dwindled, and the Bank reduced the number of its
staff assigned to Chile. During the 1982-83 crisis the IMP was active in
Chile while the Bank was passive--with a few exceptions. An important one
was the Bank's assistance in assessing the magnitude of the financial
crisis. It was only by mid-1984 that a change in attitude on the part of
the Chilean authorities, a Bank Vice-President's go-ahead, and-new Bank
staff in charge of Bank activities In Chile returned the Bank to active
involvement in the country. In the meantime, for three years Chile muddled
through one of its worst economic crises, and a possible way out--in 1983,
under Finance Minister Caceres--lapsed.
4.115    A consequence of this  lapse  is  that, when  the Bank did become
active again, the main lines of the Government's economlc policy had
already been devised. The Bank made important additional advisory contri-
butions, but the basic design of measures to ameliorate the crisis already
existed. The point is of little practical importance, since there were
fundamental overlaps of agreement, but it could be significant elsewhere.
The lesson here is the convenience that can be gained by the Bank's keeping
a more permanent, ongoing dialogue--if no physical presence--with a country
even in periods of relatively little lank activity.141 Of course there are
141 For cost reasons, this may not be possible for very small countries.



- 141 -
cost considerations, but it is a question of quality rather than quantity
of Bsank staff involvement.   Coordination  and division of labor with other
international financial institutions could reduce sone of the costs to the
Bank, but there are limits  to  this.    Not only do the specific, mandated
functions of the international institutions dlffer, but some courterpoint
in following the evolution of a country's economy is beneficials it pro-
vides more than one point of view (for example, the IMP was overoptimistic
about its monetary approach in Chile in the late 19709), it avoids feedback
effects of small groups, and it fosters more realistic discussion of the
issues. Perhaps the most important lesson is that loss of contact with
developments In a country during periods of low Bank activity there places
the Bank in a passive position, devoid of empirically-rooted initiative.
4.116    Once the Bank became  active  again  in  Chile, preparation of the
SALs complied with practically all the major yardsticks. With the excep-
tion of some aspects of SAL II, discussed later, there was economic and
sector work and policy dialogue for all three main components of the SALs.
There was a fairly explicit, comprehensive macroeconomic frame.  Stabiliza-
tion and adjustment policies were integrated. There was no excessive opti-
mism. either about external conditions or burden sharing by the interna-
tional commercial banks. The SAP bet initially on intensity; as it turned
out, it was a well-calculated political risk because the program proved to
be realistic and flexible.  Host  important,  the country 'owned' the pro-
gram. Not only did Chile have the initiative, it also had implemented a
large (key) part of the adjustment program by the time SAL I was signed.
Meanwhile, Chile was supported by the IMF's SF? and the Bank encouraged the
authorities to proceed 'as if' the SAL and the financial package were
forthcoming. This arrangement was mutually reinforcing and worked success-
fully, and here there is a second lesson to draw.
Conditionality
4.117    Before the current SAP,  Chile underwent a decade-long liberaliza-
tion period, interrupted by the  1982  crisis.   When the Bank approved its
SAL I in 1985, the IMP had already approved an EEF for Chile. These two
facts explain why the macroeconomic conditions for Bank assistance to Chile
were less strong than for  other  countries.   Changes in emphasis from one
SAL to another were reflected in these conditions.
4.118    In general, SAL I  is  considered  as  the  one with the strongest
design. Well-focused on the larger SAP, its emphasis was on two key varia-
bles, savings and exports, in the transition to a more open economy with
better resource mobilization and allocation.    SAL TI placed the accent on
financial rehabilitation. On this point there are different perceptions.
Several observers think that the Bank's worries over the public cost of
financial rehabilitation were  exaggerated.    Most  others, however, think
that the Banks insistence on stopping Government subsidies to the finan-
cial sector was right and timely--and anticipated reconstruction of the
system, even if it caused some penuries in the short term. SAL III was



- 142 -
different. The targets were more dispersed and less focused on supporting
the core objectives of the SAP.  At the suggestion of the Bank's Executive
Board and some staff members--probably out of caution for the social
unsustainability of the SAP--various social programs were incorporated.
They were designed hurriedly by the Chileans when the Bank could not be
dissuaded from its insistence, some on the basis of hurried missions.
Although some of these programs were successful, they were not integrated
in long-term sectoral strategies.   They also quantitatively increased, but
qualitatively reduced, conditionality.    More  important, they lowered the
institutional level of administration and involved different jurisdictions,
making control of program execution very complex.
4.119    This turn in the last  SAL  is the subject of widespread criticism
in Chile. The high-level economic authorities would have preferred mainte-
nance of SAL I's emphases--the groundwork had been laid--rather than pro-
liferation of  new  targets  and  some  diffusion  of  original objectives.
Lower-level officials in the social sectors concerned, while conceding some
benefits, resented interventions and ad hoc activities outside their
general strategies.  Government critics point  out the number of targets as
an example  of politically motivated  foreign  interference  in internal,
microeconomic affairs and emphasize the inequities resulting from isolated,
single-sector efforts.
4.120    A first observation  here  is  the  convenience  of having a small
number of policy  conditions--four  to  six  as  a  rule of thumb--that are
related to the core objectives  of  the  SAP and whose compliance should be
strictly controlled.   Other  less  essential  aspects  could be subject to
program monitoring unrelated to  tranche  releases.    Even if unrelated to
tranche releases, it is advisable to limit to a minimum the number of con-
ditions imposed on subjects that do not strictly pertain to the key adjust-
ments. A corollary is to select, as far as possible, results rather than
actions as subjects of control. This has to do with the first observation,
since it is more common to find a target requiring several actions than an
action achieving several targets.    The  corollary, however, should not be
absolute because there are considerations (for example, sustainability)
that call more for institutional developments than for observable results,
and results can be the effects of causes other than the actions undertaken.
Another lesson that seems obvious--to make 'adequate' modifications in the
policy conditions of successive SALs--ls not so obvious if posed in slight-
ly different form. That is, to give equal weight to the following two
rules: revise the policy conditions if they are 'wrong," and not to revise
the conditions if they are right." Es post analysis of behavior is likely
to show that the first rule weighs more heavily than the second.15/ Last
but not least, another lesson is the importance of careful preparatory
151 Clearly this is a far cry from the suggestion that SALs should be given
for longer terms with more tranching, which makes the targets rigid and
the cost of noncompliance higher for the country through its effect on
expectations.



- 143 -
economic and sector work, in close contact between the country and the
Bank, as a basis for the determination of reasonable conditionality, vhich
then becomes the shared objectives.
Monitorins
4.121    Chile's compliance with policy conditions  was so good and evident
that it left little role for monitoring. On the one hand, there were a few
cases of near compliance: adjustment slower than expected in 1985 (SAL I
was signed in October); fiscal results-worse than expected, by 0.12 of GDP,
in 1986 and--as public savings--0.32 in 1987; an inflation slippage of 6.5S
in 1987--more a concern of the IMP--corrected to 0.3X in 1988. On the
other hand, there were notable overcompliances, as in growth of the exter-
nal gap and in the current account of the balance of payments. Thus, the
monitoring function was more one of the Bank's alerting the Chilean author-
ities about some developments and speeding up the relevant actions. But
the Bank's monitoring performed another important function: the mid-term
missions, on whose reports tranche releases were decided, also set the
basic elements for the design of the next SAL.
4.122    A message is, then, that six-month  tranching leads to a timing of
missions that enables monitoring to perform both the function of control
and of setting the basic elements of the next SAL.16/ A corollary is that
monitoring should not be too narrowly focused on compliance, but also
should consider relating the SAL to the overall progress of the larger SAP.
Phasing Out
4.123    The Tables 4.22 and 4.23  present a recent (April 1989) projection
of the capital account of the Chilean balance of payments and also portray
the phasing out of adjustment assistance from international financial
institutions. The figures in the tables show the usual pattern of rapid
disengagement by the IMP and slower decline of net flows from the Bank,
which keeps up the level of gross disbursements while amortizations
increase. These disbursements will come from the financial market and from
second Bank housing, road sector, and industrial finance loans, which are
sectorally focused continuations of SAL aspects. The Chilean authorities
would have prepared a fourth SAL, but the Bank regarded this gradual phase-
out as reasonable. The first observation is the convenience of setting a
time limit--three to five years at most as a rule of thumb--for successive
SALs and a prospective phase-out program from the outset (lest the support
become a substitute for domestic effort and prolong the debt situation, and
also to avoid the accumulation of  amortizations).   A second lesson is the
need for a fairly gradual phase-out scheme in countries whose export earn-
ings are volatile and dependent on external factors.
16S SAL III had twelve-month tranching, but no further SAL was foreseen.

4.124    A final matter related with  the  phasing  out, is the feeling of
some Chilean observers that the country's good behavior may not have been
worth the effort and pain, since other countries that did not undertake as
stringent an adjustment effort are currently able to repurchase their debts
on terms cheaper than those esacted from Chile and may be bailed out by the
Brady plan. This line of thought requires careful consideration, because
first, early adjustment sacrifices were supported financially by interna-
tional institutions, and these institutions have received compensation from
the sustained progress since 1986; second, the market--although belatedly-
-will reward that behavior in the future;17/ and third, because the Brad;
plan will have positive spillovers in Chile too. Still, a corollary is to
171 This point  should  not  be  overemphasized.    Resumption of voluntary
lending has been slow to corn. Future lending decisions depend on past
debtor behavior but on other factors as well.



- 145 _
avoid, as far as possible, the introductiou of perverse incentives--those
which encourage countries not to adjust--in the design of solutions to the
debt problem, a well known principle,
The Bank's Relations with Country Authorities
4.125    In the whole process of  a  SAL sequence, the Bank's relations are
with the debtor country, with other international institutions (especially
the IMF) and with other lenders.    The relations with Chile were not close
until the 1982 crisis and became active only in the second part of 1984.
The reason for this may lie in, among other causes, a misunderstanding
originating in the Bank's draft economic report of 1981, which apparently
sounded a warning of impending difficulties.  Another Bank document of that
tine, in contrast, predicted  sustained  prosperity.    It seems clear that
there was a mutual lack of strong interest on both sides; perhaps due to
other priorities and, perhaps,  political  aspects.   This state of affairs
was reflected in weak--or no--Chilean informational efforts in the Bank and
in little Bank staff effort devoted to Chile. There is no indication of a
strong interaction between Bank staff and Chllean authorities until a small
revival of activity in late 1982 and then the turning point in 1984.
Although there are explanations for this discontinuity, it did not go
unnoticed in Chile that during the worst of the crisis the IMF was present
and active and the Bank was not.18/
4.126    Since then, Bank relations with  Chile  have been close and excel-
lent, and the level and performance of both the Chilean authorities and
negotiators and Bank's staff have been very good. In addition, there is in
Chile a widespread appreciation of the open attitude of Bank staff, (shown
by their contacts with different groups) in and outside the Government. It
has been mentioned by several Chilean officials, however, that the (high)
rate of job rotation of Bank personnel, which implies rebuilding contacts
anew,  and  (frequent)  organizational   changes,  requiring  learning  new
procedures, take their toll  on  smooth  relations.   In a few nonessential
areas, it was also considered by the Chilean authorities that some Bank
missions were not always necessary or well-prepared.
4.127    Relations  between  Chile  and  other  international  institutions
-especially the IMP--were cordial and close, allowing collaboration, cross-
fertilization, and coordination of respective functions and, avoiding con-
flicts and, to a large extent, duplication of efforts. This was evident in
the homogenization of conditionality.    In  some cases, upon Bank evidence
the IMF modified the values of some targets. Finally, relations with cred-
itors was neither close nor distant.   The Bank could not persuade the com-
mercial banks in 1985 to waive their requirement of a state guarantee of
foreign private debt, and negotiation of the B-loan cofinancing was diffi-
cult but successful. Later that year, the Bank played a significant role
181 Of  course  there was  some  assistance  and  some  lending,  but not
involvement in an essential sense.



- 146 -
in helping the Chilean authorities--through a demanding tour- to secure
from commercial banks the additional funding needed to complete the finan-
cial package. It also regularly provided supportive statements for finan-
cial packages involving debt renegotiations.
4.128    There are few lessons  to  draw here.  Some of the possibilities
involve choosing a locus in the trade-off between closeness of relations
and cost, a subject addressed under the next heading, below. Relations
with the IVW are defined with reference to overall objectives and the
charter of that institution. A final reflection is that resolution of the
developing country debt problem--to which solution the SALs contributed--is
likely to require that some  institution play a leading role--including the
shepherding of coamercial banks.    But  in  that case, such an institution
would need enhanced resources, capabilities, and political support to carry
out its mandate.
Questions of Cost and Efficiency
4.129    In drawing lessons from the Chilean SAP and the Bank's SALs, ques-
tions arise about efficiency and cost aspects of the Bank's operations.
Some of these are specific to the SALs, and others are more general.
4.130    The first centers around SAL  III,  which In contrast to the other
SALe--in which there was a coincidence of views--a compromise had to be
reached to include several studies and programs in the social and other
sectors. The perception in Chile is that, at the Bank's insistence, SAL
III delved too deeply into sectoral microeconomics, with a resultant sharp
deterioration of cost efficiency.    At  first the sectoral authorities re-
sisted the intervention, which they felt abrogated sectoral strategies in
favor of disconnected, alien  programs.    Complexities were encountered in
dealing with and coordinating different jurisdictions and multiple agencies
of lower hierarchy. All these factors cause delays, poor organization, and
difficulties in control. The conclusion is that as far as possible, SALs
should stay macroeconomic and general and should avoid becoming enmeshed in
minute administrative details. Such concentrated microeconomic involvement
is not in their essence. Nor is it the essence (or expertise) of the job
of the Government officials charged with complying with such conditions.
Sector loans are a better vehicle to achieve this type of objective.
4.131    The more general questions refer  to the losses in cost efficiency
from organizational changes and personnel rotation in the Bank, to the
adequacy of the Bank's institutional memory and to the supposedly excessive
frequency or (superficiality) of the Bank's missions.191 Clearly, these
subjects exceed the scope of this audit evaluation and must be consigned to
the Bank's review of  its  overall  organizational  policy.   Perhaps it is
useful simply to observe that, in many cases, the increase in efficiency
justifies the cost of adequately briefing Bank mission members.
19/ These last comments refer particularly to missions related to the
peripheral components of the SALs.



- 147 -
Evaluation
4.132    It is in the nature  of  evaluations  to  try to encompass all the
richness of reality with an open mind, without preconceived and rigid for-
mats and beyond mere registration of compliance with targets. But it helps
an evaluator to know, as explicitly as possible, the simplified 'model' of
reality that underlay the program and targets pursued. The Chilean SAP
made use of the econometric model of the Central Bank of Chile, and some
exercises were also done using the RMSM of the World Bank.
4.133.  Another important element is institutional memory.  It is not only
the question of the  availability  of  records--and for the matter persons-
-but also of the easy and speed of its retrieval--or interviews--and of
their selectivity and classification.   In  the  case of Chile, this exper-
ience has been satisfactory, given the logistical support of OED. All key
documents were made accessibles   initiating memos, back-to-office reports,
decision meeting minutes, President's reports, letters of development
policy, Board discussions, loan agreements, progress reports, country
program and strategy reports, and files on the whole SAL process. A large
majority of the Bank staff who participated in SAL decision making were
also made available. The briefing in Washington before the audit mission
to Chile was extremely useful.
4.134.   A final element is the methodology of evaluation, whether there is
a blueprint or an index of topics for the exercise. Although evaluation
does not lend itself easily to typologies, the Bank has developed useful
guidelines. As a contribution to this end, for those parts of the exercise
amenable to rigorous analytical treatment, 'A Methodology for Quantitative
Ex Post Evaluations' is included as a Technical Appendix to this report.
Programs and evaluations, however, can rely only partially on rigorous
modeling exercises of this kind--and in any case, it is difficult to choose
the 'best' among such models, if  this  exists.   To have available the few
computer runs that were taken into account in designing a program or set-
ting its targets, however, would be useful for understanding the rationale
applied and the options faced in the design stages to grasp the 'black
box' of interrelations mong variables, as well as among objectives and
policies.
4.135    Considerations of efficiency and cost--also a term of reference in
evaluations--point in the same  direction.   Institutional memory is satis-
factory and certainly quite good in comparative terms. The cost of obtain-
ing additional improvements are probably prohibitively high in terms of
scarce institutional resources--staff time.   The marginal benefits seem to
be modest, given the limits of the absorptive capacity of both the Bank's
staff and outside evaluators. A practice of the utmost importance is con-
tact with a large and varied sample of persons in the recipient country.
These individuals bring points of view, empirical data, and variety of
perceptions and value judgments impossible to grasp or imagine from outside
the country context. It is this vivid experience that adds content and
conviction to the evaluation, an exercise always in danger of remaining
formal or superficial.

METHODOLOGY FOR QUANTITATIVE BE POST EVALUATIONS
BY
PROFESSOR JoSt MARIA DAGNINO-PASTORE
June 26, 1990



- 161 -
TECHNICAL APPENDIX
METHODOLOGY FOR QUANTITATIVE EX POST EVALUATIONS
Since their inception in the late 19609 and early 1970s ex post
evaluations of macroeconomic programs and projects, especially by
international institutions, have become widespread.11 Yet, I have seen no
satisfactory comprehensive methodology for such appraisals.21 Maybe this is
due to the importance of qualitative assessments in these exercises.
This paper is an attempt to provide a formal comprehensive
methodology for ex post evaluations amenable to treatment in quantitative
terms. Since the aim of this practice is more to learn lessons for the
future than to judge past performance, it becomes even more important, when
comparing targets with results, to assign the deviations to different
causes. The typology of causes should not only permit such allocation but
also be designed so as to facilitate the feedback of learned lessons to
future actions.
A natural classification of sources of deviations (results versus
goals) is between those due to design and those due to implementation.31 In
turn, inadequate design can originate in the inaccurate assessment and
projections of external events, in the setting of inappropriate or
unrealistic goals, or in poor understanding of the way the relevant system
works. Poor implementation, the failure to achieve targets, may be due to
insufficient performance at different levels of the chain of conmand (see
footnote 2).
At this stage, it becomes clear that the Tinbergen approach to
economic policy,4J with its classification of variables, provides a good
starting point for constructing a methodology for ex post evaluations
--since projects and programs are policies in action.
1/ For reviews of methods, organization, and experience, see: Organization
for Economic Cooperation and Development, OECD (1972 and 1976).
21 I do not claim, however, an exhaustive knowledge of the literature on
the subject. The efforts I have seen focus on the chain of ends and
means and, try to evaluate performance at different implementation
levels. See, for example: Practical Concepts, Inc. (1980).
31 A third source of deviations would be the control of projects and
programs, which can be appraised in terms of speed and success of
reactions to deviations from targets.
41 See Tinbergen (1952 and 1956).



- 162 -
A. The Tinbergen Model of Economy Policy
In this model economic variables are classified in four groups
according to data, objectives, instruments and irrelevants. The data
variables (vi) are those exogenous to the model. The objective variables
(y1) are those considered by the authority as the best to improve general
welfare. The instrument variables (zi) are those whose values can be
determined by management. The irrelevant variables (xi) are all other
economic variables.
Economic models consist of structural relations which include
variables of the various types mentioned. These relations are classified
in demand, supply, technical, and definitional equations. Their number
depends on the disaggregation level of the model. Under certain
conditions, relations can be treated as linear.
The Complete Form
The general form of a set of relations ist
J         K         L          M
[1]   E aijxj + E bikyk + E cilzl + E dis vm -

wheres

- number of instrument variables,
mml,...,M (MrJ) - nuiAber of data variables,
aij - coefficients of irrelevant variables,
bik - coefficients of objective variables,
cil = coefficients of instrument variables and,
dim - coefficients of data variables.

objective, instrument, and data variables, respectively, and x, y, a and v
are vectors representing irrelevant, objective, instrument, and data
variables, respectively. 0 is a zero vector.
For the model to be determined exactly, the number of equations
should be equal to the number of variables that are unknowns in the problem
posed; that is, irrelevant and objective variabless

For the authority, the "economic policy problem' is to set the

model. to achieve those obiectives. Since the authority sets the values
(goals) of the objective variables, they become known by definition --
exogenous (together with the data); and since it has to find the values
(targets) of the instruments variables that comply with the former, they
become unknown by definition -- eadogenous (together with the irrelevant).
Again, for the problem to have a uniaue solution, the number of
equations should be equal to the number of unknowns; in this case, to equal
the number of irrelevant and instrument variables:

The number of instruments must be ecual to the number of
obiectives. If there are more objectives than instruments (K>L), there are
more equations than unknowns; the model is overdeterminated; and there is
no solution to the problem except by chance. If there are more instruments
than objectives (L>K), there are more unknowns than equations; the model is
undetermined, there are infinite solutions to the problem.

B. An Estimation of an Ex Post Tinberten Model
The equation system z represents a consistent projection of the
economy, which provides ex ante values for each of the variables, including
the paramters (matriz coefficients). Ex post, once the period of the
projection has ended, other values of the variables, excluding the
parameters, are 'observed,' but the new set of matrix coefficients is not
observed. In fact, there are many sets of matrix coefficients which,
together with the observed values of the variables, conform to an equation
system representirg a consistent 'realisation' -- as opposed to projection
-- of the economys
t14]  A' x  + B y0 + C'Os0 e  DO vO,
where Ak, B', CO, and DI are mattices of coefficient' of irrelevant,
objective, instrument and data variables that satisfy equation (14] --
unknown -- and x0, 70, s0 and v0 are the 'observe4' vataes of vectors
representing irrelevant, objective, instrument, and data variables,
respectively.
The equation system [141 has I equation and I(2L + J + M) unknowns
-- the elements of matrices Al, 3', C' and D'. For it to be exactly
determined, I(2L + J + M-1), additional restrictions are required. The
question is whether there is a set of additional restrictions that makes
'economic sense.' I think there is. The rationale is the following. The
mere fact that the model includes not only identities but also behavioral
equations implies 0me faith in the explanatory (or forecasting) value of
the latter and consequently, in the stability (or predictability) of their
parameters.51
5/ For instance, the model y E x + z, *here y = unknown, s,z O exogenous,
is replaced by the model y p x + z, x - a z, where a is a parameter and
if the value of s is determined better as a function of a than
exogenously. This implies some confidence in the stability or
predictability of parameter a. In fact, making these decisions is what
model building is about.



- 166 -
So, a 'reasonable' assumption -- in the absence of any other
information -- is that the elements of the new (parameter) matrices A', I',
C', and D' should differ as little as possible from the elements of the old
(parameter) matrices A, 3, C and D, respectively. Thus, a set of addi-
tional restrictions is needed that selects among the [I(2L+J+M -1)*(1/4)]
matrices of parameters that satisfy the equation system t14], the set of
four matrices that minimize some weighted (increasing) function of the
elements of dA, dl, dC, and dD.
Such a function should at least comply with the following
conditions:
(a)   to be invariant to the selection of units in which the variables
-- and parameters -- are measured;
(b)   to penalize larger rather than smaller relative changes in the
value of each of the parameters; and
(c)   to be neutral as to the Importance of the changes in the values of
each parameter.
The first condition is met by using relative changes, (that is daijlais).
Condition (b) is usually taken care of by working with the square of tie
relative changes (dai /aij)2. Finally, condition (c) is that the sum to be
minimized should weig equally all of its elements, Ej Ei wij (daij/aij)2;
Iw   (i-l,,..,I  jl,.*J)
The problem, then, is to minimize the functions
[15] Z - Ej Ei (daij)2/1aij)2 + Ek Ei (dbik)2/Ibik)2 +
+ l1 Ei (dcil) /(cil)2 + Em Ei (ddim) /(dim)
subject to:
(16]  A' zO + DI yO + C   O + DIw  =O.
Consequently, the Lagrangian function is:
[l1] Lu 1 EJ3E (13j -. ej)2  .1    E8  - bik - bik)2

*1 El El (cWTl  - 2  -   E l (d'l; - dTN2

- U(A' :0, S, yC' CO * *' IO),
and the set of conditions for the values of the variables to be stationary:
El$3  ii   a - t *Ii   +@ X30 a o, which ar IxJ,;

nt In economic models some of the coefficients of the variables are defined
to be zero (when there is no relation between the variables) or unity
(in accounting identities). In such cases, the function to minimize
becomes:
a a xi El        tor a11 *oij    e and  j # 1, ....
subject to j E1 a'ij xj   0, and to the additional conditions:
a'ij - aij for all aij - 0 and aij  - 1.

abandoned conditions for stationary values of the Lagrangian function:
."0 , forall 0rij    n rij a 1.
Thus, the model remains exactly determined. It is also possible to
introduce additional restrictions to parameter values; that is, to bound
s' between 0 and unity. These restrictions rule out solutions that
include outliers.

C. The Differences Between the Ex Post and Ex Ante Estimations
The Complete Form
The differences between the ex ante and the ex Post estimations of
a complete form Tinbergen model are obtained by deducting (2) from (14]:
[211    A'l z  - A x I B' y - B y + C' z - C a I D' v - D v - 0.
The differences between the goals set for the objective variables
and their ex post values, yo - y, are the deviations from the program or
project objectives. The aim of this paper is to explain their origins;
that is, to assign them to different causes. In order to do so, it is
necessary to solve equation system [211 for yo - y. It is possible to
arrive at the following expressions
[221    3' dy - - [A' m + B y + c' z + D' vO + dB y],
but dy - y - y can only be solved if B-3 exists. For it to exist, B has
to be squared, or I-K. But then, from [31 or t41, we know that J-0. That
is, there should be no irrelevant variables, and this happens only In the
reduced form. The irrelevant variables are irrelevant indeed!9/

observed.
S. Apvlication of the Methodolo&y to the DIF-Bank Approach
Kahn and othersl2l have modeled an integration of the World Bank
and the IHF's analytical approaches to adjustment and growth -- which are
policy oriented. Thus, their Fund-Bank model is both relevant and suitable
for the application of the methodology for quantitative ex post evaluations
just explained. I have introduced only minor adjustments, to make it
exactly determined instead of undetermined.
The changes are the followings Kahn's equation 1221 was changed
to [22a]; this export equation was totally modified, to make exports
endogenous: and a public savings equation was introduced. Finally, some
excess irrelevants were transformed into objectives (denoted by an
asterisk).
The Comglete Form
The resulting complete model has 14 equations and 36 variables
(including 13 parameters), of which 17 are data (including 13 parameters)
and five are objectives; the remaining unknowns are 14 (nine irrelevants,
five instruments), the same as the number of equations:131

An ex post Estimation of the Model's Parameters
To evaluate the results of a program it is necessary to estimate
values of the 13 parameters ex post, minimizing the sum of the squares of
the relative changes with respect to the values of the parameters ex ante.

F. Application of the Methodology to the Chilean SAP, 1985-87
The Chilean SAP is a good case for application of the Fund-Bank
model and the methodology proposed for quantitative ex post evaluations.
The application has only demonstrative purposes; due to limited time,
resources, and data availability, it was necessary to adopt assumptions
such that an Interpretation of the results for the purposes of drawing
policy or performance conclusions is not warranted.
The Model
Some changes have been introduced in the model according to the
particular behavior of the parameters in the Chilean economy. The
modifications area

The Data
The values of the parameters and data used are shown in Table
TA.1. Parameters r, a, v, dFP, dFR9 a, b, c, and f were estimated from
data for years 1979 through 1085, while Py and P. were taken from the
documents presented by Chile's Government to the Bank when negotiating SAL
I in 1985. For 1986 and 1987, r and a were assumed to be constant.
In Table TA.2 the objectives set for the period under analysis can
be observed. With this information and that of Table TA.1, the results of
the model ore obtained, as seen in Table TA.3.
Table TA.4 has the observed values of irrelevant, data, objective,
and instrument variables. On these basis, the values of the parameters in
Table TA.5 are estimated, which satisfy the observed values of the
variables as shown in Table TA.4.



- 189 -
Then, Table TA.6 presents a disaggregation of the deviations that
occurred in the objectives.  Given the model design, there are only four
data types; consequently, there are no errors of types II, V, and VIII.
For each objective there are 27 different explanations of the deviation
between the observed value and the programed value (goal). The deviations
due to each of these causes can have different signs and large values, and
the total deviation a smaller value. The total deviation in an objeftive
due to a given type of error is assigned to different data, or instruments.
The Results
In 1986, in dY* -- see elII in Table TA.6 -- lower-than-expected
fiscal consumption, Cg, and credits to the private sector dD., not totally
compensated by higher-than-programmed public investment, explain why the
program stopped short of its goal. Instead, errors in instrument
parameters underestimated real GDP growth, thus leading to a lesser total
overestimteLon of dY*.
Public savings, A,*, exceeded its goal, mostly due to a larger
than expected public invesiment, dKg. Positive outweighed negative partial
errors in the case of change in domestic prices, 4PD*, the principal cause
being the smaller than expected credit to the private sector, dDp. With
respect to net international reserves, dR*. its observed value was also
larger than the predetermined, and again the credit to the private sector,
dDp, explains the largest share of the total deviation. But in this case
the influence of errors in data parameters was also important, notably that
of the lagged exchange rate, e_.11, which tended to underestimate the level.
Finally, in the case of X*, implementation errors hindered export
performance (again dDO), while errors in instrument parameters
underestimated X*. The net effect of the two factors was small
overcompliance.
In sumary, in 1986 observed values were above projected values,
with great influence from a dDp smaller than expected.  For 1987, the
observed dY* was larger than projected, due to implementation errors,
compensated by Type-VI errors. Here the largest influence is from d4%. In
Ag*. errors in data parameters -- notably in that of e-1 -- underestimated
the value and outweighed implementation -- especially in dDs -- errors in
the opposite direction. The observed dPD* value exceeded expectations,
mainly due to implementation errors (dD).
The parameter dR* fell below its planned value. The total
deviation is distributed, with the largest influence due to data parameter
and implementation errors. Among them, the most important are e-1; dDp,
and dDg, respectively; also, in the parameter of de, error of type VI was
important.  There is a great difference in Xv (the observed value far
superior), mainly due to implementation errors (the largest weight is of
dDp) and to errors of type VI. In 1987, errors were more significant than
in 1986 -- in accordance with expectations, since projections were based in
1985 figures. Again there is an important weight of implementation errors
and also of data parameters. The most influential variables were dDp and
e-2, respectively.
