PROGRA1 COMPLEION REPORT
FIRST, SECOND, AND THI  STRUCTURL ADJUsTMT LOANS
(LOANS 2625-CH, 2767-CH, AIID 2892-CH)
Agust 15, 1989
Coutry Operations Departaet IV
i.tin America and the Caribban Regional Office



-193 -
I. INTRODUCTION
1.01     There were three consecutive Structural Adjustment Loans (SALs) to
Chile, each the equivalent of US$250 million. SALs I and III were dis-
bursed in two equal tranches; SAL II was disbursed in tranches of $150.0
million and $100.0 million. respectively. The first SAL (Loan 2625-CH) was
sipned on October 25, 1985, SAL II (Loan 2767-CH) on November 21, 1986, and
SAL III (Loan 2892-CH) on December 16, 1987. The second tranche of SAL
III, which is the last disbursement in this cycle of SALs, was largely
disbursed on December 22, 1988.
1.02     The three SALs supported the Government's structural ad4ustment
program that began in 1985. The SAL program concentrated on four areas of
economic policy: export incentives and management of the balance of pay-
ments; savings generation and investment; rehabilitation and development of
the financial system; and improvement of the pension program and health
care system.
Ii.  BMZGINCE OF STRUCTURAL PRO1SMS
1.03     The stage for Chile's economic crisis was set in the late seven-
ties and early eighties, before the country experienced major terms of
trade and international interest rate shocks. A combination of domestic
economic policies and external shocks interrupted the structural adjustment
process and economic growth that were initiated in the mid-seventies.
1.04     These strmctural adjustments of the mid-seventies aimed at
increasing economic efficiency, and included the improved international
integration of the economy, the promotion of private initiative as the
State played a diminishing role in the economy, and the use of market
forces for the allocation of resources. The above efforts resulted in the
growth of exports and the economy, the reduction of the public sector
external debt, and the improvement in public finances. However, these
accomplishments were accompanied by a high rate of unemployment. Further-
more, policies implemented particularly during the 1979-81 period resulted
in the creation of a macroeconomic disequilibrium.
1.05     Chile's opening of its capital account, which started gradually in
1978, was completed by April 1980. This, in conjunction with the fixing of
the exchange rate in June 1979 and the full indexation of wages to past
inflation, led to massive inflows of unguaranteed long-term credit. These
capital flows financed an unsustainable expansion of private consumption
and investment in 1980 and early 1981. The Chilean boom of that period was
fundamentally financed with foreign credit. As a consequence, external
debt rose from US$6.7 billion at end-1978, to US$17.2 billion by end-1982,
or the equivalent of 69 percent of GDP. More than 100 percent of the
increase in debt during this period was contracted by the private sector,
since government net foreign liabilities declined continuously from 1975 to
1981.
1.06     At the same time, the competitiveness of the country's tradeable
sector was being eroded by the fixed exchange rate that was instituted in
June 1979. Furthermore, the corporate sector was generally weakened by
excessive real domestic interest rates, whicl; exceeded 30 percent per year



- 194 -
at their peak in the late seventies. Finally, the financial sector was
affected by the deterioration in the quality of their loans. This was due
to two main causess the general problems of the corporate sector, and the
granting of credits to companies the ownership of which was related to that
of the lending institutions. This generalized practice undermined the
stability and the public confidence in the financial system.
III.  SEX CRISIS AND TE FIRST ADJUSTulT BFVORTSs  1982-05
1.07     At the end of 1981 the Chilean economy was burdened by high
foreign debt, a weak corporate sector, and a crippled financial system.
Domestic imbalances, which led to the slowdown of the economy in early
1981, were aggravated by the deteriorating external environment. Steep oil
price increases followed by falling copper prices, surging real interest
rates and subsequent deep international recession battered the economy.
Between 1979 and 1983, Chile's terms of trade fell 26.5 percent, among the
severest losses for any Latin American economy.
1.08     The sharp curtailment of capital flows in early 1982, which tran-
spired even before the generalized debt crisis, amplified the problem and
led the economy to the depression of 1982-83. In 1982 alone, real CDP
declined by 14.1 percent, and open unemployment rose to over 23 percent.
The collapse in domestic demand led to the decline in Imports from US$6.5
billion to US$3.6 billion, while the current account deficit contracted by
US$2.4 billion, or over 10 percent of the GDP.
1.09     Substantial uncertainty prevailed in the country from mid-1982 to
the first quarter of 1983. Various exchange rate regimes were tried in
that short period of time (fixed, free float, crawling peg), and several
finance ministers held that position in rapid succession.
1.10     During the rest of 1983, the Government continued with its austere
policies, reflecting the 'Stand-by' program agreed with the IF, without
however adopting adjustment policies of a structural nature. In 1983,
Chile's trade surplus reached US$l billion, with imports continuing their
decline to less than half their 1981 levels. In that year, after providing
state guarantees to the private financial sector's external debt, Chile was
able to restructure its debts and secure additional resources from
international banks. Despite the deterioration in the international
environment, Chile was in full compliance with its IMF 'Stand-by' program.
1.11     On the domestic front, the most pressing issues during 1983 and
1984 were the need to reduce high and rising rates of unemployment and
strengthen the private financial and corporate sectors, which confronted a
severe financial crisis. Toward the first of these goals, the Government
devoted significant resources to the development of special employment
programs and increased public investment in infrastructure and housing.
Towards the second. a major part of the financial sector was intervened in
January 1983: all major banks and two corporate groups. In order to
strengthen the recovery of the financial system, the Government established
a preferential exchange rate to serve dollar debts, and rescheduled debts
in 1983 and 1984 for viable companies and financial institutions at con-
cessionary terms. In addition, in 1984, the Central Bank, in order to
strengthen the banking system, purchased bad loan portfolios from banks
which agreed to repurchase them with future profits.



- 195 -
1.12     Public finances deteriorated markedly after 1981, owing to a num-
ber of factors. Quantitatively, the most important was the social security
reform of mid-1981. The transfer of many workers from the State pay-as-
you-go system to the private capitalization scheme implied a big fiscal
burden to the Government. On the other hand, public revenues were badly
hurt by the 1982-83 depression and, in 1984, by expansionist fiscal pol-
icies. The consolidated nonfinancial public sector deficit in 1984 reached
4.3 percent of GDP, as compared to the yearly surpluses generated between
1976 and 1981.
1.13     Between April and September 1984, a new finance minister attempted
to reactivate the economy by stimulating aggregate demand. As a con-
sequence, the economy grew that year by 6.3 percent, but the current
account deficit almost doubled. It became increasingly clear through that
year that the expansion was unsustainable and that the economy lacked a
coherent long-term economic plan. It also became clear that in order to
generate the resources for a satisfactory economic growth, the Chilean
economy required a profound structural adjustment.  Thus, in February 1985,
with a new finance minister at the helm, and with the assistance of the
World Bank, the Government proceeded to prepare a structuril adjustment
program for the three year period of 1985-87. The program attempted to
strengthen the structural adjustment strategy that was initiated in the
mid-seventies.
IV. SAL I
A. The AdJustment Pro8rm
1.14     The structural adjustment program of 1985 had as an objective the
recovery of economic activity and employment, while gradually reducing the
external debt burden. This program attempted to achieve these objectives
through the implementation of policies directed at resolving three priority
structural problems facing the economy: insufficient growth and excessive
concentration of exports; low levels of savings and investment; and the
precarious situation of the financial system.
1.15     More specifically, the adjustment program attempted to expand
nontraditional exports through the uniform reduction of tariffs, the estab-
lishment of a stable and realistic exchange rate policy, the creation of a
mnchanism to smooth the fluctuations of copper prices (the Copper Stabil-
ization Fund), the more expeditious refund of the value-added tax (VAT) to
exporters, the refund of indirect taxes to small exporters, the study of
forest industries towards the promotion of this sector, and the strengthen-
ing of PROCRILE, a public institution commissioned to assist exporters, by
providing more direct support to small and medium exporters (the latter
received funding from a Technical Assistance Loan (2504-CH) signed in April
1985).
1.16     On savings and investment, since the private financial and non-
financial sectors faced serious financial difficulties, it was understood
that the major source of an increase in savings, at that stage, should be
the public sector. Therefore, the adjustment program called for austerity
in current fiscal expeaditures, emphasizing the increase in employment over



- 196 -
wages, and the pricing of public services that would reflect the opportun-
ity cost of resources devoted to them. Furthermore, the public sector
sought to improve the return on its investments, with resources scheduled
to flow principally to copper and energy, and undertook additional infra-
structure investments to reduce bottlenecks affecting private investment.
1.17     As far as the rescue of the financial system was concerned, the
substantial transfer of public resources already required by the various
rescue operations prompted the Government to halt additional subsidies to
the banking system, and reduce, to the extent possible, existing subsidy
programs. Furthermore, they halted further subsidized reschedulings of
debt, strengthened financial and corporate legislation regarding market
concentration and quality of assets, and created legislation allowing the
State to assist in the recapitalization of intervened banks.
S. IuPlemeutation and Results
1.18     The Chilean Government complied satisfactorily with the content
and timing of the prescribed policy actions, which are described in detail
in Annex 2.
1.19     Regarding the balance of payments, during 1985, the Government
reduced its uniform import tariff from 35 percent to 20 percent, and depre-
ciated the peso versus the US dollar by 25 percent, to its lowest level in
ten years. In 1986, the depreciation of the US dollar against other cur-
rencies led to a further 7 percent real depreciation of the peso. In addi-
tion to the peso's depreciation, the Government implemented a series of
tax, banking, administrative and fiscal measures as export incentives.
1.20     To ensure that future upward movements of volatile copper prices
would not result in a real appreciation of the peso, a pilot Copper Stabil-
ization Fund was created on January 1, 1986.  It acted as a mechaniem to
ensure, through budget adjustments, that part of CODELCO's copper revenues
from higher than budgeted copper prices would be sterilized in a reserve
fund.  The funds deposited would, in turn, be utilized partly to smooth out
copper revenue shortfalls if the copper price were lower than budgeted.
Because of low international copper prices, no deposits were made in the
Copper Stabilization Fund during SAL 1.
1.21     The above adjustments created a favorable environment for private
investment in new export activities and efficient import substitution lead-
ing to a moderate growth-with-adjustment result, with 1985 GDP growing by
2.4 percent. Noncopper exports grew at a real rate of 9 percent; imports
declined by 12 percent, although imports of capital goods rose by 10.6 per-
cent. Chile's trade surplus increased sharply to US$790 million. a sub-
stantial rise from the US$290 million of 1984. For the 1985-86 period,
despite a 10 percent deterioration in Chile's terms of trade, the trade
surplus almost quadrupled, rising to US$1.1 billion. Lower-than-expected
international interest rates and the repricing obtained through external
debt negotiations complemented Chile's trade effort. The current account
deficit was reduced from USS2 billion in 1984 to US$1.1 billion in 1986 as
programmed.



- 197 -
1.22     Prudent management of Chile's external debt shaped the performance
of its capital account. The first SAL, the IMF's EFF, and the partial IBRD
guarantee of a US$300 million B-Loan opened the way for a favorable agree-
ment with the commercial banks on the 1985-86 financial package. Chile and
its creditors agreed to restructure US$5.7 billion of maturities due in
1985-87, and the banks provided US$1.1 billion of new money for 1985-86.
This debt management strategy was complemented by one of the region's first
and most innovative debt conversion programs, exchanging discounted foreign
debt for equity and domestic liabilities.
1.23     Gross domestic savings increased by nearly one-half in the adjust-
ment period--from 12.6 percent of GDP in 1984 to 18.4 percent in 1986.
National savings rose to #.7 percent of GDP in 1986, up from 2.6 percent in
1984. As anticipated, public savings led this effort. Private savings
continued to hover around 3 percent, dipping to 1.3 percent in 1985, but
rebounding to 3.3 percent in 1986.
1.24     A conservative fiscal policy markedly improved public finances.
The Government increased collections by 1.2 percent of GDP between 1984 and
1986, through improvements in administration of the value-added tax. It
made a sustained effort to restrain expenditures by holding increases in
salaries and pensions at or below the rate of inflation; the fall in open
unemployment permitted lower transfer payments for emergency employment
programs; the Government also cut other subsidies, mainly affecting the
middle class. Operational surpluses of the public enterprises also con-
tributed to the increase in public savings; they were three percentage
points higher as a share of GDP in 1986 than in 1984. Finally, lower
foreign and domestic interest rates helped keep expenditures down. Public
savings increased from 0.5 percent of GDP in 1984 to 4.4 percent in 1986.
1.25     Total investment as a share of GDP continued to hover around
14 percent in 1984-86 after strong recovery from the depression nadir of
9.8 percent in 1983. Public sector investment rose to 7.6 percent of GDP
in 1986, up from 6.4 percent in 1984. A Bank evaluation in December 1986
found public investment to be generally consistent with the adjustment
program. Private fixed investment grew by more than 10 percent annually in
1985 and 1986. However, overall rates of investment still lagged those
recorded in the mid-seventies; gross private domestic investment as a share
of GDP averaged about 8.3 percent of GDP in 1975-79, compared to about
7 percent in 1985-86.
1.26     Regarding the corporate and financial sector program, to reduce
the losses that the Central Bank incurred in the 1983-84 rescue operations
(para. 11), and return credibility to the banking system, the Central Bank
gradually phased out by end-1986 the preferential exchange rate for all-
but-small debtors, and the dollar swap and deposit subsidies by early 1987.
These policies reduced the operational deficit of the Central Bank to
3.2 percent of GDP in 1986.
1.27     In mid-1985, the Government began a recapitalization program for
the two major intervened banks, the Banco de Chile and Banco de Santiago.
Additionally, all domestic banks were permitted to restructure the liabil-
ities of heavily indebted domestic productive companies, and, in some
cases, to capitalize debts. By end-1986, the restructuring program was



- 198 -
terminated. These efforts helped improve the loan portfolio of the finan-
cial system. By end-1986, 82 percent of the financial system's portfolio
held the two highest classification ratings, compared to 63 percent two
years before, largely as a consequence of the Central Bank's purchase of
bad loans. Recapitalization and reprogramming measures allowed the finan-
cial system to make an overall profit in 1985 for the first time since 1982
and to renew lending to viable firms. Net profits of the financial system
rose slightly in 1986.
V. SAL UI
1.28     During 1985, the Chilean economy started building the foundations
for sustained growth, on the basis of higher, more diversified exports,
improved internal savings, and reduced dependence on external financiog.
In view of the initial success of the adjustment program and the need to
strengthen such foundations, early in 1986, the Chilean Government
requested from the World Bank a second SAL.
1.29     At that stage, the Country was still not able to cover even half
of its interest payments with domestically generated resources.  Savings
levels were still lower than necessary to finance a strong investment
effort, and investment was insufficient to support a targeted long-term GDP
growth rate of about 5 percent per year. Real wages and per capita incomes
were well below their 1981 peaks and the social costs of the crisis were
significant. In view of the above, the World Bank agreed to continue sup-
porting the adjustment program with an additional loan.
A. The Adjustment Program
1.30     On November 21, 1986, the World Bank signed SAL II with Chile.
Once again, the value of the loan was for US$250 million, to be disbursed
in two tranches. In this second phase, the priorities of the program were
to accelerate the reprivatization and recapitalization of the banking and
corporate sectors, increase the rate of public savings to 4.4 percent of
GDP, promote an austere and efficient public sector investment program, and
strengthen the economy's export incentives and social programs.
1.31     More specifically, the adjustment program aimed inter alia at
improving the efficiency and safety of the financial markets. Measures
taken in that direction includedt the recapitalization of the private
nonfinancial sector through the case-by-case renegotiation of debts that
had a chance of being repaid; the recapitalization of the banking system;
and the reprivatization of intervened banks. Furthermore, the adjttstment
program introduced a new Banking Law that aimed to strengthen the super-
visory and legal framework required for the sound functioning of Chile's
banking system.
1.32     As for attaining the target for public sector savings, the program
envisaged the continuation of the austere current expenditure program that
was initiated in 1985, in vhich employment growth took priority over wage
increases. Furthermore, the program sought improvements in the efficiency
of public sector investments, both by following through with large scale
projects in copper and energy, and medium-sized infrastructure projects
that aided in the development of export and import substituting regions.



- 199 -
1.33     In order to strengthen export incentives, the program sought to:
maintain a stable and realistic exchange rate policy; continue with the
Copper Stabilization Fund; amplify bank credit margins to exporters; study
more specifically the investment requirements of the forestry sector; and
continue to enhance the support PROCHILE provided to medium and small non-
traditional exporters.
1.34     Finally, in view of the need to target more efficiently social
expenditures, the adjustment program included the decentralization of
administration of the main social programs. Furthermore, it sought to
increase the financial resources for the national public health system by
increasing the compulsory health contribution. This action was comple-
mented by financial and administrative changes to improve the use of cur-
rent health facilities.
X. luplementation and Results
1.35     The Chilean Government complied satisfactorily with the content
and timing of the prescribed policy actions, which are described in detail
in Annex 3.
1.36     Regarding the balance of payments, during 1986 and 1987, the Gov-
ernment kept its uniform tariff rate at 20 percent. The peso continued to
depreciate progressively, by the difference between domestic and inter-
national inflation. However, the depreciation of the US dollar against
other currencies led to the real effective depreciation of the peso by
15.5 percent in 1986, and an additional 7.4 percent in 1987.
1.37     The stable trade regime created a favorable environment for
private investment in new export activities and efficient import substitu-
tion. Noncopper exports were 22.4 percent higher in 1987 than the year
before which, together with higher copper prices, resulted in an increase
in overall export revenues in 1987 of 24.4 percent. In view of the better-
than-anticipated noncopper export performance both in 1986 and 1987, it was
agreed not to modify the export incentive framework. Imports increased by
29 percent as imports of capital goods, indicating a growth in investment,
expanded by 35 percent. Despite the substantial growth in imports, Chile's
trade surplus increased to US$1,229 million in 1987, a substantial rise
from the US$302 million in 1984.
1.38     The Government maintained its Stabilization Fund for copper
revenues.  However, as was the case in SAL I, during SAL II international
copper prices remained below those activating the Fund and, consequently,
no deposits were made in it.
1.39     Lower international interest rates complemented Chile's trade
efforts. The current account deficit was reduced from US$2 billion in 1984
to US$809 million in 1987. However, with no new disbursements from its
commercial creditors, Chile's net international reserves at end-1987 stood
at US$1.9 billion, indicating a loss of US$124 million over the year.
Nevertheless, international reserves covered 5.5 months of imports.



- 200-
1.40     The continuous involvement of multilateral organizations in Chile
facilitated the February 1987 agreement between the Government and its
commercial creditors on vays to fund the 1987-88 financial gap. The agree-
ment became effective in June 1987. It consolidated interest payments into
an annual payment, which deferred the July 1988 payment of about US$450
million to January 1989. In addition, maturities in 1988-91 were resched-
uled on a serial basis. Also current spreads on past new money facilities
(1983, 1984, 1985 and the cofinancing agreement) were reduced to 1-118
percent over LIBOR, while those on restructured maturities (1983-91) were
reduced to 1 percent, producing savings of US$65 million in 1987-88.
Together with the reschedulings agreed with creditor governments, these
measures were expected to close the 1987-88 financial gap.
1.41     Improvement in public finances made an important contribution to
the balance of payments and recovery. The overall fiscal deficit declined
from 4.4 percent of GDP in 1984 to 0.6 percent in 1987. Revenues benefited
from the increase in copper prices and higher tax collections (particularly
international trade). Public savings in 1987 increased to 5.3 percents
improvement came largely as a consequence of restraint in current expendi-
tures, which grew at a real 1.1 percent, reflecting low cost-of-living
adjustments for salaries and pensions, lower transfer payments for unem-
ployment and related subsidies, and the lower level of international
interest rates.
1.42     The decrease of the public sector deficit was consistent with the
further growth in public sector investment which rose to 7.3 percent of GDP
in 1987, up from 6.4 percent in 1984. The concurrent increase in private
investment, contributed to the growth of overall investment from 13.7 per-
cent of GDP in 1985, to 14.6 percent in 1986, and 16.9 percent in 1987.
1.43     The Government's public sector investment program for 1986 was
reviewed with the Bank during an economic mission to Chile in December
1986, and the mission concurred with the project priorities of the Govern-
ment. Public sector investment that year amounted to 7.5 percent of GDP,
in close accord with the Government's original forecast.
1.44     The program to recapitalize the banking system was initiated in
1984 and consisted of the following main measuress ti) recapitalizing of
the five intervened banks; (ii) requiring shareholders of other weakened
banks to subscribe new capital; (iii) permitting the sale of bad portfolio
to the Central Bank up to a limit of 3.5 times the subscription of new
capital as required by the Superintendency of Banks and Financial Institu-
tions; (iv) eliminating deficiencies in provisions; and (v) provisioning
adequately for loan losses, investments, and real repossessed collateral.
Items (i), (ii) and (iii) were completed by December 1986. Provision defi-
ciencies were eliminated by the end of 1986, except for provisions against
losses from earlier periods which were not made up fully by the Banco de
Chile until the end of March 1987. Thus, by early 1987, the recapitaliza-
tion of the banking system was essentially complete.
1.45     Another fundamental target of the program was the elimination of
the various public subsidies to private debtors. This target was nearly
totally met by December 1986, with the phasing out of the preferential
exchange rate for debtors of over US$50,000. Dollar swap and deposit sub-



- 201 -
sidies were phased out in early 1987. Generalized reprogramings at sub-
sidized rates had ended a year earlier, as were the generous credit lines
to the banking sector.
1.46     Sy late 1986. short-term solvency had been mostly secured in the
financial and corporate sectors. The financial sector, for example, showed
an overall profit in 1985 (for the first time since 1982) and improved its
performance in 1986. Recapitalization of the corporate sector also pro-
ceeded at a rapid pace, in the face of incentives for case-by-case finan-
cial workouts between private debtors and their commercial creditors.
During 1986, operationally viable firms generated sufficient profits to
service their financial payments; by December 1986, most of them had
reached satisfactory financial workout arrangements that had strengthened
their overall debt-equity ratio.
1.47     During SAL II, attention focused on the recapitalization of the
banking and corporate sectors to assure their long-term solvency, the re-
privatization of several intervened banks and nonfinancial companies, and
the provision of a better regulatory and legal framework for the banking
system. Those actions marked a clear shift from short-term crisis manage-
ment to addressing economic issues relevant to the long-tetm growth of the
economy.
1.48     In the first stage of the program, supported by SAL I, social
programs centered on the generation of jobs and the provision of emergency
employment programs for the needy. Principally as a result of the economic
recovery, workers in the emergency employment programs were reduced from
500,000 in 1983 to less than 200,000 in August 1986. Nevertheless,
declines in public spending on health and education deteriorated the infra-
structure, equipment and wages in these sectors. For example, public
sector health expenditures declined 18 percent in real terms between 1981
and 1986.
1.49     During SAL II, an effort was made to reduce these shortcomings.
The compulsory health contribution from salaries was increased from 6 to
7 percent, an effort was made to improve the productivity and efficiency of
current health facilities and the emergency employment programs. In order
to improve the targeting of expenditures and decentralize the administra-
tion of major social programs, the Government proceeded with the munici-
palization of schools. While at the time of the second tranche release of
SAL II, it was too early to evaluate the effectiveness of school municipal-
ization, it became clear that the increase of the health tax on salaries
was not sufficient to finance the needed improvements in infrastructure and
equipment expenditures in the health sector.
1.50     There were some difficulties with the execution of studies agreed
upon under SAL 1I. The Government was to have undertaken four studies.
Two studies, on employment and health, were satisfactorily completed, albe-
it with some delay. The Government did not undertake the studies of the
impact on domestic savings of pension funds, and public enterprise effici-
ency. On the study of the impact on domestic savings, after some consider-
ation, the Government decided, in consultation with the Bank, that other
issues were of greater concern, namely the restrictions on interme. ion
of the pension funds in long-term markets. Therefore, the latter study was
comissioned under SAL III.



- 202 -
3.51     The study of public enterprise efficiency, financed under a Tech-
nical Assistance Loan (IBRD Loan No. 2504-CH), was also slow to get under-
way as the Government concentrated its efforts on privatization. Although
a consulting firm was selected in May 1986, contract negotiations were
significantly delayed as the Chilean agencies involved realized that the
rapid progress on privatization had changed the issues highlighted in the
original terms of reference. The selected firm eventually withdrew. This
led to a major change in the terms of reference for the study based on new
proposals advanced by the Ministry of the Economy, charged with administer-
ing the TAL.  However, the Ministry of Finance did not approve the
study--which would have focused on a management information system report-
ing to the Economy Ministry--on grounds that it would not be cost-
effective, since an information system reporting to CORI0 was already well-
established. Given this apparent stalemate, the Bank felt it improvident
to press the issue for purposes of the structural adjustment program.
VI. SAL II
1.52     During 1986, the Chilean economy continued its progress. on the
basis of sustained growth in production and employment, the improvement of
external accounts, and the relative decline in external indebtedness. With
the successful completion of the second SAL, at the beginning of 1987, the
Chilean Government requested from the World Bank a third SAL, in order to
consolidate the advances made, and strengthen the adjustment process in
other areas, like the social sectors.
A. The Adjustment Prosram
1.53     On December 16, 1987, the World Bank signed SAL III with Chile.
The value of this loan, as was the case with the previous two, was for
US$250 million, to be disbursed in two tranches. However, while in the
previous two SALs the second tranche release was scheduled seven months
after the first tranche, in SAL III, the second tranche release was sched-
uled for twelve months later to provide time for the Government to enact
the needed reforms, especially in the social security and health sectors.
1.54     The program focused again on the promotion of exports, investment,
and savings, and the completion of the financial sector's rehabilitation.
Additionally, it placed particular emphasis on the social sectors, to
ensure that efforts toward structural adjustment would continue to be
accompanied by constant improvements in living conditions, particularly for
the poorest segments of society.
1.55     As far as exports are concerned, the adjustment program continued
emphasizing the need to retain a favorable macroeconomic environment and
continue with the reduction of disincentives imbedded in the tariff
structure. More specifically, the program endorsed the maintenance of
uniform and low tariffs, the continuation and strengthening of the Copper
Stabilization Fund, and the adoption of tariff offsets to encourage
exports, particularly those from medium- and small-sized firms.



- 203 -
1.56     On public expenditures and investment, the program incorporated
the increase in the contribution of the public sector to domestic savings
from 4.8 to 5 percent of GDP, sought improved social returns to public
investment (limiting expenditures on oil refining, new highway construction
in the south, railroad subsidies and middle-class housing, while increasing
resources for health), and the maintenance of overall public sector per-
formance consistent with sustainable growth.
1.57     Jointly with the increase in public savings and investment, the
adjustment program sought to enhance private savings and investment.
Towards that goal, the program sought the creation of a stable macro-
economic environment, and a tax system with the least possible disincen-
tives. Furthermore, and for the same reason, the program sought to
strengthen the capital markets and ensure a sound, competitive banking
sector. Measures for that purpose included plans to create a private and
more flexible system of risk classification, the encouragement of firms to
develop into open corporations, the regulation of institutional investment,
the monitoring of banks' collection procedures of the portfolio sold to the
Central Bank, and the preparation of studies that sought means to strength-
en both sectors.
1.58     As far as the pension system was concerned. the adjustment program
sought to enhance the security and diversification of investment by private
pension funds, enhance their members' sense of idenitity with the institu-
tions, increase the long-term coverage of the system, reduce costs and
increase the competitiveness of pension funds.
1.59     Finally, on the health sector, the program sought to consolidate
the gains of past health investments in maternal and infant care and to
improve the cost-effective prevention strategy to early detection of
specific chronic and debilitating diseases. The program also sought to
improve efficiency in the use of human and financial resources allocated to
health services, of the private health care delivery system, and of health
investment by the public sector.
S. Implementation and Results
1.60     The Chilean Government complied satisfactorily with the content
and timing of the prescribed policy actions, which are described in detail
in Annex 4.
1.61     Regarding the balance of Da1ments, the Government maintaitned the
structure of export incentives, the value of the real effective exchange
rate, and a uniform tariff policy. In January 1988. the Government lowered
ad valorem tariffs from 20 percent to 15 percent. The real effective
exchange rate in September 1988 was unchanged from the rate prevailing in
November 1987, when SAL III negotiations took place. Thus, between
November 1987 and September 1988 the real effective exchange rate preserved
the 40 percent depreciation that materialized between 1980 and 1987.
1.62     During 1988, the above measures contributed to the increase in
export volume by 6.1 percent over 1987. Noncopper export volume increased
by 11.7 percent. Compounded by the strong improvement in the terms of
trade, the overall growth in exports contributed to the increase of the



- 204 -
merchandise trade surplus from US$1,229 million in 1987, to US$2,219
million in 1988, i.e., an increase of 80 percent. The strong trade per-
formance negated any need to alter the existing incentive framework for
exports, although a few distortions against indirect exporters were cor-
rected.
1.63     The strong trade performance contributed to the increase in gross
international reserves from US$1.9 billion in December 1987, to US$2.5
billion in December 1988. These reserves included those in the Copper
Stabilization Fund, which was maintained and strengthened during 1988,
reaching US$354 million. These efforts were complemented by the Govern-
ment's debt conversion program, which during 1988 reduced Chile's external
debt by US$2.9 billion, bringing the cumulative reduction in debt over a
four year period to US$6.2 billion.
1.64     The good trade performance in 1988 contributed to meeting Chile's
external financing requirements for that year. Likewise, available finan-
cing from vultilateral organizations and coomercial banks were assessed to
adequately cover Chile*s external financing requirements for 1989 and 1990.
1.65     The adjustment program was also successful in increasing savings
and investment. Nonfinancial public sector savings in 1988 reached
5.2 percent of GDP, despite the decrease in June of the value-added tax
from 20 percent to 16 percent, and in January of import tariffs from 20
percent to 15 percent. Furthermore, public savings do not reflect those
generated by the public enterprises that were privatized during the year,
nor the deposits in the Copper Stabilization Fund. The latter alone
accounted in 1988 for 2.5 percent of the GDP. Public sector savings,
jointly with the transfer of public sector savings to the private sector,
contributed significantly in the increase in national savings to 15.4 per-
cent of the GDP, from 12.6 percent in 1987. Fixed capital formation in
1988 increased by a real 10.7 percent, raising its share to GDP from 16
percent in 1987, to 16.3 percent in 1988.
1.66     The banking sector has undergone significant restructuring, fol-
lowing the financial crisis of 1982-83. In 1988, the acceleration of eco-
nomic growth facilitated the increase in savings, investment, and financial
intermediation. Thus, evidence points to the continuous recovery of the
banking sector. Banks' operational surplus continued to increase in 1988,
accelerating the repurchase of their bad loan portfolios sold to the
Central Bank. The improved operational performance took place despite
increased competition from financial companies and institutional investors
in a number of financial markets which banks have traditionally dominated.
At the same time, banks have been attempting to enter into new financial
markets. In spite of the improved situation of the banking sector. it will
take a number of years for various banks, including the largest two (Chile
and Santiago), to repurchase their bad loans from the Central Bank.
1.67     In contrast to the experience of the 1976-81 growth period, the
current economic recovery has been accompanied by a significant increase in
bonds and stock transactions. The development of the capital markets has
been facilitated by the reform of the social security system, which created
a significant body of institutional investors, the privatization of state
enterprises via the capital markets, and the supply of a large stock of
Central Bank and Treasury bonds, issued in 1983-86. The increasing import-



- 205 -
ance of pension funds and life insurance companies as institutional invest-
ors is the main characteristic of Chile's financial system. Further expan-
sion of the capital markets depends on the incentives for private companies
to issue new bonds and stocks.
1.68     The emphasis on social sectors was, once more, enhanced in the
conditionality of SAL III. Health, housing and social security were the
fundamental concerns in this stage. Reversing a declining trend since
1904, the proportion of active contributors over registered insured in the
pension system increased from 70.4 percent in 1986, to 72.4 percent in
1987.  This improvement was mostly the result of better overall economic
conditions and control measures. Likewise, the decline in open unemploy-
ment contributed to the increase in the percentage of the labor force
covered by the pension system in June 1988 to 79.2 percent. Another devel-
opment was the greater flexibility of pension firms to invest in financial
instruments issued by private nonfinancial corporations, resulting in the
decline of the share of pension funds invested in government paper from
44.9 percent in 1987 to 40.1 percent in June 1988. Furthermore, pension
funds conducted an educational campaign to increase the knowledge of the
insured about the system and its benefits, and statements to the insured
became more transparent: However, there were delays in simplifying and
unifying the collection of social security contributions. Nevertheless,
the Bank, upon evaluating progress made, appreciated the complexities
involved in this process and concluded that delays in the implementation of
this component were justified.
1.69     A review of activities during the past year in the area of chronic
diseases and adult health showed that efforts were made to standardize
norms for diagnosis and treatment, appoint program coordinators, organize
workshops for communication, training or study purposes, and develop pro-
grams that were intended to reduce the disease and death burden caused by
chronic and debilitating diseases. On maternal and child care, during
1988, 135 primary health care facilities were transferred to municipal-
ities. This municipalization program, which is now completed, is intended
to improve the quantity and quality of primary health care, both through
the availability of additional resources to such facilities and easier
access to them. The Government's actual investment expenditures during
1988 for repair and replacement of biomedical equipment and the rehabilita-
tion and replacement of deteriorating medical buildings was double the
amount budgeted. Emergency employment programs were essentially phased out
as the reviving economy generated adequate employment on its own.
1.70     At the time of supervision of the second tranche release, the
Government, despite the presentation of a draft law to the Legislature. was
unable to secure passage of a law taxing cigarette advertising. On
November 1988, the Government committed itself to reintroduce such a law,
which is expected to be presented as part of the omnibus Budget Law, in
October or November of 1989.
1.71     There were various studies commissioned under SAL III.  Three
studies on the financial sector system were completed satisfactorily, on
time.   The Government also completed a study relating to the social secur-
ity system. However, during supervision of the second tranche release, the
Government agreed to expand the existing study on the efficacy of including
the self-employed in the pension system, in order to incorporate the



- 206 -
results of a survey among the self-employed centering on the causes of
their lack of participation in the pension system. Completion of this
study was delayed, and the new completion date is for June 1989. The
Government also agreed to complete a study that will evaluate the effect of
voluntary supplementary savings accounts at the private pension fund com-
panies, on overall savings, the costs to these companies, and the enhance-
ment of their members' sense of indentity. This study was also delayed for
completion, and the new date is for June 1989. Another study that was
delayed, until June l9e9, was on the social costs of cigarette smoking.
VIZ. THE RELEVANCE OF TIM STRUCTURAL ADJUSIEUT LOANS FOR TE
CHILEA  SwCOOm
1.72     The three SALs supported a coherent and long-term adjustment pro-
gram that the Government initiated in early 1985. Although the program had
already been launched at the time of the first loan agreement, the SAL
conditionality helped strengthen policy measures in key areas. There was a
substantial coincidence of economic objectives between the World Bank and
the Chilean Government, and fundamental agreement in the three main objec-
tives of the program: the promotion of noncopper exports;'the enhancement
of domestic savings; and the strengthening of the corporate and financial
sectors. Beyond those objectives, SALs II and III influenced the Govern-
ment's focus on social programs, and the policies designed to mobilize the
private savings of the pension funds for productive investment.
1.73     Regarding the promotion of noncopper exports, the World Bank
influenced the implementation of the Copper Stabilization Fund. This is an
important tool with which to stabilize aggregate demand and the real
exchange rate.
1.74     The Bank also played an important role in the definition of the
public investment program. After a careful review of this program, several
projects were affected. In addition to indefinite postponement of the
expansion of Con-Con's oil refinery and scaling down of the southern high-
way and the northern railroads, the construction of the third line of
Santiago's metro and a port in Easter Island were eliminated in an earlier
period, and investments in the copper sector were increased. In general,
SALs encouraged a more careful evaluation of public investment programs.
1.75     While the Bank shared the goal of strengthening the corporate and
financial sectors, it also conveyed its concerns on the costs of various
support programs, and played an important role in phasing out the preferen-
tial exchange rate program, and in terminating generalized subsidies in
debt reprogrammings. The Bank also had an input in the strengthening of
the legal and regulatory framework of the financial sector and, in particu-
lar, the provision of a partial deposit insurance.
1.76     On the social sectors, the Bank influenced health policies,
including increased expenditures in this sector, contributed to the con-
tinued improvement in targeting of housing subsidies, and the recovery of
the Government's mortgage portfolio. Policy adjustments in the social
security system to promote efficiency, protect the security of the funds,
increase its coverage and improve its fairness also received an important
input from the Bank.



- 207 -
1.77     The above impact of SALs on various economic sectors was amplified
by their impact on the overall economy, either through direct financing or
by facilitating other forms of external financing. For an economy carrying
the heavy external debt burden that Chile does, financing 6 percent to
8 percent of the Country's annual import bill, as the SALs did in 1986 and
1987, had significant consequences for the well-being of the entire
economy. Additionally. the SALs and the IMF's Extended Fund Facility (EFF)
to Chile served as catalysts for other sources of financing.
1.78     Jointly, the World Bank and the IMF programs were decisive in
facilitating new loans from commercial bank creditors. The World Bank and
the IMF worked closely with the Chilean Government in the 1985 debt renego-
tiations with foreign banks, joining Government officials in major inter-
national financial centers to express support for Chile's financial
package. Chile restructured US$5.7 billion of principal payments to com-
mercial banks falling due during 1985-87, and obtained US$1.1 billion in
new money for 1985-86. In that package, the Bank partially guaranteed a
US$300 million B-loan to Chile. In July 1985, eight creditor governments
also agreed to reschedule US$145 million, representing 65 percent of prin-
cipal payments due during July 1. 1985-December 31, 1986 on Chilean public
or publicly-guaranteed debt with an original maturity of more than one
year.
1.79     In February 1987, the Government reached another agreement with
its commercial creditors. This agreement consolidated interest payments
into an annual payment, which deferred the July 1988 payment of US$450
million to January 1989. In addition, maturities in 1988-91 were re-
scheduled, and spreads on past new money facilities were .reduced to 1-118
percent over LIBOR, while those on restructured maturities were reduced to
1 percent. This agreement, which aimed at closing Chile's 1987-88
financing gap, was facilitated by the Bank's agreement to reduce the
guarantee fee for the US$300 B-Loan of which the Bank guaranteed
US$150,000.
1.80     In April 1988, Chile and its Commercial Bank Advisory Committee
reached an agreement further to reduce the spread of their outstanding
loans. The banks agreed to accept a price of LIBOR plus 13/16ths for old
debt and 7/8ths for the new money facilities. They also agreed that up to
US$2 billion in debt of Chilean entities may be exchanged for debt held by
any creditor, and that the Government may use the lesser of US$500 million
or the reserves accumulated in the Copper Stabilization Fund as collateral
for the above exchanges or debt buybacks, that Chile can grant liens on its
assets of uip to US$500 million as collateral for future new borrowings, and
that Chile can pledge up to US$150 million as collateral security for hedg-
ing transactions. Again, to facilitate this agreement, the Bank accepted
the use of the Copper Stabilization Fund deposits for the purposes
described above, and reduced once more its guarantee fee for its B-Loan to
Chile.
1.81     The release of the second tranche for SAL III also paved the way
for a US$200 million equivalent cofinancing loan from the Export-Import
Bank of Japan (EXIM Bank). The cofinancing loan was signed on December 14,
1988 and was disbursed jointly with the second tranche of SAL III. The
Bank played a crucial role in this cofinancing operation having twice in



- 208 -
1988 accompanied EXIM Bank missions to Chile to assist them in their
appraisal of economic conditions in the country. Furthermore, the EXIM
Bank relied on the Bank's assessment of conditionality for disbursing its
loan to Chile. It was made clear that the Chilean Government would not
have accepted additional conditionality from another government and, con-
sequently, the role played by the Bank in facilitating this loan was essen-
tial in obtaining the Japanese cofinancing.
1.82     The above contributions of the SAL program helped create an
environment of macroeconomic stability which was crucial to the recovery of
private investment from the lows reached in the 1982-83 debacle. Without
the SALs and the IMF's EFF program, Chile would have been faced with a
tough choice. The absence of such programs would not only have deprived
Chile of a very significant source of financing, but would have made
unlikely any additional money and concessions originating from comiercial
banks. Under such circumstances, Chile might have been forced to suspend
foreign interest payments andlor reduce its economic growth. In either
case, already depressed living conditions would have suffered further.
Instead, the Bank's program contributed to a successful case of macro-
economic adjustment in Latin America, where a heavily indebted nation has
been able not only to service its external debt, but resume a satisfactory
pace of economic expansion.
VIII. THE ROLS OF TUB IF
1.83     In January 1983, in the midst of Chile's economic crisis, the IMP
entered into a two year Stand-by arrangement with Chile, for SDR 500 mil-
lion. That program basically focused on the reduction of public sector
expenditures, in the absence of any structural changes in the economy.
Subsequently, in August 15, 1985, the IMF provided support to the Govern-
ment's structural adjustment program with a three year (1985-87) Extended
Fund Facility (EFF) providing SDR 750 million. The principal objective of
the EFF was to reduce by half, and in the period of three-years, the 1984
current account deficit, within the context of moderate economic growth and
decreasing inflation. Towards that goal, the EFF established quantitative
limits on the expansion of net domestic assets of the Central Bank, inter-
national reserves and the fiscal deficit as a share to GDP.
1.84     At the beginning of 1986, after Chile had met all EFF targets
established in 1983, the IMF decided to continue its support of the Govern-
ment's adjustment program. The principal objective at this stage was to
reduce subsidies to the banking sector and the consolidated deficit of the
public sector. This was in'addition to ongoing reduction of the current
account deficit and the pursuit. of low inflation and moderate economic
growth. To that effect, the EFF established new quantitative targets for
1986.
1.85     A similar process was developed in 1987, following Chile's compli-
ance with all EFF targets for 1986. The last disbursement from the three-
year EFF took place in August 1988. At mid-year 1988, the Chilean Govern-
ment requested continued Fund support for their economic program through a
one-year lengthening of the current EFF arrangement. The IM assessed that
the macroeconomic and structural policies followed by the Government would
contribute to the attainment of the objectives of their program and decided



- 209 -
to support the Government's request. The fourth year extension of the EFF
(to August 1989), provided an additional SDR75 million to Chile. New per-
formance criteria were set, along the lines of previous conditions.
1.86     The Bank and the IMF established an excellent working relationship
in the SALIEFF-supported adjustment program, with frequent consultation
between institutions in each critical phase of these respective operations.
U.  PROCIRD3?T AND DISBURSEKHIT
1.87     The proceeds of each of the three loans were disbursed in two
tranchess US$125 million upon effectiveness (US$150 million for SAL II)
and the remaining US$125 million (US$100 million for SAL II) after satis-
factory reviews of progress on the overall program of structural adjustment
and the criteria for second tranche disbursement were met. Full disburse-
nent of SAL I and SAL II took place in a period of less than nine months
after approval; the second tranche release for SAL III was completed within
a year of approval.
1.88     Disbursements for these operations were authorizes on the basis of
import documentation presented through statement of expenditures (SOgs).
The imports permitted were governed by a negative list cited in Schedule 1
of the loan agreements. The Central Bank was responsible for preparation
of the withdrawal applications and for collection and retention of evidence
in support of these applications. The applications, which were based on
import documentation developed by commercial banks, were prepared in a
skilled and orderly fashion. The only problems, related to misunderstand-
ings about dates of imports that could qualify for second tranche with-
drawals, were minor and adequately clarified. Supervision missions by
regional and loan department staff verified that the required documentation
was retained and in easily accessible and good order.
1.89     Procurement for all three operations proceeded well.  Interna-
tional competitive bidding (ICB) was not used because all purchases were
for goods costing less than $5 million each, or for petroleum products,
which, as specified in the Loan Agreements, do not require ICB. There is
no information on the breakdown of public and private procurement. How-
ever, the Central Bank has indicated that, in its view, the majority of
contracts were awarded through the private sector, utilizing commercial
practices. Although documentation is not available, given the open market
policy pursued in Chile, it is assumed that most of these importers used
the normal commercial practice of obtaining competitive quotations.
1.90    The lank reviewed the documentation provided by the Central Bank
supporting the SOEs as part of supervision. These field reviews, performed
in relation to tranche releases for SAL I and SAL 1I, confirmed that proper
procurement procedures were adhered to and that imports were not from the
negative list. Based on the performance of SAL I and SAL II, the Bank
relied on the audit reports to monitor procurement performance for SAL III.
(As detailed in the following paragraph, these audit reports concluded that
the goods imported under SAL III complied with the restrictions provided in
the loan agreement.) In addition, a sample of SOS documentation used for
tranche release for SAL III will be reviewed ex post by a Bank mission
scheduled for late June 1989.



- 210 -
1.91     The audit reports were prepared in a timely fashion by a reputable
private firm (Langton Clarke) and were considered satisfactory when
reviewed by LAC audit specialists. (In fact, in the opinion of one LAC
audit specialist, the reports provide a good reference for future audits of
structural adjustment operations in other countries.) The reports, which
detail all import documentation reviewed on site at commercial banks in
Chile, with specific information on suppliers, commodity and amount of
contract, concluded that:
(a) goods imported comply with the restrictions of t4e loan agree-
ments:
(b) SOEs prepared by the Central Bank are in compliance and fairly
present the import disbursements for goods provided in the
loan agreement; and
(c) Central Bank documentation showed no difference with that
reported to the Bank.

(Translation from Spanish)
CENTRAL BANK OF CHILE
Santiaao                                           SAL I
FIRST STRUCTURAL ADJUSTMENT LOAN: BACKGROUND TO IMPLEMENTATION OF THE PROGRAM
I.        Period Preceding the Program
1.        The sudden change in the external economic climate at the beginning
of the 1980s gave rise to a crisis that dealt a severe blow to the economies
in this region. For the Chilean economy in particular, it brought to an
abrupt although temporary halt the structural adjustment and growth process
initiated in the mid-70s to increase the efficiency of the economy through
greater integration into the international trading system, the development or
private initiative, a subsidiary role for the state in the economy, and
preferential use of the market for the allocation of resources. Despite
efforts to foster exports, reduce external indebtedness in the public sector
and put public finances on a sounder footing, the heavy debt commitments of
the private sector, coupled with a drastic deterioration of the terms if trade
and a sharp rise in international interest rates in 1981, caused cons;_erable
macroeconomic imbalances. which began to worsen in 1982 as a result of the
dramatic drop in private financing flows to the region.
2.        In response to these pressures, an energetic program was instituted
to adjust domestic expenditure and bring it into line with the real
possibilities of financing then available. This made an increase in the real
exchange rate inevitable, which, since it affected loan recoverability
seriously, led to government intervention in the management of a sizeable
group of financial institutions. As a consequence of the program, there was a
sharp drop in aggregate expenditure, reflected in a smaller current account
deficit, which fell from US$4.733 billion in 1981 to US$1.117 billion in
1983. In combination with the weakened capital position of the banking
system, however, this caused a drastic reduction in levels of activity,
employment and real wages.
3.        Throughout 1983 and 1984, Chile pursued its macroeconomic policy
against the background of a stand-by arrangement with the IWF designed
basically to promote the required adjustment in expenditure in the absence of
a structural change. Concurrently, after the Government had agreed to
guarantee the private financial sector's external credits, the outstanding
debt was renegotiated and a commitment obtained from the tnbernational banking
system to provide additional resources so that the costs of the adjustment
process could be spread over time. The goals embodied in this program were
accomplished in full, despite the subsequent unexpected deterioration in
external conditions. Its results were positive, much of the improvement
observed in the country's external accounts being explained by a successful
combination of exchange, monetary and fiscal policy.



ANNEX 5
Page 2 of 36
- 221 -
4.        On the domestic front, the major concerns in 1983 and 1984 were to
reduce the high and Prowing unemployment rates observed from mid-1982 onward
and to strengthen the financial and nonfinancial segments of the private
sector, which.Xere confronted with a grave liquidity erisis. The
authorities therefore allocated a considerable volume of resources at this
stage to funding special temporary employment programs and substantially
increased government spending on housing and infrastructure. In addition,
in the process of returning the financial system to a healthier footing,
debtors with foreign-currency commitments were allowed a preferential
exchange rate and in both 1983 and 1984 the obligations of viable debtors in
this group were rescheduled so as to cushion the impact of the crisis on
Chile's industries and financial institutions. Furthermore, in order to
solve the financial problem created by the nonrecoverability of a proportion
of the loans made by those institutions, a mechanism was set up in 1984
through which their outstanding portfolios were purchased, with arrangements
for repurchase subsequently.
5.        In mid-1984, on the basis of assumptions regarding future external
conditions that proved ultimately to have been optimistic, projections were
made of the external savings requirements needed to finance a program of
sustained growth over the three years from 1984 to 1986. Following the
realization that they would be considerable, a conviction arose that
far-reaching adjustment of the structural type was becoming unavoidable. An
additional and unexpected deterioration in the terms of trade and an
increase in international interest rates - reversals that at least
partially explained the substantial current account deficit in 1984 -- made
the urgency of the need for such a change all the more apparent.
Accordingly, in February 1985, with World Bank assistance, an adjustment
program for the three-year period 1985-87 was drawn up in an attempt to
reinforce the basic structural transformation strategy initiated in the
mid-70s.
II.       Scope of the Proaram
Government obiectives
6.        The structural adjustment program set up in early 1985 was
designed essentially to reconcile a recovery in activity and employment
levels with a gradual reduction in the external debt burden. A realistic
exchange rate policy, consistent with what the Chilean economy could afford
to expend over the medium term, was adopted as a means of stimulating
increased growth in nontraditional exports while simultaneously fostering
the development of those secte's engaged in efficient import substitution.
In the absence of margins that lould make higher domestic savings possible
by reducing consumption, the program aimed not only at paving the way for
increased savings in the public sector through a policy of austerity in
current expenditure, but also at stimulating private savings through
progressive normalization and revitalization of the capital market against a
background of positive real interest rates. Tn brief, then, the central
objective of this adjustment program was export-based growth, coupled with a
more intensive domestic savings effort that would reduce the dependence on
external financing.



ANNEX 5
Page 3 of 36
- 222 -
Priorities
?.        In the initial stages, given the overall objective just described,
the adjustment-program was used as the vehicle for implementing a package of
measures intended to solve three priority structural problems confronting the
economy: insufficient growth and excessive concentration in exports, an
imbalance between savings and investment, and the precarious situation of the
financial system. There was also equal concern to increase the capacity of
the economy to generate productive employment, although the authorities
recognized that substantial improvements in this area would occur only
gradually.
8.        In particular, the adjustment program attempted to expand
nontraditional exports, mainly by: setting a timetable for uniform tariff
reductions, so as to bring down the cost of imported inputs used in the
manufacture of export goods; instituting a realistic and stable exchange
policy, so as to increase the competitiveness of Chilean exports; creating a
mechanism to neutralize fluctuations in copper prices (the Copper
Stabilization Fund), so as to eliminate one cause of exchange instability;
making reimbursement of the value-added tax payable by exporters on their
imported inputs quicker and more flexible; creating a simplified system for
the refund of indirect taxes on minor exports; conducting preliminary studies
for and drawing up a forestry program, so as to facilitate adequate planning
of the public and private investment requirements that would guarantee rapid
growth in this sector; and, finally, restructuring PROCHILE to provide more
direct support for small and medium exporters of nontraditional goods - for
example, by organizing new markets and by identifying potential export
products.
9.        Since the financial and nonfinancial segments of the private sector
were facing serious liquidity problems, the major savings effort, at least at
this initial stage of the program, needed to come from the public sector.
Accordingly, the adjustment program attempted to raise the public-sector
savings rate not only by following a policy of accountability and austerity on
currer- expenditure, under which the creation of more job opportunities would
take precedence over wage increases that were not sustainable over the
medium-term, but also by introducing a policy of fixing rate scales for public
services to reflect changes in their opportunity costs and at the same time
help at least partially to finance the related necessary capital spending.
10.       The adjustment program also aimed at raising the returns on
government capital spending by: executing projects in the copper and energy
sectors, to promote exports directly; developing infrastructure, to eliminate
the bottlenecks affecting the growth of private investment in the
agricultural, forestry and industrial export sectors; and, finally,
re-examining projects already approved and pending, with a view to
reallocating the resources earmarked for those not in line with development
strategy priorities.
11.       Finally, considering the substantial transfers of public funds
needed to finance previous - and future - recovery operations, and given the
importance of achieving less dependence on external financing, the adjustment
program, at least during this first stage, attempted to help solve the
liquidity crisis by: instituting a monetary policy that would preclude



ANNEX 5
Page 4 of 36
- 223 -
additional subsidies for the banking system and phase out existing subsidies
as far as possible; putting a stop to further general debt reschedulings;
strengthening financial and corporate legislation governing the concentration
and quality of-assets; and, finally, enacting legislation that would enable
the state to assist in recapitalizing the banking institutions in whose
management it had intervened.
Role of the IMF. the World Bank and other sources of financing
12.       The IMF supported Chile's structural adjustment program with an
Extended Fund Facility (EFF) of SDR 750 million for the period 1985-87. Its
key objective was to bring down the current account deficit recorded in 1984
by half over the three-year period, in a climate of moderate economic growth
and declining inflation. To that end, the EFF program began with the
introduction of specific quantitative limits for 1985 on growth in the net
domestic assets of the Central Bank, international reserves and the fiscal
deficit as a proportion of GDP.
13.       Paralleling this, the World Bank in October 1985 officially approved
a US$250 million structural adjustment loan (SAL I) for 1985-86. Intended to
support the measures to be implemented in 1985 and the first half of 1986, it
was disbursed in two equal tranches, the first being payable on signature of
the loan itself (December 1985) and the second subject to compliance with
certain pre-established conditions (June 1986).
14.       According to official projections, after allowing for IMF credits of
US$508 million, additional external financing requirements to cover the
1985-86 balance of payments deficit would amount to US$1.995 billion. The
support Chile received from the World Bank and the IMF in its 1985-86 external
debt negotiations with the international banking community was crucial not
only in obtaining that financing but also in rescheduling the commitments that
matured during the 1985-86 period. These funds came principally from the
World Sank, in the form of disbursements amounting to US$400 million under SAL
programs, and from the international financial community, in the form of fresh
funds amounting to US$1.085 billion (a figure that included US$300 million in
cofinancing arrangements between the World Bank and the international banking
system).
III.      Program Achievements
Policy implementation
15.       During the first half of 1985, as proof of their determination to
promote the required structural adjustment from the outset, the Chilean
authorities instituted the key measures designed to provide the necessary
incentives for development of the export sector and efficient import
substitution. In addition, an early start was also made with important
actions aimed at bringing about both an increase in public savings rates and
recapitalization and normalization of the fjnancial system.
16.       In view of the restriction on financing confronting the Chilean
economy, and since the achievement of significant and lasting results in this
area meant that the country must persevere with these policies through the
medium term, evidence of fulfillment of the requirements of the initial



ANNEX S
Page 5 of 36
- 224 -
program agreed on with the World Bank was of vital importance. This was the
case because integral implementation of the specific measures agreed to as
conditions for disbursement of the second tranche of SAL I - as detailed in
the accompanying Table I - would alone give access to the additional similar
financial support arrangements needed to fund continuation of the 1985-87
adjustment program.
Results achieved
17.       In 1985, as a result of implementation of the policies described,
the key objectives of the adjustment program were satisfactorily
accomplished. In addition to a drastic cutback in the level if domestic
expenditure, there was moderate but sustained growth in export-based activity,
coupled with a greater domestic savings effort. As proof of the substantial
contraction in aggregate expenditure over the period, the current account
deficit fell by 351 with respect to the 1984 figure, a result ascribable to
fewer imports and - although to a lesser degree - increased exports (refer
to Table 2). With regard to the latter, it should be noted that without the
significant decline (13.9S) in the prices of noncopper exports, the surprising
increase (14.62) in the volume of those exports would have meant a drop of
over 401 in the current account deficit (refer to Table 3).
18.       In addition, the extent of Chile's dependence on external financing
was also reduced significantly in 1985: with virtually identical investment
rates, gross national savings rose from 32 of GDP in 1984 to 5.42 of GDP in
1985. As demonstrated by the change in the public-sector savings rate over
the same period, namely an increase from 0.6% of GDP to 3.812of GDP, the major
effort in this arena - at least in the initial stage of the program - took
place in the government sector. A decisive element here was the real
improvement achieved in the deficit of the nonfinancial public sector, which
was brought down from 4.41 of GDP in 1984 to 2.61 of GDP in 1985 (refer to
Table 4).
19.       There was also substantial improvement in the equity situation of
banks and debtors in 1985, a basic prerequisite if private-sector savings
levels were to increase over the medium term. The introduction in mid-1985 of
novel mechanisms of external debt conversion undoubtedly favored this
improvement, since they changed the liability structure and capitalization of
banks and corporations. Some of these mechanisms also helped raise levels of
investment (funded with external resources) and employment from late 1985
onward.  Consolidation of the advances achieved in connection with both
external and domestic accounts began in 1986.
Projected and actual performance
20.       In 1985, macroeconomic indicators behaved largely in accordance with
the official projections made at mid-year. 1/ The balance of payments current
account deficit, for instance, was slightly below the projected level, the
result of an overestimate of payments for financial and nonfinancial services
which more than offset an overestimate of the trade account surplus (refer to
Table 2). As far as the elements which go to make up the latter are
1/ These projections, contained in the June 1985 document "Republic of Chile
Financial Package," were more optimistic in general than those put out by
the World Bank in October 1985 in its Report No. P-4131-CE.



ANNEX S
- 225 -                         Page 6 of 36
concerned, there was an overestimate of noncopper exports, but examination of
the key assumptions set out in Table 3 indicates the explanation, namely a
sharp and unexpected fall in the prices of the major nontraditional exports,
itself at least partially neutralized by a doubling of the projected rate of
growth in this class of export merchandise. The accuracy of the projections
of copper price and volume of copper exports were similarly affected by the
same kind of trend, except that here the overestimate of price was amply
offset by the underestimate of volume increase. The end result was that
copper exports were slightly higher than projected.
21.       Although level of activity increased significantly, it was
approximately one-third less than projected, mainly as a consequence of the
unforeseen real decline in aggregate consumption over the period (refer to
Table 4). As far as 1986 is concerned, although the level of investment grew
at slightly less than the projected rate, the behavior of aggregate
consumption led to higher than expected growth in activity (5S, according to
Table 4). Despite fully offsetting overestimates of noncopper exports and
imports in the same period, the balance of payments current account deficit
was clearly more positive than predicted (refer to Table 2). This was
fundamentally a result of the substantial and unexpected decline in
international interest rates that began in late 1985 (and totaled between 2
and 3 percentage points by the end of 1986), an improvement that would have
meant a lower current account deficit had the price of copper during the
period not been so significantly overestimated (by approximately 10%,
according to Table 3).
